Wright\nLangley\nResearch\nCenter\nHampton,\nVirginia\n\nProceedings\nof a conference\nsponsored\nby\nNASA Langley\nResearch\nCenter,\nHampton,\nVirginia,\nand Air Force\nWright\nAeronautical\nLaboratories,\nWright-Patterson\nAFB, Ohio,\nand held in Norfolk,\nVirginia\nNovember\n\n18-21,\n\n1986\n\nN/_A\nNational Aeronautics\nand Space Administration\nScientific and Technical\nInformation Branch\n1986\n\nPREFACE\nThe National\nAeronautics\nand Space Administration\n(NASA) and the\nDepartment\nof Defense\n(DOD) are actively\ninvolved\nin the development\nof a validated\ntechnology\ndata base in the areas of control/structures\ninteraction,\ndeployment\ndynamics\nand system performance\nfor\nlarge\nflexible\nspacecraft.\nThe generation\nof these\ntechnologies\nis essential\nto the efficient\nand confident\ndevelopment\nof this\nnew class\nof\nspacecraft\nto meet stringent\ngoals\nin safety,\nperformance\nand cost.\nAs a major element\nof this\ntechnology\neffort,\nthe NASA Office\nof\nAeronautics\nand Space Technology\n(OAST) has initiated\nthe Control\nof\nFlexible\nStructures\n(COFS) Program which provides\na major\nfocus\nfor\nthe Research\nand Technology\nbase activities\nin structural\ndynamics\nand\ncontrols\nand complements\nlong range development\nprograms\nat the Air\nForce Wright\nAeronautical\nLaboratories\n(AFWAL).\nThese activities\nprovide\na systematic\napproach\nto address\ntechnology\nvoids\nthrough\ndevelopment\nand validation\nof analytical\ntools,\nextensive\nground\ntesting\nof representative\nstructures,\nand in-space\nexperiments\nfor\nverification\nof analysis\nand ground\ntest methods.\nIn order to promote timely dissemination of technical information\nacquired in these programs, the NASA Langley Research Center and the\nAFWAL will alternately sponsor an annual conference to report to\nindustry, academia and government agencies on the current status of\ncontrol/structures\ninteraction technology.\nThe First NASA/DOD CSI\nTechnology Conference is the beginning of this series.\nThis publication\nis a compilation of the papers presented at the\nconference and is divided into two parts.\nPart I is to be distributed\nat the conference and Part II will be distributed after the\nconference.\nThis publication was expedited and enhanced through the efforts of the\nResearch Information and Applications Division, Langley Research\nCenter.\nThe use of trade\nnames or names of manufacturers\nin this\npublication\ndoes not constitute\nan official\nendorsement\nof such products\nor\nmanufacturers,\neither\nexpressed\nor implied,\nby the National\nAeronautics\nand Space Administration.\nH. Armstrong\n\n547\n\nvii\n\nN87-\n\nADVANCED\nLARGE\n\nCOMPOSITES\nFOR\nNAVY\nSPACECRAFT\n\nWilliam\nComposite\n\nFirst\n\nE.\n\nDavis\n\nAmetek,\nInc.\nMaterials\nand\nApplications\nAnaheim,\nCalifornia\n\nNASA/DOD\n\nCSI\nNorfolk,\nNovember\n\nTechnology\nVirginia\n18-21,\n1986\n\nDivision\n\nConference\n\n16015\n\nCONTRACTSUMMARY\nFuture spacecraft\nwill\nbe an essential\npart of the US defense\nsystem and ensuring\nsurvivability\nis critical\nto the spacecraft\ndesign.\nPotential\nthreats\nto the spacecraft\ninclude\nspace-based\nlaser,\nground-based laser,\nand direct\nascent nuclear\nand KEW\n(pellet)\nweapon systems.\nMany of these systems will\nbe large\nin comparison to conventional\nspacecraft\nand the requirements\nfor\nprecision\nstatic\nand dynamic dimensional\ncontrol\nare very severe.\nPayloads such as IR sensors,\nRF antennas,\netc. must be pointed\nvery accurately,\nwhich requires\ndimensionally\nstable\nsupport\nstructures\nthat also have inherent\npassive damping thus resulting\nin precise alignment\nunder static,\ndynamic and thermal disturbances.\nThis paper is an overview of work conducted on contract\nfor\nthe Naval Sea Systems Command. The objective\nof this contract\nwas to provide direction\nfor the development of high modulus\ngraphite\nreinforced\nmetal matrix\ncomposites.\nThese advanced\nmaterials\ncan have a significant\neffect\non the performance\nof a\nspacecraft\nbefore,\nduring and after\nan evasive maneuver.\nThe information\ncontained\nin this paper is based on a paper\nentitled\n"Spacecraft\nSurvivability\nby Maneuvering-KEW Environment"\n(Reference i) and the contract\nfinal\nreport\n"Effects\nof Materials\nand Structures\non Spacecraft\nControls",\nN00024-83-C-5353\n(Reference 2).\n\nTITLE:\n\nEFFECTS OF MATERIALS ON SPACECRAFT\nCONTROL AND MANEUVERABILITY\n\nOBJECTIVE:\n\nASSESS THE IMPACT OF MMC ON SPACECRAFT\nMANEUVERING\n\nCONTRACT\nPERIOD\n\nMONITOR:\nOF CONTRACT:.\n\nMARLIN\n\nKINNA\n\nSEPTEMBER\n\nFigure\n\n1\n\n1983-MARCH\n\n1985\n\nSUMMARY PROGRAM\nOF\nTASKS\nThe work conducted\ntechnical\ntasks;\nFigure\nTask\nFinite\n\n1\n\nwas\ndevelopment\nelement\nmodels\n\non this program was organized\n2 provides\nan overview\nof\nthe\nof\na generic\nof\ncandidate\n\nNavy\nspacecraft\nstructural\ndesigns\n\ninto\n\nseven\n\nprogram.\nmodel.\nwere\ndevelop-\n\ned.\nIn\nTask\n2, the\nfinite-element\nmodel(s)\nof\nthe\nstructure\nwere\nused\nto\nconduct\nanalytical\nassessments\ninvolving\nconventional\nmaterials,\nresin\nmatrix\ncomposites\nand metal\nmatrix\ncomposites.\nIn Task\n3 and\n4, MMC\nmaterial\ndesign,\nfabrication\nand\nevaluation\nwas\nconducted.\nThis\nconsisted\nof\ngenerating\nmaterial\ndesigns\nand\ndeveloping\na data\nbase\nfor\na broad\nrange\nof\ngraphite\nreinforced\nMMC\nmaterials.\nAll\nmaterial\nwas\nprocured\naccording\nto\nspecifications\nwhich\nset\nmaterial\nquality\nand\nmaterial\nproperty\nstandards.\nIn\nTask\n5, a set\nof evasive\nmaneuvering\nrequirements\nwere\nderived\nand\nused\nin Task\n6 to\nconduct\nanalytical\nsimulations.\nThese\nanalytical\nsimulations\nused\ncurrent\nSOA\nmaterial\nproperties\nand\nprojected\nmaterial\nproperties\nto\nprovide\nan\nindication\nof\nkey\npayoffs\nfor\nmaterial\ndevelopment.\nIn Task\n7,\na set\nof material\ndevelopment\nrecommendations\nwas\ngenerated.\n\nCONTRACT\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDEFINE\n\nGENERIC\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ANALYTICAL\nAND INITIAL\n\nOVERVIEW\n\nITSS finite-element\nmodel\nof\na\ngeneric\nspacecraft\nand\nperformance\nrequirements\nthat\nincorporate\ncharacteristics\nof\nthe\nITSS\nspacecraft\nconcepts\nwere\ndefined.\nThe\nITSS\nwill\nbe\na combination\nof\nIR and\nRF\npayloads\nassembled\non one\nspacecraft.\nThe\nRF\nsystem\nwill\nbe\nrelatively\nlarge\nand\nflexible\nand\nthe\nIR\nsystem\nwill\nbe comparatively\nrigid.\nThe\ncombined\nspacecraft\nwas\nmodeled\nas\nan RF\nantenna\nwith\nslewing,\npointing,\nand\nmaneuvering\nrequirements\nand\nthe\nIR\npayload\nwas\nassumed\nto be\na rigid\nmass\nlumped\nat the\nspacecraft\nbus.\nSpecific\nnumerical\nperformance\nrequirements\nand\ndistrubances\nfor\nthe\nspacecraft\nwere\ndefined\nin this\ntask.\n\nprimary\nantenna\nbus.\nments\nmodel\ni.e.,\nbased\n\nFigure\n3 illustrates\nthe\ngeneric\nspacecraft\nmodel.\nThe\ncomponents\nwhich\nimpact\nits\ndynamic\nbehavior\nare\nthe\ndish,\nthe\nantenna\nfeed,\nthe\nfeed\nboom,\nand\nthe\nspacecraft\nThe\nrigid\nIR payload\nmust\nmeet\nstringent\npointing\nrequirewhile\nmounted\nto\nthese\nflexible\ncomponents.\nThe\ngeneric\nwas\ndefined\naround\nthe\nflexible\npart\nof\nthe\nspacecraft,\nthe\nRF\nantenna.\nBased\non\nthe\nresults\nof previous\nspacesurveillance\nstudies,\nan\nRF dish\ndiameter\n(antenna\n\naperture)\nof\nchosen.\nThe\nthe\nspacecraft\nnautical\nweighs\n\n30\nIR\n\nmeters\npayload\nbus.\nA\n\nmiles\nwas\napproximately\n\nand\nan operational\nis cm\nwere\nand\npart\nof\nof\n5600\n\nMANEUVER\n\nspacecraft\n\nOPERATIONS\n-- 150 NMI,\n25 MINUTES\n-- 22 \xc2\xb0, 33 SEC\n-- 45 \xc2\xb0, 15 SEC\n\nMATERIAL EVALUATION\nMaterial\ndesign and fabrication\ndevelopment tasks were\nconducted wherein a broad range of MMC\nmaterial\nvariables\nwere\nused to develop specimen designs using graphite\nfibers\nin\naluminum and magnesium matrices.\nThe materials\nevaluated\nin this\nprogram are shown in Figure 4. Two sets of material\ndesigns were\nevaluated\nin this program.\nThe initial\nset of materials\nwere\ndesigned based on data developed in previous\nwork. Material\ntest data developed in Task 4, was used to design the second set\nof materials.\nMaterials\nwere procured in flat\nplate and tube\nform and specifications\nwere imposed for all of the fabrication\nactivities.\nBatch acceptance tests were performed on the precursor wire material\nprior\nto proceeding with fabrication\nprocesses.\nDiffusion\nbonding,\npultrusion\nand Rapipress processes\nwere used to fabricate\nthe material\ndesigns evaluated\nin this\nprogram.\nA comprehensive\nfabrication\nquality\nassessment task\nwas conducted where all parts were tested by ultrasonic\ninspection methods (C-scan) and photomicrographs\nwere taken.\nSUMMARY\n\nMaterial\n\nOF\n\nRound\n\nFoil\n\nFABRICATED\n\nPANELS\n\nLayup\n\nSize\n\nManufacturer\n\n!\n\nP100/6061\n\nA1\n\nP55/6061\n\nA1\n\nP55/6061\n\nA1\n\nP55/6061\n\nA1\n\nPI00/AZglC\n\nTi\n\nPI00/AZglC\n\nMg\n\nP55/AZ91C\n\nMg\n\n(0) matrix\ncomposites\nare\nideal for application\nto large truss structures\nin space.\nThey\nare attractive\nfor use when thermal distortions\nare critical,\nand\nin addition,\ntheir\nextremely\nhigh stiffness\nto weight ratio\nsuits\nthem for use in dynamic environments.\nStructural\ndesigns based\non the use of very high modulus MMCwill\nbe lightweight\nand may\nenable operation\nduring an evasive maneuver without\nthe need for\nstructural\ncontrol\nsystems.\nThe use of metal\nmatrix\ncomposites\ncan\n\nreduce\n\nstructural\n\nmaneuvering\nadditional\n\nmass\n\nthrusts\nfuel\nand\n\nand\n\ninertia,\n\nrequiring\n\nand\nscanning\ntorques,\nand\nactuator\nweight\nsavings.\n\nsmaller\n\nresulting\n\nin\n\nREFERENCES\ni.\n\nDavis,\n\nWilliam\n\nE.;\n\nLevin,\n\nRichard\n\nN.;\n\nGeorge\nA. Maneuvering\nComposites\nTechnology\n\n1985\n\nStructures\non\n(NSSC\nContract\n\nSpacecraft\nControls.\nNo.\nN00024-83-C-5353),\n\nN87-\n\nN-ROSS:\n\nTHE\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nRobert\nNaval\n\nFirst\n\nAND\n\nCONTROL\n\n16016\n\nISSUES\n\nE. Lindberg\n\nResearch\nLaboratory\nWashington,\nDC\n\nNASA/DOD\nCSI\nNorfolk,\nNovember\n\nTechnology\nConference\nVirginia\n18-21,\n1986\n\n11\n\nMISSIONANDAPPROACH\nThe NavyRemoteOcean Sensing System (N-ROSS)satellite\nwill be\nlaunched in 1990 to provide the Navy with the operational capability to\nmeasure sea surface parameters on a worldwide year-round basis in all weather\nconditions.\nThe satellite\nwill carry four primary instruments, two active and\ntwo passive, in a low-earth sun-synchronous orbit.\nThe radar altimeter,\nsimilar to the instrument currently flying on GEOSAT,\nwill measure absolute\naltitude above the geoid and will contribute to the determination of wave\nheight. The scatterometer, an evolutionary design derivative of the SEASAT\ninstrument, will be capable of both wind speed and wind direction measurement.\nThe microwave imager (or SSM/I) and the Low Frequency Microwave Radiometer are\npassive scanning instruments, the first operating at 19.3, 22.2, 37.0 and 85.5\nGHz, and the second at 5.2 and 10.4 GHz. The SSM/I, currently under\ndevelopment for the DMSP\nprogram, will measure water vapor and map sea ice\nedges. The LFMR a new instrument design that will measure sea surface\nis\ntemperature to better than l\xc2\xb0C, to contribute to the mapping of currents,\nfronts and eddies in the ocean surface structure.\n\nTHE\n\nN-ROSS\n\nSATELLITE\n\nMISSION:\n\nMEASURE SEA SURFACE PARAMETERS OVER 95% OR MORE OF\nTHE WORLD\'S OCEANS UNDER ALL WEATHER CONDITIONS\n\nTHE\n\nAPPROACH:\n\nLOW ELECTRONICS;\nBOX\n\nSPACEI\nBOOM\n\nFigure\n\n2\n\n13\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nAND\n\nCONTROL\n\nCONCERNS\n\nThe flexibility\nof the LFMR and the other appendage\nstructures,\ntogether\nwith the active\nspin drive system\nand the 0.05 deg pointing\nknowledge\nrequirement\nfor the LFMR sensor\nboresight,\ncombine\nto immediately\nidentify\ncontrol-structure\ninteraction\nas a technology\nissue\nin the N-ROSS\nbaseline\ndesign.\nFigure\n3 highlights\nsome fundamental\nconcerns\nand control\nperformance\nof flexible\nsatellites.\nThese\nmost satellite\nconcepts\nincorporating\nlarge lightweight\neven those which\ndo not spin, and they were considered\nbaseline\n\nN-ROSS\n\nTHE\n\ndesign.\n\nFUNDAMENTAL\nCONCERNS\nWITH\nDYNAMICS\nAND CONTROL\nOF FLEXIBLE\nSPACECRAFT\n\nMOTION\nOF THE FLEXIBLE\nSTRUCTURES\nATTITUDE\nCONTROL\nSYSTEM\n\nATTITUDE\nCONTROL\nRESONANCES\n\nSYSTEM\n\nEXTERNAL\nDISTURBANCES\nRESONANCES\n\nSTRUCTURAL\nFLEXIBILITY\nSOURCES\nCAN DEGRADE\nTHE SPECIFIED\nVALUE\n\nCAN\n\nCAN\n\nEXCITE\n\nEXCITE\n\nCAN\n\nDESTABILIZE\n\nSTRUCTURAL\n\nSTRUCTURAL\n\nALONG\nWITH INHERENT\nPOINTING\nPERFORMANCE\n\nFigure\n\n14\n\ninvolving\nthe dynamics\nissues\nare common\nto\nflexible\ncomponents,\nsignificant\nin the\n\n3\n\nERROR\nBEYOND\n\nADDITIONAL\n\nN-ROSS\n\nISSUES\n\nThe design\nand concept\nof operations\nfor the baseline\nN-ROSS\ndesign\nraises\nseveral\nspecific\nissues,\nrelated\nto control-structure\ninteraction,\nbut\nnot typically\naddressed\nin the development\nof technologies\nfor the control\nof\nlarge space\nstructures.\nWhile the LFMR is designed\nto operate\nat a constant\nspin rate,\nthe initial\nspin up (and contingency\ndespin)\nof the sensor\nraises\nconcern\nthat it might\nact as a frequency\nsweep disturbance\ninput to the\nspacecraft,\nwith the potential\nto excite\nstructural\nresonances\nup to 0.26 Hz\n(15.8\nrpm).\nAdditionally,\nthe LFMR antenna\nand support\nbooms are expected\nto\ndeform\nmeasurably\nunder\ncentrifugal\nforces when spinning\n(which\nis taken into\naccount\nin the design,\nso that the deformed\nconfiguration\nhas the desired\ngeometry).\nThe deformation\nwill result\nin a change\nin mass properties,\nthereby\ninducing\nboth a static\nand a dynamic\nimbalance.\nThis then is expected\nto lead to a requirement\nfor an on-orbit\nbalance\nmechanism.\nFinally,\nthe\nmomentum\nof the LFMR and the SSM/I are each proposed\nto be compensated\nby a\nseparate\nmomentum\nwheel\ncontrolled\nindependent\nof the reaction-wheel-based\nattitude\ncontrol\nsystem.\nThese separate\ncontrol\nloops,\nall coupled\nthrough\nthe vehicle\nrigid body dynamics,\ncan lead to a system\nwhich\ncannot\nbe\nguaranteed\n\nto\n\nbe\n\nstable\n\nADDITIONAL\n\nSPIN-UP\n\nOPERATIONS\n\nLFMR\nMAY\nREQUIRE\nSTATIC\nDEFLECTIONS\n\nMOMENTUM\n\nfor\n\nall\n\ninputs.\n\nCONCERNS\n\nMAY\n\nON-ORBIT\nUNDER\n\nCOMPENSATION\n\nSWEEP\n\nSPECIFIC\n\nSTRUCTURAL\n\nBALANCE\nSPIN\n\nREQUIRES\nFigure\n\nTO\n\nTO N-ROSS\n\nRESONANCES\n\nCOMPENSATE\n\nSEPARATE\n\nCONTROL\n\nFOR\n\nLOOP\n\n4\n\n15\n\nDYNAMICS\nANDCONTROL\nCONTRIBUTORS\nManyaspects of the N-ROSS\nbaseline design have the potential to\ncontribute to a control-structure\ninteraction problem for this vehicle.\nFigure 5 summarizesthe most significant\nof these. They include interacting\nflexible structures and rotating instruments and devices on the vehicle,\nindependently designed and implemented control systems that are coupled\nthrough either vehicle dynamics or structural dynamics, and external\ndisturbances that have the potential to degrade pointing performance and even\ndestabilize the attitude of the satellite.\n\nCONTRIBUTING\n\nFLEXIBLE\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nLFMR Reflector\n\nand\n\nLFMR Deployment\nNSCAT\n\nSOURCES\n\nROTATING\nLFMR\n\nBooms\n\nSSM/I\n\nBoom\n\nAntennas\n\nMomentum\nReaction\n\nSolar Array\n\nCONTROL\nAttitude\n\nSYSTEMS\n\nCOMPONENTS\n\nOTHER\n\nControl\n\nThruster\n\nCompensation\nWheels\n\nDYNAMICS\nfiring\n\nLFMR\n\nDrive\n\nSystem\n\nDeployment\n\nSSM/I\n\nDrive\n\nSystem\n\nExternal\n\nMCA Drives\nFigure\n\n16\n\n5\n\nsequences\n\ntorques\n\nAssemblies\n\nSTATIC\n\nas the\ndevice\n\nAND\n\nDYNAMIC\n\nBALANCE\n\nThe static\nand dynamic\nbalance\nof a deformable\nspinning\ninstrument\nsuch\nLFMR warrants\nspecial\nexamination.\nIn balancing\na rigid spinning\n(such as the SSM/I),\nthe static\nand dynamic\nbalance\ncan be performed\n\nsequentially.\nIn a nonspinning\nstate,\nthe center\nof mass can be adjusted\nto\nlie on the spin axis.\nThe dynamic\nbalance\ncan then be achieved\nby spinning\nthe sensor,\nand symmetrically\nadjusting\nballast\nmass to eliminate\n(or reduce)\nthe cross-products\nof inertia\nwith respect\nto the spin axis.\nFor the SSM/I,\nthis will be accomplished\nin ground\ntest prior to integration\nwith the\nsatellite.\nFor\n\nan\n\nasymmetric\n\nflexible\n\nstructure\n\nsuch\n\nas\n\nthe\n\nLFMR,\n\nthe\n\ncenter\n\nof\n\nmass and inertias\nof the structure\nwill change\nwith spin rate, and the\nalignment\nof both the center\nof mass and the principal\ninertia\naxis can only\nbe accomplished\nafter the instrument\nis spinning.\nThese same mass properties\nalso vary between\na one-g and a zero-g\nenvironment,\nand between\natmosphere\nvacuum.\nThis leads to a requirement\nfor either\nextensive\ntesting\ncoupled\nsimulation\nto extrapolate\nto on-orbit\nconditions,\nor an active\nmethod\nof\nachieving\n\ninstrument\n\nFOR\n\nA RIGID\n\nSTATIC\n\n-\n\nDYNAMIC\n\nFOR\n\nbalance\n\nthe\n\nvehicle\n\nis\n\nin orbit.\n\nSTRUCTURE\nPLACE\n-\n\nA FLEXIBLE\n\nC.G. AND\n\nonce\n\nand\nwith\n\nC.G. ON THE SPIN AXIS\n\nALIGN\n\nTHE\n\nPRINCIPAL\n\nINERTIA\n\nAXIS WITH\n\nSPIN AXIS\n\nSTRUCTURE\n\nINERTIA\n\nAXES\n\nWILL MOVE\n\nBOTH "STATIC"\nAND "DYNAMIC"\nAT FULL SPIN RATE\n\nAS INSTRUMENT\n\nBALANCE\n\nFigure\n\nIS SPUN\n\nUP\n\nMUST BE ACHIEVED\n\n6\n\n17\n\nDYNAMIC\n\nSTABILITY\n\nSTUDY\n\nIn response\nto the recognition\nthat control\nstructure\ninteraction\nwas a\ntechnology\ndriver\nfor the N-ROSS\nbaseline\nsatellite\ndesign,\nthe Naval Research\nLaboratory\nwas commissioned\nin September\n1985 to lead a six month\neffort\nto\nevaluate\nthe N-ROSS/LFMR\nconfiguration.\nA Dynamic\nStability\nStudy would\nfocus\non the baseline\nconfiguration,\nassuming\na design\nfrozen\nto that detailed\nin\nthe April\n1985 conceptual\ndesign\nreview.\nThe study objectives\nare recounted\nin Fig. 7.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n\nDEVELOP\nINTEGRATED\nFLEXIBLE\nAND CONTROL\nSIMULATION\nOF\n\nDETERMINE\nOPERATION\n\nATTITUDE\nSTABILITY\nOF THE LFMR\n\nASSESS THE\nINTERACTIONS\n\nEXAMINE\nMARGINS\nBASELINE\n\nBODY STRUCTURAL\nDYNAMICS\nTHE ON-ORBIT\nN-ROSS\nCONFIGURATION\n\nIN\n\nAND\n\nCONTRIBUTION\nOF STRUCTURE\nAND\nTO LFMR BORESIGHT\nPOINTING\n\nOFF-NOMINAL\nCONDITIONS\nTO\nAND PARAMETER\nSENSITIVITIES\nDESIGN\nFigure\n\n18\n\nSPIN-UP\n\n7\n\nDETERMINE\nINHERENT\n\nSTEADY-STATE\n\nCONTROL\n\nCONTROL\nIN THE\n\nDYNAMIC\nSTABILITYSTUDY\nPARTICIPANTS\nThe original organization of the study called for two independent teams\nof investigators, using software tools and simulation techniques of their own\nchoosing but considering a common\ndesign database, to each assemble an\nintegrated simulation capable of addressing the four study objectives.\nThe\noriginal teaming arrangements paired RCAwith Aerospace Corp. and Harris with\nCambridge Research. During the course of the study the government announced\nits intention to competitively procure the N-ROSS\nsatellite;\nat that point\nHarris and RCAchose to voluntarily cease further participation\nin the study.\nUsing control system and structural models previously developed by these two\nparticipants,\nthe two remaining team members\ncontinued to develop the\nintegrated simulations.\nThe MULTIFLEX\ncode was developed internally at\nAerospace for this purpose, while CambridgeResearch employed the DISCOS\ncode\noriginally developed at Martin Marietta for NASA\nGoddard Space Flight Center.\n\nRCA\n\nASTROELECTRONICS\n\nProvided\n\nvehicle\n\nstructural\n\nProvided\n\nattitude\n\ncontrol\n\nHARRIS\n\nmodels\nsystem\n\nmodel\n\nGASD\n\nProvided\n\nLFMR structural\n\nProvided\n\ndrive motor\n\nAEROSPACE\nDeveloped\n\nCAMBRIDGE\nDeveloped\n\nmodel\n\nand MCA control\n\nmodels\n\nCORP.\nintegrated\n\nsimulation\n\nusing\n\nMULTIFLEX\n\nsimulation\n\nusing\n\nDISCOS\n\nRESEARCH\nintegrated\n\nFigure\n\n8\n\n19\n\nCOMMON\nASSUMPTIONS\nANDGROUND\nRULES\nThe two remaining study participants continued their work independently, with the Naval Research Laboratory maintaining a common\nand consistent\nset of model data to be used by both parties.\nNRLalso provided resolution of\nmodeling issues raised by the participants and defined the scope and\nlimitations of the simulations and analyses to be performed.\nFigure 9 lists the principal modeling assumptions. The number of\nstructural modesincluded for each of the flexible components, together with\nthe total numberof states in the simulation, are listed to the right. These\nare taken from the Aerospace simulation; Cambridge Research employed two\nmodels - the first with 63 states modeled only the LFMR flexible,\nas\nthe\nsecond included all flexible appendagesand contained 109 states.\n\n(modes\nRigid\n\nspacecraft\n\nbus\n\nDetailed attitude\nmodelreaction\n\ndetermination\nwheel significant\nconcerns,\nand those which\nreceived\ncareful\nexamination\nduring\nthe course of the study,\nwere the coupling\nof the LFMR spin frequency\nand the lower solar array modes with the 15.1\n\nFigure\n\ntO\n\n21\n\nISSUES\nThe\n\ndevelopment\n\nof\n\nextensive\n\nCONSIDERED\nintegrated\n\ncontrol-structure\n\nsimulations\n\nprovided\nthe opportunity\nto examine\na wide range of issues\nof concern\nin the\nbaseline\ndesign.\nThe list of issues\nexamined,\nsummarized\nin Fig. II, attests\nto the capacity\nof such simulations\nto go far beyond\nthe relatively\nstraightforward\ntask of demonstrating\nstability\nand determining\noverall\nsteady-state\nstructure\nand control\nperformance.\nSuch simulations\ncan be used effectively\nto refine\nthe design\nfor a particular\nconcept.\nResults\nof the N-ROSS\nsimulations\nled directly\nto recommendations\nfor revised\nLFMR imbalance\nspecifications\nand improved\nvalues\nfor attitude\ncontrol\nsubsystem\nloop gains.\n\nINDIVIDUAL\n\nISSUES\n\nSTEADY\n\n- STATE\n\nEFFECT\n\nOF STATIC\n\nEFFECT\n\nOF efforts,\nthe N-ROSS\nDynamic\nStability\nStudy team\nconcluded\nby consensus\nthat the frozen\nApril 1985 design\nwas viable\nand\ncontained\nno "show stoppers",\nalthough\nit was also clear from the study\nresults\nthat the configuration\nrequired\nfurther optimization.\nWhile\nthe\nfrozen\nN-ROSS\nconfiguration\nused has since been superceded,\nand the vehicle\nis\nnow under\ncompetitive\nprocurement,\nseveral\nother results\nremain\nfrom the study\nthat will have lasting\nvalue\nto the N-ROSS\nprogram.\nThe importance\nof\nconstructing\nan integrated\nsimulation,\nto serve as a design\nand verification\naid, has been clearly\nestablished.\nThe two team approach\nto the study\nafforded\nthe Navy a higher\ndegree\nof confidence\nin the results\nthan could have\nbeen accomplished\nby a single\nsimulation,\nand the approach\nled to results\nthat\nhighlighted\nsubtleties\nin the model and simulation\ndevelopment\nthat surely\nwould\nhave been overlooked\nwithout\nthe benefit\nof an independent\ncompanion\nsimulation\nwith which to compare.\n\nCONCLUSIONS\nN-ROSS\nAPRIL 1985 1986\n\n25\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nA number of future DOD and NASA spacecraft\nsuch as the space based radar will be not\nonly an order of magnitude\nlarger\nin dimension\nthan the current\nspacecraft,\nbut will\nexhibit\nextreme\nstructural\nflexibility\nwith very\nlow structural\nvibration\nfrequencies.\nAnother\nclass of spacecraft\n(such as the space defense platforms)\nwill combine\nlarge\nphysical size with extremely\nprecise pointing\nrequirement.\nSuch problems\nrequire a total\ndeparture\nfrom\nthe traditional\nmethods\nof modeling\nand control\nsystem\ndesign of\nspacecraft\nwhere structural\nflexibility\nis treated\nas a secondary\neffect.\nWith these\nproblems\nin mind, the Air Force Rocket Propulsion\nLaboratory\n(AFRPL)\ninitiated\nresearch\nto develop\ndynamics\nand control\ntechnology\nso as to enable\nthe future\nlarge space\nstructures\n(LSS).\nAFRPL\'s\neffort\nin this area can be subdivided\ninto the following\nthree overlapping\nareas:\n(a) Ground\nExperiments,\n(b) Spacecraft\nModeling\nand Control,\nand (c) Sensors\nand\nActuators.\nThis paper summarizes\nboth the in-house and contractual\nefforts\nof the\nAFRPL\nin LSS.\nHowever,\nonly Air\nForce\nfunded\nprograms\nare discussed,\nongoing\nStrategic\nDefense Initiative\nOffice funded efforts\nare not covered in this paper.\n\nGround Experiments\n* Spacecraft Slew\n* Vibration Control\n* Shape Determination & Control\n* System Identification\n\nSpacecraft Modeling & Control\n* Deployment Dynamics\n* System Identification\n* Modeling & Control\n\nSensors & Actuators\n* Distributed Piezoelectric Actuation\n* Distributed Fiber Optic Sensor\n\nFigure\n\n26\n\ni.\n\nORlGlNAL PAGE ?S\nOF POOR QUALITY\nSLEWING AND VIBRATION SUPPRESSION FOR FLEXIBLE STRUCTURES\n\nA number o f future DOD and NASA space systems will require rapid slewing\n(retargeting) of the spacecraft. The large moment of inertia of such spacecraft coupled\nwith rapid retargeting requirement results in slew torque requirement o f IO5 - I06 Nm.\nCurrently only ON-OFF reaction control system thrusters can provide the large torque\nrequired, however, ON-OFF thrusters by their very nature have the tendency of\nsignificantly vibrating the structure. The objective of this project was t o develop control\nlaws using thrusters for spacecraft slew which minimize structural vibration and\ndemonstrate the theory on a ground experiment.\nThe experimental set-up consists of a rigid hub on which four flexible appendages\nare mounted. The hub i s mounted on an air bearing table, thus allowing it t o rotate freely\nabout the vertical axis. hlb control thrusters are mounted in pairs on the ends of two\narms. Sensors consist of a hub angle resolver and 4 accelerometers, one on each arm (fig.\n\n2)\n\nF i g u r e 2.\n27\n\nSLEWING\n\nAND\n\nVIBRATION\n\nSUPPRESSION\n\nUSING\n\nON-OFF\n\nTHRUSTERS\n\nIn the initial\nset of experiments,\nthe thrusters\nwere used to perform\nIS-degree\nslew\nmaneuvers.\nA computer\nsimulation\nprogram was developed\nto predict\nthe performance\nof\nthe control\nsystem.\nFig. 3 shows the performance\nof the controller\nfor one of the tests\n(ref.\nI).\nThe solid lines correspond\nto the simulation\nresults whereas\nthe dots are the\nexperimental\nresults.\nThe figure gives the response of the first\nthree modes (YI - hub\nrotation\nin degrees; Y2, Y3 - amplitude\nof first\nand second vibration\nmodes in milli g,\nrespectively).\nThe experiment\ndemonstrated\nthat\nON-OFF\nthrusters\ncan be used for rapid slew of\nflexible\nspace structures\nwhile minimizing\nstructural\nvibration.\nHowever,\nthe exclusive\nuse of thrusters\nlimits the performance\nof the control\nsystem in the terminal\nphase of the\nslew maneuver.\nThis is because of the minimum\nimpulse bit of the thruster\nsystem, thus\nany attempt\nto dampen structural\nvibrations\nbelow a particular\nenergy level throws the\ncontrol\nsystem into limit cycles.\nOne way of overcoming\nthis problem\nis to include small\nlinear actuators\nin the control\nsystem.\nThis was confirmed\nby computer\nsimulations.\n\n1\n\ni\n\n-\n\ni\n\ni\nmr\n\nq.t.-\n\n=!\n_1-10\n\n-15\n\ni\n\n!/\n\n!i\n\nZ\n\n0\n\n- 15 \xc2\xb0 Slew, Rotation\n\nSLEWING\n\nAND\n\nVIBRATION\n\nSUPPRESSION\n\nFOR FLEXIBLE\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nOne of the conclusions\nof the earlier\nstudy was that vibration\ncontrol\nof the flexible\nspacecraft\nstructure\nduring\nslew can be significantly\nimproved\nwhen linear\nactuation\ndevices are used in combination\nwith thrusters.\nIn the current\neffort\nboth lumped and\ndistributed\nlinear\nactuators\nwill be used.\nControl\nlaws are currently\nbeing developed\nwhich use, along with ON-OFF\nthrusters,\nthe hub motor,\nfour bi-directional\nproof mass\nactuators\n(one on the tip of each flexible\narm) and piezoelectric\nfilm as a distributed\nvibration\nsuppression actuator\non all four arms.\n\nFlexing Structure Slew Control\n* AFRPL/CSDL\n\nExperimental\n\nFigure\n\nStructure\n\n4.\n\n29\n\nADVANCED\n\nDISTRIBUTED\n\nPIEZOELECTRIC\n\nACTUATOR\n\nOne of the most exciting\nconcepts\nfor structural\nvibration\ncontrol\nis the use \xc2\xa3f a\npiezoelectric\nfilm as a distributed\nactuator.\nThe concept\nconsists of a thin (\'v3xl0-Zmm\nin thickness)polyvinylidene\nfloride\nfilm polarized\nin one direction.\nThe film elongates\nor\ncontracts\nin the polarized\ndirection\ndepending\non the magnitude\nand polarity\nof the\nvoltage\napplied across its nickel plated faces (fig. S). The strain occurs over the entire\nlength of the film,\nthus making\nit a truely\ndistributed\nactuator.\nThis actuator\nprovides\nthe possibility\nof controlling\nall the modes of the system,\nthus avoiding\nthe problem\nof\nspillover\nof the uncontrolled\nmodes (ref. 2).\nPreliminary\ntesting of the actuator\nconcept were done on a I Scm long cantilevered\nsteel\nbeam.\nFig. S compares\nthe performance\nof two active control\nlogics with the open loop\ndecay of the vibrations.\nThe constant-amplitude\ncontroller\n(a nonlinear\ncontroller)\nprovided\ndouble the damping\nof the uncontrolled\nsystem for large vibrations.\nFor small\nvibrations\nthe controller\nperformed\nextremely\nwell, increasing\nthe damping by a factor of\n40 over the uncontrolled\nsystem for small vibrations.\n\nPVF 2 Properties\n28 X 10 -3 mm\n_ickei\n\nPlating\n\nV(x)\n\n_\nPVF2\n\nIj\n\nPolymer\n\n2.0 X 109 Nm 2\n\nThickness\nModulus, E\n\n1800 kgm payoff\nis the possibility\nof using a fiber\noptic waveguide\nas a lightweight\ndistributed\nsensor.\nA short and intense pulse of laser\nlight is injected\ninto one end of the fiber.\nThe forward\ntraveling\npulse is continuously\nbackscattered\ntowards\nthe source\ndue to Rayleigh\nscattering,\nObservation\nof the\nRayleigh\nbackscattered\nenergy is made by placing a beam splitter\nbetween\nthe fiber and\nthe source and directing\nthe returned\nenergy at a high speed detector.\nThe fiber is bonded\nto the structure\nwhose deformation\nis to be monitored.\nThe fiber is made to pass through\na small transducer\n(see fig. 6) at the locations\nin the structure\nwhere the bending is to be\ndetermined,\nThe transducer\npinches the fiber thus reducing\nthe return\nsignal from that\npoint.\nThe reduction\nin the return\nsignal\nis proportional\nto the structural\nbending,\nCurrently\nthe AFRPL\nis funding\na proof of concept\ndemonstration\nof the lightweight\ndistributed\nsensor,\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Demonstrate Feasibility of Distributed\nFiber Optic Deflection Sensor\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Lightweight\n\nDistributed selected\nflexible\nstructures\nusing a physical\n\nshape determination\nand\nstructure\nin a laboratory\n\nThe test article\nconsists of an umbrella-like\nstructure\nwith loft\nlong ribs and a 12ft long\nboom.\nThe neighboring\nribs are connected\nto each other through a pair of tension wires so\nas to provide coupling.\nThe article\nis rigidly\nsupported\nat the center\nto the backup\nstructure,\nalso each rib is levitated\nto offset the effects\nof gravity.\nThe levitation\nsystem\nallows the ribs to freely flex in a vertical\nplane.\nAn exciter\nin the feed will be used to\nintroduce\nnoise in the structure.\nMultiple\nsensors and actuators such as the SHAPES\nsensor under development\nat the Jet Propulsion\nLaboratory,\nrib control force actuator,\n2directional\ndisplacement\nsensor on the feed, etc., will be attached\nto the structure.\nThe\nexperiments\nto be conducted\nunder this effort\ninclude (a) static\nshape determination,\n(b)\ndynamic\nmodel identification,\n(c) transient\nregulation\nby distributed\ncontrol,\nand (cl)\nparameter\nerror and model truncation\ncompensation\nusing Space-based\n\nFigure\n\n32\n\n7.\n\nRadar\n\nIN-HOUSE\n\nEXPERIMENTS\n\nIN CONTROL\n\nAND\n\nIDENTIFICATION\n\nThe objective\nof the recently\ninitiated\nin-house experimental\neffort\nis to provide\nthe\nAFRPL\nwith\nthe capability\nof (a) verifying\nnew parameter\nidentification\nand control\nmethods being developed\nand (b) performing\na quick feasibility\ncheck on new innovative\nideas prior to substantial\ninvestment\nin that area. Most importantly,\nthis facility\nwill be\nmade available\nto university\nfaculty\nand DOD contractors\nfor conducting\nexperiments.\nThe details\nof the means through which the facility\ncan be made available\nare currently\nbeing worked on.\nThe approach\ninvolves\ndesigning\nthe control\nsystem on a control\ndesign software\npackage\n(e.g.,MATRIXx)\nand then directly\nimplementing\nit on a desktop real-time\ncontroller.\nIn\nthis approach,\nthe control\nsystem is down loaded to the controller\nand implemented\nin a\nmatter\nof minutes without\nthe engineer having to program the controller.\nIn the immediate\nfuture,\nthe plan calls for testing the optimal\nsensor location\nlogic for\nunique identification\n(being developed\nunder a separate\neffort)\non a 2-dimensional\ngrid\nstructure.\nAlso work has just begun on a new facility\nwith a test stand which is anchored\ndirectly\nto the bedrock and isolated\nfrom the rest of the structure\nto minimize\nvibrations.\nThis facility\nshould be available\nfor use in roughly one year.\nIn addition,\nAFRPL\nhas a\nnumber of vacuum chambers of various sizes, the largest one being 30 ft in diameter.\n\nWHY:\n* Provide Feasibility Check on New Ideas\n* Provide Test Bed for Control & Identification Logic Developed for AFRPL\nw Provide an Equipped Facility for University Faculty/Students\nHOW:\n* Use Simple 1, 2, & 3-D Structures\n* Use Design Software Coupled with Real-Time Control/System\nIdentification Hardware for Rapid Turnaround\nw Up to 3Oft Diameter Vacuum Chambers Available\nCURRENT PLAN:\n* Cantilevered Beam & Grid Structure\nt Use MATRIX x + Real-Time Controller\n" Experiments\n\xe2\x80\xa2 System Identification,\n\nVibration Suppression,\n\nFigure\n\nNew Sensor & Actuator,\n\n...\n\n8.\n33\n\nDEPLOYMENT\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nOF LARGE\n\nSPACE\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nA number of future spacecraft\nsuch as Space Based Radar (SBR) will be packaged\ninto a\ncompact\nstructurally\ndense form for launch, and the spacecraft\nwill automatically\nunfold\nto the operational\nstate once in orbit.\nThe anticipated\nlarge structural\nsize ( > 30\nmeters) coupled with extreme\nstructural\nflexibility\nprecludes\nextensive\nground testing of\nthe deploying\nprocess.\nAn alternate\napproach\nto obtain\nconfidence\nin the spacecraft\ndeployment\nis to simulate\nthe dynamics on a computer.\nNone of the currently\navailable\ncomputer\nprograms\n_an simulate\nthe deployment\nof a\nlarge\nclass of spacecraft.\nFor\ndeployment\nstudies,\nDISCOS\nhas proven\nto be\ncomputationally\ninefficient\nand requires\nsignificant\nmodifications\nfor each application.\nSince 1982, AFRPL\nhas made significant\ninvestments\nfor the development\nof a general\npurpose spacecraft\ndeployment\nprediction\ncode.\n\nState-of-the-Art\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Analysis\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Dynamic\n\nInteractive\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Requires\n\nSimulation\n\nSignificant\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Computationally\n\nof Controls\n\nModification\n\nand Structures\n\n(DISCOS)\n\nfor Each Application\n\nInefficient\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Experiments\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Ground\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Mostly Limited to Simple Structures/Elements\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Terrestrial\nEffects also Limit Utility\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Space\n\nExperiments\n\nTechnology\n\nfrom\n\nShuttle\n\nin FY89\n\nPayoff\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Reliable and Accurate Computer\nSimulation of Large Space Systems\nto Minimize Number of Tests\nRequired on any Given Structure\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Deployment Simulation\nof Large Space Systems\n\nTransition Target\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Space Based Radar\n\nFigure\n\n34\n\n(by Size}\n\n9.\n\nLARGE\n\nSPACE\n\nSTRUCTURE\n\nDEPLOYMENT\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nAFRPL\'s\ninitial\nstudy in the development\nof a deployment\ndynamics\ncomputer\nprogram\nconsisted\nof two major tasks.\nIn Task 1, a new mathematical\nformulation\nfor simulating\nthe deploying\nprocess was developed and a preliminary\ndesign of the software\narchitecture\nwas laid out. In Task 2, a pilot computer\nprogram was developed\nto test the mathematical\nformulations.\nThe computer\nprogram\nis not a general\npurpose code and is set up to\nsimulate\nthe deployment\nof the Grumman\nbicycle wheel SBR concept only (see ref. 3 for\ndetails).\nIn its present form, the code is fairly\ncomputer\nintensive,\nrequiring\nfour hours of\nCPU time on a VAX\n11-750 computer\nfor a 4310 second deployment\nsimulation.\nThe\nspacecraft\nwas modeled\nas consisting\nof eight tubes and eight bridges.\nThe deployed\nportion\nof each gore (the radar reflector)\nwas replaced by two cables.\nEight stays (four\neach connected\nto the upper and lower ends of the hub) provide stiffness\nto the structure\nand can stabilize\nthe deployment.\nIn the current\nversion of the code, torsional\nsprings\nbetween\nthe bridges and tubes are the source of energy for deployment.\nAn alternate\napproach is to use motors instead of springs.\n\nTime\n\nTime\n\n= 0 sec\n\n= 3000\n\nTime\n\nFigure\n\nsec\n\nTime\n\nsec\n\n= 1500\n\n= 4310\n\nsec\n\ni0.\n35\n\nDEPLOYMENT\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nSOFTWARE\n\nDEVELOPMENT\n\nThe objective\nof the ongoing program\nis to develop a general\npurpose computer\ncode for\nsimulating\nthe deployment\ndynamics\nof a large class of future\nspace structures.\nThe\neffort\nconsists of 5 major\ntasks.\nIn Task 1, future\nDOD and NASA missions\nwill\nbe\nreviewed\nfor their\ndeployment\nneeds.\nAlso an assessment\nof the state of the art in\ndeployment\ntechnologies\nwill be made.\nIn Task 2, which\nis the heart of the effort,\nextended\nmodeling\ncapability\nsuch as active hinge point control\nand mass flow modeling\nwill be incorporated\nin the code.\nThe program\nwill have the capability\nof evaluating\nthe\nimpact of distributed\nenvironmental\ndisturbances\nsuch as gravity\ngradient,\nmagnetic\nfield\ntorques,\netc., on the deployment\nprocess.\nThe capability\nof linearizing\nthe nonlinear\nsystem about an operating\npoint of interest\nso as to study the system stability\nwill be\nprovided.\nAlso an entanglement\nindicator\nin the form\nof a post processor\nshall be\ndeveloped.\nA host of extended\nsoftware\nfeatures\nwill be incorporated\nin the code under\nTask 3. This is to include common\ni-O structure,\nrestart\ncapability,\nCAD interfaces\nand\ngraphics\noutputs, etc.\nTask 4 shall investigate\nthe impact of nonlinear\nstructures\neffects.\nTechniques\nfor generating\nopen and closed loop deployment\nsequences shall be developed\nunder Task 5. Also under this task the closed loop response of a number of test examples\nwill\n\nbe generated.\n\nDeployment\n\nState-of-the-Art\n\nExtended Analysis\n* Active\n* future\nlarge space\nstructures\nwill\nrequire\nan accurate\nmathematical\nmodel\nof the plant.\nCurrently\nmathematical\nmodels are obtained\nusing either distributed\nor lumped parameter\nmethods.\nIn the latter\ncategory,\nfinite\nelement\nmodeling\ncodes(e.g., NASTRAN)\nhave become very\npopular.\nHowever,\nunmodeled\nvariations\nin the physicalmaterial\nproperties\nand\nunmodeled\nnonlinearities\nsuch as joints result in large errors in the frequency\nand shape\nof the higher modes.\nFor the current\nclass of spacecraft,\nextensive\nground based modal\ntesting\ncan be done to validate/update\nthe mathematical\nmodel.\nHowever,\nfor future\nlarge space structures,\nthe large physical\nsize coupled\nwith\nin some cases extreme\nstructural\nflexibility\nwill preclude\naccurate\nground based parameter\nidentification\ntests.\nA solution\nto this problem,\nwhich has gained popularity,\nis to perform\non orbit system\nidentification.\nCurrently\nthere are available\na large class of methods which have been\nused for ground based parameter\nidentification\nof diverse plants.\nIt is, however,\nnot clear\nwhich\nof these\nmethods,\nif any,\ncould\nbe adapted\nto perform\non-orbit\nsystem\nidentification.\nWith this in mind, AFRPL\nfunded a study with the American\nSociety\nof\nCivil\nEngineers.\nThis study (being performed\nby a committee\nsystem identification)\nwill identify\nareas requiring\nfuture effort.\n\nof experts\n\nin the\n\narea IDENTIFICATION\n\nMathematical\nmodeling\nof dynamic\nsystems\ninvolves\nthe creation\nof a model\nby the\nanalyst and prediction\nof the response of the system to given excitations.\nThe idea is to\nmeasure\nthe dynamic\nresponses\nfor the same inputs\nand update\nthe model\nso as to\nminimize\nthe difference\nbetween\nthe predicted\nand measured\nresponses.\nHowever,\ncontrary\nto expectations,\nit was found that elimination\nof the mismatch\nin the time\nhistories\nwas not sufficient\nto guarantee\nunique identification\n(ref. 4). Also a preliminary\nstudy revealed that by properly\nlocating\nthe sensors, one can obtain unique identification.\nThe objective\nfor optimally\nidentification\n\nof this effort\nis to develop and test by computer\nlocating\nsensors\nin a large\nspace structure\nof its structural\nmodel.\n\nsimulations\nso as to\n\na methodology\nenable\nunique\n\nProblems With Current Techniques\n* Identification Problem is in General ill Posed\n* Large Number of Sensors Required\n* Large Amount of Data Generated\n\nIssues Addressed\n\nin Current Effort\n\n* Optimal Sensor Location for Unique Identification\n* Minimum Number of Sensors Required\n* Data Compression Techniques to Alleviate Data Handling\n* Numerical Studies to Assess the Efficacy of Methods Propose(\n\nFigure\n\n38\n\n13.\n\nMODELING\n\nAND\n\nCONTROL\n\nOF FLEXIBLE\n\nRecent research\nin control\nsystem theory\nshowed that\ndelay in the feedback\nloop (regardless\nof how small the\nof instability\nin the system (ref. 5). The FDL is responsible\nfor the planning and execution of research and development programs in\nthe areas of structures and dynamics, flight controls, vehicle equipment/\nsubsystems, and aeromechanics. Someof the areas being researched in the\nfour FDL divisions are as follows:\nlarge space structures (LSS)\nmaterials and controls; advanced cockpit designs; blrd-strike-tolerant\nwindshields; and hypersonic interceptor system studies.\nTwo of the FDL\ndivisions are actively involved in programs that deal directly with LSS\ncontrol/structures\ninteraction:\nthe Flight Controls Division and the\nStructures and DynamicsDivision.\n\nFlight\n\nControls Division\n\nAreas of Research\n\nThe Flight Controls Division has several programs that address control/\nstructures interaction technology for large space structures.\nThese\nprograms include pointing and shape control studies for large (lO0-meter)\nradar systems and robust control systems development. Research is\nperformed in advanced controls/flghter\ntechnology integration,\nattitude\nand trajectory control for hypersonic vehicles, advanced cockpit designs,\nand flight simulator technology.\n\nStructures and Dynamics Division\n\nAreas of Research\n\nSomeof the technology areas being developed in the Structures and\nDynamics Division are advanced composite aircraft\nand spacecraft\nstructures, cast aluminum structures technology, stores/aircraft\nflutter\ntesting, and structures/dynamics/controls\ninteraction.\nPrograms have\nalso been developed to research LSS control hardware, passive and active\nvibration control, suspension systems, and design optimization.\n\n42\n\nORlGMAL PAGE W\nOF POOR QUALnv\nLarge Space Structure Concept\nMany defense systems have been proposed that incorporate space based\nstructures an order of magnitude larger than those in orbit today. With\nthe tremendous size and adverse environment associated with these systems\ncome many challenges to the design and test engineers. Weight\nlimitations imposed on LSS designs have made them much less rigid. This\nhigh flexibility combined with low damping results in structures very\nsensitive to disturbance forces. The structural precision required by\nmany proposed missions creates the need for passive and active control\nsystems. In t h e figure below i s a space-based l a s e r system concept whose\nflexibility, required precision, and size (approx. 15-m dia.) necessitate\nthe use of combined passive and active control systems and specialized\nground test procedures.\n\n43\n\nTechnology\n\nDrivers\n\nDevelopment\nof large space structure\ncontrol\nsystems\nis hampered\nby the\ndifficulty\nof the necessary\nperformance\nvalidation\nground\ntests.\nWhile\nsome systems\nare too large\nfor full-scale\ntesting\nand/or\ndo not lend\nthemselves\nwell to scaling\ntechniques,\nthe dynamics\nof a system\non the\nground\nmay be radically\nchanged\nwhen placed\nin orbit.\nThe microgravity,\nlow atmospheric\npressure,\nthermal\ncycling\nenvironment\nof earth orbit\nproduce\nmaterial\nproperty\nchanges\nand unique\nstructural\nloads that may\ndegrade\ncontrol\nsystem\nperformance.\nWhat\nis needed\nare technologies\nthat\nallow\nearth-bound\ndevelopment\nand testing\nof LSS control\nsystems\nto\nensure\nreliable\non-orbit\nsystem\nperformance.\nThe figure\nbelow\nillustrates\nsome of the LSS technology\ndrivers.\n\nLARGE\nSIZE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SCALINGLAWS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 GROUND\nTESTTECHNIQUES\n\nFLEXIBILITY\n\xc2\xb0 ACTIVE& PASSIVECONTROL\n\nHIGHPERFORMANCE\n\xc2\xb0 Convair Division, has\ndefined mission drivers and environmental factors, performed control\ntrade-off studies, and developed control algorithms for a realistic\nlarge\nflexible space antenna. PVC\ntubing used in the facility development portion of the LSSTP. The\nstructure was used to test and develop sensing, actuation, and data\nanalysis equipment and methods. The 40-foot truss testbed is shown\nlocated in the large chamber of the Flight Dynamics Laboratory Sonic\nFatigue Facility. Designed for the vibration and acoustic testing of\nlarge aircraft and missiles, the chamber is over 12 meters high, 17\nmeters wide, and 21 meters long. With its large size and excellent data\ngathering and analysis equipment, the facility is ideal for large space\nstructures testing.\n\n54\n\nLarge Space Structures Active Vibration\n\nControl\n\nThe objectives of Large Space Structures Active Vibration Control program\nare to verify and extend the results of laboratory control tests. Through\nground tests of a fifth-scale\nversion of the NASA-LangleyControl of\nFlexible Structures (COFS)60-meter deployable beam, one-g test\nprocedures will be developed and verified.\nActive and passive control\ntechniques will be tested on the scale beamin the AFWAL\ntest facilities\nand the results will be validated with data from the shuttle flight tests\nof the full-scale\nhardware. Figure 1 i s a n\na r t i s t \' s i l l u s t r a t i o n of a representative spacecraft w i t h a v offset-feed\nantenna t h a t will require active ccntrol o f the alignment of the feed\nniast. The Air Force goal i s t o develop vibration control techniques for\n1arge f l exi b l e spacecraft by addressi ny sensor, actuator, and control\nhardware and dynamic testing. The Pctive Coritrol Evaluation f o r Spacecraft\n(ACES) program will address the Air Force goal by looking a t two leading\ncontrol techniques and implementing them on a structural model of a flexible\nspacecraft under 1aboratory testing.\n\n.\n\nFigure 1\n\n68\n\nACTIVE\n\nCONTROL\n\nOF SPACE STRUCTURES\n\n(ACOSS)\n\nSIUDIES\n\nThe control techniques that will be studied in this program were initially\ninvestigated in the Active Contro_ Of Space Structures (ACOSS) program\nsponsored by Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (DARPA).\nThe ACOSS\nprogram started in 1978; in six years it involved eighteen contracts and\nseven major contractors to develop and demonstrate the technology to\nsuppress vibration in large space structures.\nThe control goal was to\nmeet the stringent line-of-sight and jitter control requirements of\nrepresentative\nspacecraft.\nVarious experiments with beams, plates,\ntrusses, and frame structures were conducted using damping augmentation\nand elastic mode control. Disturbance models were developed, sensors and\nactuators were surveyed, and system identification studies were performed\n(Figure 2).\nThe ACOSS studies were chiefly analytical, with limited\nlaboratory testing.\nThe leading techniques resulting from this program\nwere High Authority Control/Low Authority Control (HAC/LAC), Positivity,\nand Filter Accomodated Model Error Sensitivity Suppression\n(FAMESS).\n\nENVIRONMENTAL\n\n_VIBF,\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nATION\n\nANALYTICAL\nCONTROL\n\nDEVELOP\n\nACTIVE\n\nCONCEPTS\n\nCONTROL\n\nI\n\nSENSORS\n\n-LOW\nFREQUENCIES\n-SPACECRAFT\nMODELS\n\nACTUATORS\n\n-CONTROL\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SLEWING\n\nAND\n\nALGORITHM\nENVIRONMENTAL\n\nDISTURBANCES\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 QUANTIFYING\nPERFORMANCE\n\nPOINTING\n\nt\nV VvV\' ,\'vv\nSLEWING\n\nINTERNAL\nVIBRATION\n\nFigure 2\n\n69\n\nVIBRATION CONTROL OF SPACE STRUCTURE (VCOSS) FARDWARE\nThe VCOSS I program extended the ACOSS program by applying modern control\ntechniques and s t a t e - o f - t h e - a r t hardware concepts t o the Draper Model two\ns t r u c t u r e developed under ACOSS. QlJALW\nACES STRUCTURE\n\nFor the ACES program, the VCOSS structure i s configured w i t h an\no f f s e t antenna and counterbalance arm weights (Figure 6). The 13-met r\ntruss structure i s cantilevered vertically from a hydraulic shaker table\nand gimbal motors. Truss members are made from fiberglass and a r e\nconnected such t h a t a helical triangular cross section truss i s created;\nthe truss supports only tension loads. There are two bays, located a t the\nm i d p o i n t and f r e e end of the structure, f o r placement of control\nactuators, A l a s e r source i s mounted a t the top o f the structure and\naimed a t a mirror on the o f f s e t antenna; the reflected beam will be used\nas feedback by the controller t o reduce the j i t t e r t o l e s s t h a n a\nspecified line-of-sight e r r o r between the antenna and the end of the feed\nmast.\n\nFigure 6\n\n73\n\nPROGRAM APPROACH\nThe first\nphase in the ACES program\nis to review\nand to assess\nthe HAC/LAC\nand FAMESS control\ntechniques\nfor BLANK\n\n7@ --]%\n\nNOT\n\nFILMED\n\n77\n\nHIGH-AUTHORITY/LOW-AUTHORITY TECHNIQUE\nCONTROL\nThe HAC/LAC\ncontrol technique, developed by Lockheed, addresses the\nparticular problem of instabilities\nin the system created by spillover\n(Figure 11). Spillover is the interaction of the controller with unmodeled\nmodes. The control design must not destabilize, poorly known high\nfrequency modeswhile controlling the low frequency modes. HAC/LAC\napproaches this problem on two levels.\nThe HACdesign incorporates high\ndampingover a low bandwidth to meet performance goals; this is a\nfrequency-shaped extension of LQGmethods. The second level, the LAC\ndesign, incorporates low damping over a high bandwidth to eliminate\nspillover-induced instabilities\n(Ref. 4).\n\nHACEFFECTS\nHAC\nIMPROVED BANDWIDTH\nDAMPING\nRATIOR\nO\nPERFORMANCE\n\nHIGHAUTHORITY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LARGE\nDAMPING\nRATIO\nCHANGES\n\nF ,AC\n\' ZEG\'ON\n\nj\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EIGENVECTOR\nCHANGES\n\nUNSTABLE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LQGSYNTttESIS\nWITHFREOUENCY\nSHAPING\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ENHANCED\nCONVENTIONAL\nLQGROBUSTNESS\nLACEFFECTS\n\nAUGMENTED\nI\nDAMPING\n\nLAC\nBANDWIDTH\n_\n_\n,I\n\nLOWAUTHORITY\n\nLAC_FREOUENCY --\'-\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 BROADBAND\nDAMPING\nAUGMENTATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ROBUST\nAGAINST\nMODELING\nERROR\n\nIMPROVED\nDAMPING =\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SIMPLIFIED\nSYNTHESIS\n(LEAST\nSOUARES,\nJACOBI\'S\nPERTURBATION)\n\nINTEGRATED\nHAC/LAC\n\nPERFORMANCE\nRATIOR [_\nO\nUNSTABLE--\'\n\nREGION\n\nFigure\n\n,\n.\n\n78\n\n..\n\n.\n,\n\n:\n\n11\n\nCONTROL CRITERIA\nThe goal of this test is to have the controller minimize line-of-sight\nerror between the antenna and the feed mast (Figure 14).\nIn minimizing\nthis error, several criteria will be considered in evaluating each\ntechnique\'s performance. These will include total reduction of\nline-of-sight\nerror, computational efficiency (meeting the speed and\nmemory requirements of the available digital system), arm robustness\n(controller performance for off-optimum structural parameters).\nThese\ncriteria will be applied to both disturbance spectra.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MINIMIZE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DETERMINE\n\n_NTENNA/FEED\nPERFORMANCE\n\nMAST\nOF\n\nLINE-OF-SIGHT\nCONTROL\n\nPOINTING\n\nERROR\n\nTECHNIQUES"\n\n-ROBUSTNESS\n-COMPUTATIONAL\n\nEFFICIENCY\n\nFigure 14\n\nPRECEDING\n\nPAGE BLANK\n\nNOT\n\nFilMED\n\n81\n\nPROGRAM RESULTS\nThe results\nof this\nevaluatior,\nwill\ngive a better\nunderstanding\nof the\ntrade-offs\ninvolved\nwith\neach control\ntechnique\n(Figure\n15).\nWith the\nnumber dnd location\nof sensors\nand actuators\nfixed\nand the control\ngoal\nspecified,\na good evaluation\nof the techniques\ncan be performed.\nBy eliminating\nparticular\nsensors\nor actuators\nand observing\nhow\nwell\nthe control\ntechniques\nrespond\nto this\nchange in the system,\nrobustness\nwill\nbe determined.\nThe control\ndesign\nmust meet the\nrequirements\nof the digital\nsystem in terms of computational\nspeed and\nmemory capacity.\nThe effects\nof sampling\nand computational\nlags should\nalso be accounted\nfor by the control\ntechnique.\nThe degree\nof\nline-of-sight\nerror\ncorrection\nwill\ndetermine\nthe limits\nof control\ntechnique\ncapability\nwith\nthe available\nhardware.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nBETTER\n\nUNDERSTANDING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 payoff from this program will be twofold.\nCapabilities of these\ncontrol techniques will be better understood, and these techniques may\nthen be applied to specific control problems in other programs (Figure\n16).\nThe Air Force Weapons Laboratory plans to use the results of this\nprogram in determining a control technique for their Joint\nOptics/Structures\nExperiment (JOSE) program.\nAFWAL plans to incorporate\none of the ACES techniques into the Large Space Structure Active Vibration\nControl program and into their in-house Large Space Structure Technology\nProgram. The results from the ACES evaluation will also increase the\nconfidence in each technique.\n\nFY\n\n86\n\nI\n\nI\n\n87\n\nI\n\n88\n\n89\n\nI\n\n90\n\nJOSE\n(AFWL)\n\nACES\n\nL\nLSS\n\nI\n\nj\n\nACTIVE(AFWAL)\nVIBRATION\n\nCONTROL\n\nLSSTP\n(AFWAL)\n\nFigure 16\n\n83\n\nREFERENCES\nI. structures\n(LSS) will\nhave as\nperformance\nobjectives\nstringent\npointing\naccuracies,\nshort\nsettling\ntimes,\nrelatively\nfast response\nrequirements,\nor combinations\nthereof.\nMany of the\nperformance\nobjectives\nwill\nbe a challenge\nto the controls\nengineer.\nAlthough\nit\nis possible\nin principle\nto achieve\nstructural\nvibration\ncontrol\nthrough\npurely\nactive\nmeans, experience\nwith\ncomplex\nstructures\nhas shown that\nthe\nrealities\nof plant\nmodel inaccuracies\nand sensor/actuator\ndynamics\nfrequently\ncombine\nto produce substandard\nperformance.\nA more desirable\napproach\nis to apply\nactive\ncontrol\nburden.\nDevelopment\nof the\nobjective\nof the PACOSS (Passive\nand Active\n(Figure\nI).\n\n0\n\nFUTURE\n\nLARGE\n\nCONTROL\n\nTO benefits\nof the passive/active\ncontrol\nstrategy\non the system\nlevel\nare\nobvious\n(Figure\n13).\nWith reduced\nsensor/actuator\nrequirements,\nthe system becomes\nsimpler,\nless expensive,\nand more reliable.\nReduction\nin control\nsystem complexity\nresults\nin weight\nsavings,\nboth for the control\ncomponents\nas well\nas reduced\nstructural\nstrength\nbecause\nof the lower control\ntorque\nlevels.\nThese savings,\ntogether\nwith\nincreased\nrobustness\nand reliability\nwill\ncompensate\nfor,\nif not\ntotally\noffset,\nthe weight\nof the viscoelastic\ndamping treatments.\nIt is essential,\naugmentation.\nIt is\n\n0\n\nthat\nto\n\nthe structure\nbe designed\nfor\npassive\npost-treat\nan existing\nstructure.\n\nDAMPING\n\nNUMBER\n\nOF\n\nREDUCING\n\nTHIS\n\n+ ACTIVE\n\nREDUCING\n\n0\n\nPASSIVE\n\nhowever,\ninefficient\n\nENERGY\n\nAND\n\nCAN DTAdesign was chosen to be dynamically similar to the RSA and will serve\nas a hardware validation of design and analysis techniques for passive damping\ntreatments. The requirements shown in Figure 14 have been selected to assure\ntraceability\nto the RSAwithin the realities of the test environment and budgetary\nconstraints.\nIn addition, a program requirement is that the DTAbe spacequalifiable.\nAmongother implications, delivery in a single Shuttle flight was\nassumed.\n\n0\n\nVALIDATION\n\nOF\n\nDAMPING\n\n0\n\nDYNAMICALLY\n\nSIMILAR\n\n0\n\nDELIVERABLE\n\nTO ORBIT\n\n0\n\nNEGLIGIBLE\n\n0\n\nSUITABLE\n\n0\n\nEASILY\n\nTREATMENT\n\nAND\n\nAND\n\nANALYSIS\n\nTECHNIQUES\n\nTO RSA\n\nAS SINGLE\n\nUNPREDICTED\n\nFOR\n\nDESIGN\n\nSHUTTLE\n\nPAYLOAD\n\nDAMPING\n\nI-G TEST\n\nINEXPENSIVELY\n\nFABRICATED\n\nFigure\n\n14\n\n99\n\nDTADESIGN\nAPPROACH\nThe DTAwas designed to be essentially a scaled-down version of the RSA.\nBecause of the l-g test environment, it was necessary to shift the fundamental\nfrequency upwardby approximately 2 Hz. This will provide for a structure that can\nwithstand the l-g test environment, but will not compromisethe control interaction\naspects of the dynamics because the control bandwidth will be shifted accordingly.\nLow-cost materials are being used to the maximum\nextent possible, but careful\nfabrication techniques are being utilized to assure that the structure is well\nknown. In addition, the design is such that inadvertent sources of damping are\navoided (Figure 15).\n\n0\n\nACHIEVE\n\nSIMILAR\n\nDYNAMIC\n\n0\n\nGEOMETRIC\n\nSCALE\n\n0\n\nFREQUENCY\n\nSHIFT OF 2 Hz FOR The spring constants for all three\nsprings are chosen based on the weight to be supported. In the equilibrium\nposition, the mechanism is adjusted so that the side spring rods are in the\nhorizontal position. The side springs are preloaded in compression in this\nposition.\nWith proper spring sizing and adjustment, the additional force in the vertical\nspring due to a downward motion of the cable is offset by the component of the\ncompressive loads in the initially horizontal springs in the vertical direction due\nto a downward rotation of the horizontal springs.\nFor this particular test, each ZSRM supported about 200 l b . The effective\nspring rate of the mechanism was approximately 0.4 lb/in. at a l/Z-inch deflection\nin the vertical direction.\n\nFigure 21\n106\n\nDTA RING TRUSS MODALSURVEYRESULTS\n\nThe results\nof the ring truss modal survey are shown in Figure 22.\nThe first\ntwo modal frequencies\nagreed very closely\nwith predicted\nvalues.\nThese modes are\ncharacterized\nby large motions at suspension points,\nand the agreement indicates\nthat the ZSRMs were contributing\nnegligible\nstiffness.\nThe first\nmode, a symmetric\nmode, had large motion of the centerline\nsuspension point and very little\nat the\nother two supports.\nThe second mode, an antisymmetric\nmode, had virtually\nno\nmotion on the centerline\nsuspension point and large out-of-phase\nmotions at the\nremaining\ntwo suspension points.\nThe higher damping ratios\nof these modes relative\nsuspension point motion is indicative\nof slight\nfriction\n\nto modes involving\nin the ZSRMs.\n\nsmall\n\nNotice the very low damping in the remaining modes.\nFor those modes that\ncould be tuned separately,\nthe decay traces provide more accurate\ndamping values\nthan the curve fits\nfrom the single-point\nrandom tests.\nFor those modes where a\nrange of values or no value for the tuned decay tests\nis given,\nsatisfactory\ntuning\nwas not achieved.\nThe uniformly\nlow damping in the higher\nassumed that higher modes have more inherent\nin precision\nstructures.\n\nFREQUENCY\n\nmodes deserves\ndamping.\nThis\n\nMODAL (VISCOUS)\n\n(Hz)\n\nemphasis.\nIt\nis not always\n\nis often\nthe STRUCTURE\nADVANCED\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nJ.E.\nGeneral\n\nDyer and M.P. Dudeck\n\nDynamics Space Systems Division\nSan Diego, California\n\nFirst NASA/DoD\nCSI Technology Conference\nNovember\n18-21, 1986\n\n_F.C.X_|N_\n\nP_l_\n\nBLANK NOT FILMED\n111\n\nFUTURE SPACE ANTENNA\nREQUIREMENTS\nDRIVE DEPLOYABLE\nSTRUCTURE\nTECHNOLOGY\nDeployable space structures can be divided into three classes: linear support members, platforms,\nand antennas.\nAlthough\neach class has unique technology\nissues, future large, space antenna systems, with severe stiffness, stability, weight, and\npackaging requirements,\nare the major drivers of deployable structures technology\n(Figure 1).\nCommercial\nand military communication,\nradiometric,\nand surveillance\nsatellites increasingly\nwith RF apertures\nstraints, reflectors\n\n5 meters or larger to satisfy future mission requirements.\nthat can be deployed in space are required for the 5-meter\n\nrequire\n\nreflector\n\nDue to launch vehicle payload\nor larger aperture antenna.\n\nantennas\n\nenvelope\n\ncon-\n\nTypically, deployable reflectors use a flexible RF reflective surface supported by a deployable back structure.\nDeviation\nof a reflector surface from that of an ideal paraboloid results in degradation of its radiation pattern. Several factors contribute\nto on-orbit surface error, including surface geometry approximations,\nfabrication\nand adjustment tolerances,\nthermal and\ndynamic distortion due to the orbital environment,\ngravity effects, and deployment\nrepeatability.\nCurrent large, deployable RF reflectors are typically designed to operate at low frequencies\nwhere large surface errors can\nbe tolerated without significant performance\nloss. However, the trend of future advanced space applications\nis toward the\nutilization of frequencies above 14 GHz where sidelobe degradation\nand gain loss due to surface error can severely penalize\nantenna performance.\nTo meet future requirements,\nthe large deployable reflector will be required to achieve on-orbit surface\nerror approaching\nthat of a solid-surface\nreflector (3-10 mils rms).\nIn addition,\nrequirements\nto maximize\ngain performance\nand radiation pattern purity are dictating the use of offset\nreflector geometry to eliminate blockage by the feed array and support structure. Collectively, these requirements\ndrive future\nantenna systems toward high stiffness and thermal stability. These characteristics\nare best provided by deployable composite\ntruss structures.\n\nANTENNA SYSTEM REQUIREMENTS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LARGE APERTURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH OPERATING FREQUENCY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH RF PERFORMANCE\n\nSTRUCTURE REQUIREMENTS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH STIFFNESS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 THERMAL STABILITY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH SURFACE ACCURACY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 OFFSET GEOMETRY\n\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nLARGE, PRECISION,\nDEPLOYABLE STRUCTURES\n\n667o-1\n\nFigure\n112\n\n1.\n\nDEPLOYABLE\n\nTRUSS\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nFOR FUTURE\n\nSPACE ANTENNAS\n\nThe deployable truss structure,\nwhen integrated with a precisely contoured mesh reflective surface, possesses the features\nneeded to meet future space antenna requirements.\nTruss characteristics\nshown in Figure 2 that provide enhanced performance capabilities\ninclude:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High Specific Stiffness -- High inherent truss stiffness permits structure and control system frequency\nseparation and\nminimizes active control needed to suppress structural vibrations excited by slewing torques and other dynamic disturbances. High stiffness also reduces deflections and reflector surface contour errors due to gravity effects during manufacture, adjustment,\nand ground test.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Low Thermal Deformations\n-- Low thermal\nan array manufactured\nfrom low-expansion,\n\ndeformations\nresult from the use of simple axial truss structural\nadvanced composite materials.\n\nmembers\n\nin\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Good Packaging Efficiency -- Folding tetrahedral building blocks provide an efficient structural package. This repeating\nmodular truss design provides versatility by permitting simple scaling to accommodate\nvarying geometric requirements.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Edge-Mount\nCapability\n-- For offset reflector applications,\nthe tetrahedral truss reflector and feed structure\nare both\ndeployed\n\nfrom one, simple edge mount thereby\n\n]\n\nsimplifying\n\ndeployment,\n\nreducing\n\nweight,\n\nand improving\n\nsystem stiffness.\n\nI DEEP 3-D GRAPHITE/EPOXY\nTRUSS SUPPORT STRUCTURE\n\nFEATURES\n\nI R,.N\nPACKA_,.O I\nEFF,C,ENCY\n\nI .,_x TRUCTURAL\nS\nEFF,C,ENCY\nI\n\n1\n\nI LOW WE,"NT\nREFLECTOR\nI\n\'I\'\nI LOW MOMENT\nREFLECTOR\nI\ni\n\nOF INERTIA\n\nIGH NATURAL FREQUENCY\n\nJ\n\nI Low\nTRERMAL I\nCOEFF,C,ENTS\n\nI LOW\nDEFLECT,ONS\nI\n\nIEOOE\nFEEO I\nSUPPORT\n\nJ\n\nI No LOCNA_E SURFACE\nD\nI I LOW CONTOURI\nD,STORT,ON\nI REOUCEDSCATTERA,N\nI .IC.\nO I\n\n;j .....\n\nCONT. Dynamics Space Systems to demonstrate\nprecision deployable, tetrahedral truss reflector technology.\nDesign requirements\nrepresentative\nof a space-borne\ncommunications\nantenna\noperating over a frequency range up to 30 GHz included\na 5-meter projected aperture with an F/D ratio of 1.3, an offset of\n2.13 meters and manufacturing\nrss surface tolerance of 7.7 mils, and total on-orbit rss surface tolerance of 11 mils.\nOn-orbit errors are created by thermal, dynamic hygroscopic,\nand visco-elastic\neffects.\nby setting an overall thermal expansion coefficient goal (near zero), and then designing the\nmeet this overall goal.\nThe deployable truss was fabricated from graphite/epoxy\ncomposites for high stiffness\nreflective surface was fabricated from gold-plated\nmolybdenum\nwire knit into a tight mesh\n\nThermal distortion is minimized\nindividual structural elements to\nand low thermal expansion.\nThe\nfor low transmission\nlosses.\n\nUPPER SURFACE\n\nLOWER SURFACE\n\nSTRUTS configuration\nwith geometry and members selected to\nmeet stiffness and surface precision requirements.\nA computerized\ntetrahedral truss analysis program (Figure 4) was used to\ngenerate the complete three-dimensional\ntruss geometry and define the required truss element properties.\nAll structural elements,\nincluding tubular truss members and node fittings, used graphite/epoxy\nlaminates designed to\nhave ah overall coefficient of thermal expansion (CTE) near zero. Zero tolerance titanium carpenter tape hinges were used to\nfold the truss members for packaging and to provide stored spring energy for deployment.\nThe large, positive CTE of the\ntitanium hinges was considered\nin the strut design\noverall CTE of the member was near zero.\n\nTRUSS\n\nPARAMETER:\n0.00 EF\n\n4 BAY 30.00 DEG AN\n283 L-RHO ROUND\n\n5M\n.400 FID\n\nUNIT WEIGHT\nCOMPONENTS\n\nKILOGRAMS\n\nSTRUTS\n\nCTE graphite/epoxy\n\nCG\n\n+ Z\n\ntubes,\n\nthe\n\nMESH\n\nWEIGHT\n\nREQUIRED\n\nKILOGRAMS\n\n(0.305)\n(0.369)\n\n0.167\n\nwith the negative\n\n(16.0 FT) RF DIAMETER\n20.00/0 CONTINGENCY\n\nNUMBER\n\n(POUNDS)\n\n0.138\n\nUPPER SURFACE\n\nso that when combined\n\n(POUNDS)\n\n102\n42\n\n14.1\n7,0\n\n(31.1)\n(15.5)\n\nTUBES\nHINGE\n\n0.099\n0.015\n\n(0.218)\n\n42\n\n4.1\n\n42\n\n0.6\n\n(1.4)\n\n0.027\n\n(0.060)\n(0.167)\n\n84\n36\n\n2.3\n2.7\n\n(5.0)\n(6.0)\n\n0.073\n0.001\n\n(0.161)\n\n36\n\n2.6\n\n(5,0)\n\n(0.003)\n\n72\n\n0.1\n\n(0.2)\n\n0.182\n\n(0.402)\n\n24\n\n4.4\n\n(9.6)\n\nTUBES\n\n0.112\n\nHINGE\nEND FITTINGS + PINS\n\n0.015\n0.028\n\n(0.247)\n(0.032)\n\n24\n24\n\n2.7\n0.4\n\n(5.9)\n(0.6)\n\nI\n\n19.1)\n\n(0.032)\n\nEND FITTING + PINS\n\n+X-\n\nDIAGONALS\n\n0.076\n\nTUBES\nEND FITTING + PINS\nLOWER SURFACE\n\nNUMBER\nOF BAYS\nSTRUT ANGLE\n\n(0.061)\n\n48\n\n1.3\n\n(2.9)\n\nSPIDER ASSEMBLY\n\n0,125\n\n(0.275)\n\n31\n\n3.9\n\n(8.5)\n\nMESH INSTALLATION\n\n2,241\n\n(4.941)\n\n1\n\n2.2\n4.0\n\n(4.9)\n(8.9)\n\nCONTINGENCY\nREFLECTOR WEIGHT\n\n24.3\n\n3_. x_\'_ _v_._\n\n(53.5)\n\nX-C.G. Consideration\nof illumination taper permits facet geometry to be varied so that the facet sizes progressively\nincrease from the center to the periphery.\nThis non-periodic\ngeometry reduces grating lobes that may result from an equally subdivided\nsurface. To meet the high\nprecision required, the 5-meter diameter surface was divided into 1,944 facets varying in size from three to six inches on a\nside.\n\nMESH\n\nGRID\n\nMESH SYSTEM\nWEIGHT SUMMARY\nITEM\n\nNO.REQ\n\nMESH TRIANGLES\nGRID LINE SEGMENTS\nDROP LINES\nBUTTONS\nCLIPS\n\n1944\n2970\n1027\n1027\n1027\n\nMESH SYSTEMTOTAL\nMESH AREA(SQYDS)\nTOTALLINE LENGTH\n(FT)\n\nDROPLINES\n\nLINES\n\nWEIGHT\n2.975\n1.194\n1.369\n1.046\n0.136\n6.719\n25.1\n3749.0\n\nNUMBER OF POINTS\nNORMALRMS\nHALF PATHL RMS\nX DEFLECTION\nY DEFLECTION\nZ DEFLECTION\nDELTAFOCALLENGTH\nROTATION\nABOUT X-X\nROTATION\nABOUT Y-Y\n\n65124\n0.00451\n0.00453\n- 0,16197\n- 0.07396\n0.04038\n0.33303\n-0.00063\n0.00031\n\n3\n0.06000\n1.50000\n6.719\n- 0.022\n183.383\n36.337\n3.763\n3.768\n4.006\n0.000\n0.000\n1.183\n\nGROSSWEIGHT LB\nX-ARM\nY-ARM\nZ-ARM\nIxx-SLUG FT2\nIyy-SLUG FT2\nIzz-SLUG FT2\nIxz-SLUGFT2\nIxY-SLUGFT2\nIzy-SLUG FT2\n\nFigure\n116\n\n16.404\n4\n1.300000\n9\n0.000000\n0.426000\n0.0000\n10.00000\n\nMESH DIA (FT)\nNO. OF DIV.IBAY\nX OFF SET- \xc2\xb0/oDIA.\nY OFFSET- \xc2\xb0/o DIA.\nHOLERADIUS (IN.)\nDB TAPER\nWEIGHTING\nO-NONE,I-AR, 2-ILL, 3-BOTH\nMESH LOADLBIIN.\nGRID LINELOADLB\n\n5.\n\n6670-5\n\nTRUSS STRUCTURE FABRICATION\nA simple but highly precise modular approach was used to fabricate the truss structure. Assembly was accomplished with the\naid of 19 tooling stanchions (Figure 6) that precisely located the truss node fittings. Individual truss members and fittings were\nassembled on this tool so that any tolerances were removed by simple, on-assembly length adjustments.\n\nBWBGMAL PAGE E3\nOF PQQR QUALITY\n\nFigure 6.\n117\n\nMESH SURFACE FABRICATION\nThe mesh surface was fabricated using 20-gauge, two-bar tricot knit fabric. The pattern results in a 0.045-inch maximum\ndimension hole size, which extends RF performance into K-band and reduces cross-polarization response significantly. The\nfabric is knit from 1.2-mil, gold-plated molybdenum monofilament wire.\nThe mesh assembly is formed to the parabolic contour of a precision plaster mold (Figure 7). The initial contour of the\nmesh is maintained by a system of grid lines interconnected to buttons located at each apex of the triangular facets subdividing\nthe mesh. The buttons, which are attached and bonded to the mesh, also provide the attach points for the 1,027 mesh control\nlines. These lines run from the truss node fittings to the mesh and are used to adjust the mesh surface.\n\nORffitNAL PAGE E\n3\nOF POOR QUALlW\n\nFigure 7.\n118\n\nCONTOUR MEASUREMENT AND ADJUSTMENT\n\nORIGINAL PAGE tS\nOF POOR Q U A W\n\nA computer-controlled profilometer contour measurement system incorporating a non-contact sensing probe (Figure 8) was\nused for measurement and adjustment of the reflective, mesh surface. The probe uses a digitally driven, capacitive transducer\naccurate to f l/2 mil for proximity detection of the mesh surface. The 1,027 control lines between the mesh surface and the\ntruss structure were loosened or tightened to adjust the mesh surface. Four iterations were required to complete adjustment of\nthe mesh surface to the required accuracy. After the surface was set, the mesh control lines were locked out and the adjustment\ndevices removed.\nPhotogrammetric measurements of 315 random targets on the surface were used to independently verify contour accuracy. After four adjustment cycles, the measured normal surface error was 8.3 mils rms using the profilometer and 8.6 mils\nusing photogrammetric results.\n\n6670-7\n\nFigure 8.\n119\n\nPACKAGED REFLECTOR\nWhen folded, the truss reflector forms a compact package whose dimensions are overall height - 1.8 meter, truss height - 1.1\nmeters, mesh diameter - 1.4 meters, and truss diameter - 0.9 meter. Packaged diameter is set by the number and size of node\nfittings used, and height is set by the length of the truss diagonal struts. The packaged reflector shown in Figure 9 shows one\nof three alternate packaging approaches, i.e., with all surface struts folded in. The other two approaches are with top struts\nfolded out and with both top and bottom struts folded outward, which produces a longer, smaller-diameter package.\n\nFigure 9.\n120\n\nORIGINAL PAGE E\n3\nOF POOR QUALITY\n\n0RIGSr:AL $AGE 6\nOF POOR QUALITY\n\nREFLECTOR DEPLOYMENT\n\nThree deployments of the reflector were performed as shown in Figure 10. The truss reflector is normally a freely deploying\nsystem employing stored energy in folded spring hinges (carpenter tape hinges). These hinges are incorporated in all truss\nstrut members of the geotruss structure supporting the reflector surface. When the package restraints are released, each strut\nbegins to unfold. This action automatically drives the node fittings apart until the surface struts lock in their deployed position.\nThe hinge provides a nearly constant deployment force until just before lock-up. At that point, the hinge force increases\nsharply to lock the truss member into its final position. This final lock-up force also ensures that the antenna surface mesh is\nstretched into its desired configuration.\n\nCV1851392\n\nCV1851402\n\nCV1851395\n\nCV1851398\n\nCV1851411\n\n6670-11\n\nFigure 10.\nI\n\n121\n\nELECTROMAGNETIC (RF) TESTING\nRF performance tests of the 5-meter tetrahedral truss reflector were performed at the Martin Marrietta Near Field Measurement Laboratory in Denver, Colorado. Linearly polarized on-focus feeds supplied by NASA LaRC were used to measure\ngain, far field patterns and cross-polarization at 2.27, 4.26, 7.73, and 11.6 GHz. Installation of the reflector on a 20-foot\ntower in the near field facility is shown in Figure 11. Results of these tests are being evaluated at NASA LaRC and General\nDynamics Space Systems. Preliminary results indicate good RF performance that correlated well with analytical predictions.\n"Qpical results at 4.26 GHz are shown in the plot in Figure 11.\n\n0\n\n-5\n\n-\n\n4.26 GHz\n\nE-PLANE\n\nFigure 11.\n122\n\nORIGINAL PAGE B\nOF POOR QUALITY\n\nDEPLOYABLE\n\nTRUSS\n\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nVERIFICATION\n\nPROGRAM\n\nThe 5-meter technology antenna program demonstrated\nthe capability to successfully fabricate and test a precision deployable\ntruss antenna. Deployable\ntruss structure technology\nissues associated with on-orbit performance\nrequirements\nneed to be\nverified next by an integrated program of analysis and ground and flight testing. These technologies\ncan be divided into five\ngeneral categories:\nstructural dynamics,\nactive control, thermodynamics,\nelectromagnetics,\nand structure/mechanisms\ndesign.\nA 5-meter deployable truss reflector integrated with a deployable truss beam (Figure 12) can serve as a low-cost testbed\nfor initial technology verification\ntesting. Subsequent testing of a larger 15- to 20-meter article could be used to verify scalingsensitive technology\nissues.\n\n./\n5-METER GROUND TEST\n\n5-METER\n\n/_\n\n15-T020-METER\n\nFLIGHT TEST\n\nGROUND TEST\n\nFLIGHT TEST\n5670-12\n\nFigure\n\n12.\n123\n\nCONCLUSIONS\nThe 5-meter technology antenna program demonstrated the overall feasibility of integrating a mesh reflector surface with a\ndeployable truss structure to achieve a precision surface contour compatible with future, high-performance\nantenna requirements. Specifically, the program demonstrated:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The feasibility of fabricating\na precision,\nedge-mounted,\ndeployable, tetrahedral t/\'uss structure.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The feasibility of adjusting a truss-supported\nmesh reflector contour to a surface error less than 10 mils rms.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Good RF test performance,\nwhich correlated well with analytical predictions.\nFurther analysis and testing (including flight testing) programs are needed to fully verify all the technology issues, including\nstructural dynamics, thermodynamics,\ncontrol, and on-orbit RF performance,\nwhich are associated\nwith large, deployable,\ntruss antenna structures.\n\n124\n\nN87\n\nDEVELOPMENT\n\nOF\n\nTHE\n\nLENS\n\nANTENNA\n\nSHUTTLE-ATTACHED\n\nH.\n\nHill,\n\nManager\n\nNASA George\nHuntsville,\n\nD.\n\nJohnston,\n\nNASA George\nHuntsville,\n\nDEPLOYMENT\nFLIGHT\n\nProject\n\nC. Marshall\nAlabama\n\nNASA/DOD\nCSI\nNovember\n\nSpace\n\nOffice/JA53\nFlight\n\nCenter\n\nManager/JA53\n\nH. Frauenberger,\nProgram\nSpace Systems\nDivision;\nBethpage,\nNew York\n\nFirst\n\n(LADD)\n\nEXPERIMENT\n\n- Experiments\n\nC. Marshall\nAlabama\n\nDEMONSTRATION\n\n- 16 022\n\nSpace\n\nFlight\n\nManager\nGrumman\n\nCenter\n\nCorporation\n\nTechnology\nConference\n18-21, 1986\n\n125\n\n-\n\nOPERATIONAL SPACE-FED SPACE BASED RADAR SATELLITE ON-ORBIT\nStudies conducted over the past decade have shown that Space-Based Radar\n(SBR) will be a critical element of the U.S. surveillance system by the\nlate 1990\'s.\nThese studies have narrowed the choice to SBR systems\nemploying space-fed or corporate-fed phased arrays. It is apparent that\nas stealth technology improves and defense against missile systems\nbecomes viable, the SBR configuration must have growth potential and the\ncapability of being deployed with large apertures to provide\nsurveillance of dim targets. In order to enhance its survivability, it\nmust be flown at high altitudes. It is equally apparent that in order\nto satisfy these requirements, a lightweight deployable aperture is\nrequired.\nThe Rome Air Development Center (RADC) and the Strategic Defense\nInitiative Organization (SDIO) have teamed together to provide technology for a deployable lens concept. These studies have addressed the\nvarious radar frequencies required for both atmospheric (L-Band; 1.0 2.0 GHz) and space (X-Band; 8.2 - 1 2 . 4 GHz) surveillance. As a result\nof these studies lightweight membrane apertures of the various radar\nfrequencies are being developed along with an associated roll-out\ndeployment technique.\nFigure 1 shows a typical large aperture\noperational space fed satellite. Utilizing the experience gained from\nthe Solar Array Flight Experiment (SAFE), NASA\'s Marshall Space Flight\nCenter (MSFC) will manage the development of a space flight\ndemonstration of several of the applicable technologies.\n\nFIGURE EXPERIMENT DEPLOYED IN THE SHUTTLE CARGO BAY\n\nAn essential and significant early step towards making these large\nspace-fed SBR satellites a reality is the Lens Antenna Deployment\nDemonstration (LADD) project that is being sponsored by the Strategic\nDefense Initiative Organization (SDIO) and NASA, managed by RADC and\nMSFC and developed under contract by the Space Systems Division of\nGrumman Corporation. The LADD is a scaled versioh of the operational\nsatellite which will be mounted on a standard Material Science\nLaboratory (MSL) pallet, carried into orbit aboard the shuttle and\ndeployed in the orbiter bay (see Figure 3 ) .\nIt is to be heavily\ninstrumented to provide appropriate thermal and dynamics data on\ncritical components.\nLADD is a single axis roll-out deployable/\nretractable test article with a membrane which is representative of an\nX-band antenna. Its structural and mechanical concepts are, however,\nequally applicable to membranes tailored to other radar frequencies.\nThe primary elements of LADD are a drum, two deployable side-masts/\ncanisters, an end beam and an 8 ft. x 20 ft. membrane populated by\napproximately 5000 dummy radar modules. The membrane dimensions were\nestablished by the width of the shuttle cargo bay and the lateral\nmounting of the LADD experiment.\n\nFIGURE 3\n128\n\nLADDSTOWED\nCONFIGURATION\n(VIEWLOOKING\nFORWARD)\nIn the stowed (lift\noff and landing) configuration\nthe membrane is\nrolled up on the drum and the side-masts are retracted into their\ncanisters as shown in Figure 4. The experiment has been designed to fit\nwithin a 6 inch shuttle bay dynamic envelope.\n\nEND\nBEAM\n\n\\\n\nCAN\nISTER\n\n\'\n\n414,00\n\n#\n\ni 400.00\no\n\n/\nENT TAPE\nRECORDER
