primary\nobjective\nof the LADD\nProgram\nis to develop\na technology\ndemonstration\ntest article\nthat can be used\nfor both\nground\nand flight\ntests\nto\ndemonstrate\nthe\nstructural\nand\nmechanical\nfeasibility\nand\nreliability\nof the single-axis\nroll-out\nSBR approach.\nAs designed,\nthe\nLADD\nwill\nessentially\nbe\na\ngeneric\nstructural\nexperiment\nwhich\nincorporates\nall\ncritical\ntechnology\nelements\nof\nthe\noperational\nsatellite\nand\nis applicable\nto a number\nof\nfuture\nantenna\nsystems.\nHowever,\nto fully\ndetermine\nits design\nintegrity\nfor meeting\nthe lens\nflatness\nand constant\ngeometry\nrequirements\nin a zero\n"g" environment\nunder\nextreme\nthermal\nconditions,\nthe LADD must be space\nflight\ntested.\nBy\naccurately\nsurveying\nthe\nstructure\nunder\nvarying\nconditions\nthe\nmembrane\ntolerance-holding\ncapabilities\nof\nthe\nstructure\nwill\nbe\ndemonstrated.\nThe flight\ntest will\nprovide\ndata\nto verify\nanalytical\ntools\nused\nto predict\nthermal\nand structural\nbehavior.\nMost\nimportant,\nthe experiment\nwill provide\nan initial\nindication\nof structural\ndamping\nin a zero\n"g" vacuum\nenvironment.\nThe operational\nsatellites\nwith apertures\nin the\n20 m by 60 m size DETERMINE STRUCTURAL AND MEMBRANE DAMPING ON-ORBIT\n\nFIGURE\n\n132\n\n6\n\nENGINEERING\n\nAPPROACH\n\nThe\nLADD\nis being\ndeveloped\nessentially\nas a protoflight\nsystem.\nA\nsingle\narticle\nis being\ndeveloped\nfor both\nfunctional\nground\ntest\nand\ncheckout\nand\nthe\nflight\nexperiment.\nAll\nsignificant\nstructural\nand\nmechanical\nfeatures\nof\nthe\noperational\nsingle\naxis\nroll-out\nantenna\nconcept\nwill\nbe incorporated\nin the LADD design\nand ground\ntested.\nNo\nchanges\nin the design\nor hardware\nare envisioned\nbetween\nthe ground\nand\nflight\ntests\nexcept\nthose,\nif any, that might be dictated\nby the results\nof the ground\ntests.\nIn\nthe\nengineering\napproach\ntaken\na number\nof\ndesign\nstudies\nand\nanalyses\nare\nbeing\nconducted\nprior\nto the\ncompletion\nof\nthe\ndesign\nphase.\nThese\nstudies\nand analyses,\nwhich include\na shuttle\nintegration\ncompatibility\nassessment\nand\norbiter\ncoupled\nloads\nanalysis,\nare\nintended\nto\nensure\nthat\nthe\nconfiguration\nselected\nwill\nbe\nshuttle\ncompatible\nand\nthat\nit will\nhave\nthe desired\nnatural\nfrequency\nto\nprevent\ncross\ncoupling\nwith\norbiter\nfrequencies\nand meet\nthe lift-off,\non orbit\nand landing\nloads criteria\nwith adequate\nmargins\nof safety.\n\ne\n\nUTILIZE PROTOFLIGHT SYSTEM DEVELOPMENT CONCEPT\n(SINGLE TEST ARTICLE FOR GROUND AND FLIGHT TESTS)\n\ne\n\nINCORPORATE ALL SIGNIFICANT FEATURES OF OPERATIONAL SATELLITE\n\ne\n\nCONDUCT ALL ESSENTIAL STUDIES AND ANALYSES EARLY IN DESIGN PHASE TO:\n-\n\nENSURE SHUTTLE COMPATIBILITY\n\n-\n\nPROVIDE ACCURATE DESIGN LOADS\n\n-\n\nGUARANTEE SUBSTANTIAL MARGINS OF SAFETY ARE ACHIEVED\n\ne\n\nCONDUCT EXTENSIVE GROUND TESTS TO THOROUGHLY QUALIFY HARDWARE\n\ne\n\nMODIFY HARDWARE ONLY WHEN DEEMED NECESSARY BY GROUND TEST RESULTS\n\nFIGURE\n\n7\n\n1 end beam\nthat supports\nthe membrane\nis constructed\nof 1 in. square\nboxed\naluminum\nextrusions\nand is the common\nlink\nor bridge\nstructure\nbetween\ntwo coilable\n(deployable/retractable)\nfiberglass\nmasts.\nThese\nmasts\nare similar\nto the\nArray\nFlight\nExperiment\nSeptember\n1984.\n\none that was successfully\nflown\n(SAFE)\non\nthe\nspace\nshuttle\n\non the Solar\nDiscovery\nin\n\nTIP FITTING\n\nFIGURE\n\n9\n\n1 35\n\nDEPLOYABLE\n\nThe\nthe\n\ndeployment\nof\nside\nmasts.\n\nthe membrane\nEach\nmast\n\nSIDE\n\nMASTS\n\nis accomplished\nthrough\nthe\nconsists\nof\nthree\ncontinuous\n\nextension\nof\nfiberglass\n\nlongeron\nmembers\nconnected\nby a triangle\nof fiberglass\nbatten\nmembers\nat\nspecific,\nequally\nspaced\nlocations\nalong\nthe longerons\n(see Figure\ni0).\nThe region between\nthe battens\nis called\na "bay".\nThe bay length\nis a\nsignificant\nfactor\nin determining\nthe mast local buckling\nstrength.\nThe\nLADD masts have been selected\nwith sufficient\nstrength\nto allow vertical\ndeployment\nin a i "g" environment.\nThree\npairs\nof fiberglass\nrods\nstranded\nstainless\nsteel wire diagonals\nconnect\nthe intersections\nof\nbattens\nand longerons\nbetween\nadjacent\nbays.\nRollers\nare located\nat\noutside\nof all three longerons\nat each batten\nlocation.\nThe rollers\npart of apex fittings\nthat are permanently\nattached\nto the longerons\nsupport\nthe battens\nand diagonals.\nDetail\n"A" of Figure\ni0 shows\ngeometrical\nrelationship\nbetween\nthe longerons,\nbattens\nand diagonals\na typical\napex fitting.\n\nor\nthe\nthe\nare\nand\nthe\nat\n\nThe masts are retracted\nby coiling\nthe longerons\nin torsion\nand stowing\nthem\nin the bottom\nof the\ncanisters.\nDeployment\nis accomplished\nby\nallowing\nthe coiled\n(stowed)\nlongerons\nto uncoil\nand\nstraighten\nin a\ncontrolled\nmanner.\nBoth\ndeployment\nand\nretraction\nis accomplished\nby\nrotating\nan elevating\nnut which\nhas internal\nthreads\nthat simultaneously\nengage\nthe\nrollers\non\neach\nof\nthe\nthree\nlongeron\napex\nfittings\nassociated\nwith\na\nbatten\nattachment.\nDuring\ndeployment,\nas\nthe\nelevating\nnut rotates\nthe rollers\nare pulled\nup through\nthe threads\nand\nthe\ncoils\nof\nlongeron\nare\nallowed\nto\nstraighten.\nReversing\nthe\ndirection\nof elevating\nnut\nrotation\ndrives\nthe rollers\nback\ninto\nthe\ncanisters\nand\nforces\nthe\nlongerons\nto resume\ntheir\ncoiled/retracted\nconfiguration.\nIn the LADD experiment\nthe deployment\nand retraction\nof\nthe two masts will be synchronized\nto prevent\nasymmetry.\n\nBATTEN\n\nONE\n\nBAY\n"_\n\nBOC\n\nt\n\nROLLER\n\nLUG\n\nCONTINUOUS\nLONCERON\n.3ONAL\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A\n\nRO3"ATINC\n\nSECTION,._IV,\n\ni_\n\nDETAIL\n\n/I\n\n_i \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n--"\n\nINTERNAL\n\nTHREADS\n\nDEPLOYMENT\nTRANSITON\n\n1 j\n\nENGAGE\n\nI"\n\nSECTION\n\nI" ;\n\nCANISTER\n\nFIGURE\n1 36\n\nOF\n\nSECTION\nLONOERON\n\n_\'\n\nJL___L\nSECT=ON _\n_\'l\nSTOWED\n\nA\n\ni0\n\nROLLERS\n\nDRUM\nANDDRUM\nSUPPORT\nTRUNNIONS\nDuring the deployment and retraction of the masts the LADDmembraneis\nbeing unwound and rewound respectively\nfrom a 29-inch diameter,\ncontinuous 8 ft. long drum. The drum has an aluminum skin supported\ninternally\nby stringers and ring frames. There is a flange at the\ncenter of the drum that duplicates the end rims of a folding drum\nenvisioned in an operational system. The membranelaunch support system\nis built into the drum skins to support the first layer of modules and\nthe membrane during launch and landing.\nEach subsequent layer of\nmembraneis supported by the modules of the previous wrap and, when\nfully stowed, an additional torque is applied to the drum and the drum\nis locked.\nThe drum is supported by trunnion fittings\nat each end that are fitted\nwith rolling element and spherical bearings. The trunnions are tapered\naluminum beams that are, in turn, connected to the drum support beam\nwhich is similar in construction to the end beam. The drum support beam\nhas four "bathtub" fittings\nat each end that attach to the sides of the\nmast canisters.\nThe drum support beam also supports the center seam\nmechanism.\n\nDRUH SUPPORT\nBEAM\n\nSUPPORT\nBEAM\n\nFIGURE\n\nIi\n137\n\nMEMBRANE CENTER SEAM ENGINEERING DEVELOPMENT TEST FIXTURE\nThe center seam, although not required on the 8 ft. wide LADD membrane,\nis being incorporated and is a key element of the technology\ndemonstration. The center seam must provide structural integrity, an\neffective RF barrier, durability and appropriate life in terms of\nformation/separation cycles and yet fit within the limited space\navailable (approximately 1 / 4 in.) between modules in the densely\npopulated x-band membrane. A center seam configuration possessing all\nof these characteristics has been developed and tested. Figure 12 shows\nan engineering development test rig with an abbreviated width (5 module\nwide) membrane which forms two parallel center seams 20 ft. long.\nCenter seams of virtually unlimited length can be fabricated using the\nprocesses developed by Gruman.\n\nFIGURE 12\n138\n\nOREWAL PAGE I\nS\nOF POOR single-axis\nroll-out\nsatellite\nmembrane.\nDuring\nflight\ntesting\nmembrane\nmodal\nshapes\nwill\nbe recorded\nwith\nthe shuttle\nCCTV\ncameras.\nDynamic\ninputs\nwill\nbe provided\nby TESTS WILL PROVIDE ESSENTIAL MODAL DAMPING\nINFORMATION FOR FUTURE SBR SATELLITE DESIGNS\n\nFIGURE\n\n140\n\n14\n\nGSI CAMERA\nANDGASCAN\nCONFIGURATION\nThe LADD is designed to meet the tolerances imposed by radar system\nrequirements. The in-plane thermally-induced deflections can amount to\na change in length of up to .04% for the long lens aperture.\nIn an\noperational\nsatellite\nthis\nmotion will\nbe\ncompensated for\nelectronically.\nThe out-of-plane flatness requirement for an x-band 8\nft. by 20 ft. membranehas also been determined. The membranewill be\nmeasured in the as-built configuration to establish baseline dimensions.\nAfter repeated deployments and retractions,\nmeasurement data will be\ncollected for the membrane\nwhile on-orbit to determine if any permanent\ndistortions have occurred.\nThe measurementof the membrane\nflatness and geometric stability\nwill be\ndetermined with the use of three Geodetic Services Incorporated (GSI)\nphotogrammetric cameras. Since existing cameraswere not designed to be\noperated in a vacuum they will be contained in Get Away Special (GAS)\ncans (Figure 15) filled with presurized nitrogen.\n\nSTROBE\nPOWER\nSUPPLY\nRING\nSTROBE\nFUZED\nOUARTZ\nFOR\nSTROBE\nFUZED\nOUARTZ\np\n\n__F\n\nFIGURE\n\n15\n\n141\n\nGENERAL\n\nThe\n\nthree\n\ncameras\n\nattached\nflown\n\nin\n\nare\n\nSupport\n\nExperiment\npallet\n\nCONFIGURATION\n\nStructure\n\nthe\nto\n\ncarried\n\npayload\n\nbay\n\nMPESS,\n\nand\n\nthe\n\nRelease/Engage\n\nMechanism\n\n-\n\nLADD\n\nin\n\nthe\n\n(MPESS)\n(Figure\nthe\n\nthird\n(REM)\n\nMPESS\n\nCAMERA\n\nshuttle\n\nmounted\n16).\nwill\nwhich\n\nFIXED of solar concentrators were considered during\nthe early stages of Phase B: Definition and Preliminary Design Studies.\nThese options included single and multiple reflection systems and\nrefraction boom\nnatural frequencies.\nThe Space Station was modelled as a lumped\nparameter system to determine the control system stability.\nThe Parabolic Offset Linearly Actuated Reflector (POLAR)concept was\nchosen as the concept with which to go forward into preliminary design.\nThe interface and support structure for this concept are currently being\nstudied.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The\no\ncontrol law derivative gain operates on the relative velocity across the\njoint, i.e., "MI" = I.\nI. The pointing controls can be a beneficial (although unintended)\nsource of system damping. The increase in damping, above the small .005\nassumedstructural damping, is a result of the active control.\n2. The pointing controls may operate in bandwidths near system\nstructural frequencies.\nThe boomneed not be designed stiff\nto avoid\ncontrol instabilities.\n3. trade\nstudies\nis\nActuated\nReflector\n(POLAR)\nsolar dynamic\n\n17_lesolar dynamic\nsystem\nconsists\nof five major assemblies:\nconcentrator,\nreceiver,\npower\nconversion\nunit (PCU),\nradiator\nand interface\nstructure\nassembly.\nThe figure\nbelow reflects\nchanges\nin the current\ndesign\nfrom\nthe concept\nconsidered\nduring\ntrade studies.\nThis includes\nmost\nnoticeably\nthe utilization\nof a pumped\nloop radiator\nsystem\ninstead\nof a\nheat pipe radiator.\nThe module\nattaches\nto the Space Station\nstructure\noutboard\nof the beta joint\nflange\nwhich mounts\nto the interface\nstructure.\nThe solar dynamic\nsystem\ninterface\nwith the\nand distribution\nsystem\nis outboard\nof the solar dynamic\nfrequency\nconverter\nand remote\nbus isolators.\n\npower management\npower module\n\nThe offset\nNewtonian\nconcentrator\nconsists\nof a segment\nof a parent\nparabola\nwith a focal length\nto diameter\nratio of 0.25 mounted\noffset\nfrom the parent\nparabolic\naxis of revolution.\nThe receiver\nis tilted\n53 \xc2\xb0 with respect\nto the parent\nparabolic\naxis of revolution\nto achieve\na nearly\nsymmetric\nreceiver\ncavity\nflux distribution.\nThe offset\nconfiguration,\nused successfully\nin RF applications\nhas a low mass moment\ninertia\nabout\nthe transverse\nboom.\nIn addition,\nthere are no serious\n\nof\n\nprimary\nor secondary\nblockage\nproblems.\nThe offset\nreflector\ndoes cause\nlarger\ncosine\nlosses\nthan a symmetric\nNewtonian\nconcept.\nThe desirable\nsymmetric\nflux distributions\nare also more difficult\nto achieve.\n\nONCENTRATOR\n\nASSEMBLY\n\n_\n\nCHARACTERISTICS\nMASS (CONCENTRATOR\nONLY)\n2418 an\nassisted\nsingle\nhinges\n\ndesigned\nassembly\nerection.\n\nto perform\ntwo functions.\nThe hinges\nand latches\nare\naid designed\nfor either\nautomated\ndeployment\nor EVA\nAfter\nassembly\nof the 19 hexagonal\npanels\ninto a\n\nunit, the hinges\nand latches\nprovide\nstructural\nand latches\nare fabricated\nfrom aluminum.\n\nintegrity.\n\n_le\n\nThe hexagonal\npanels\nare an assembly\nof I-beams\nand corner\nshear fittings\nto which\ntriangular\nfacets\nare attached.\nThe hexagonal\npanel\nsuperstructure\nis entirely\ngraphite/epoxy\ncomposite\nconstruction\nwith the\ne_ception\nof the adjustment\nscrews.\nThe triangular\nfacets\nare each\napproximately\n3 ft. on a side.\nConstructed\nwith a honeycomb\ncore,\ngraphite/epoxy\ncomposite\nfacesheets,\nand a vapor reduce\nsurface\ndegradation.\nFocusing\nof the triangular\nfacets\nis accomplished\non the\nground\nprior to packaging\nby means of the adjustment\nscrews.\n\nSolar\n\n_oncentrator\nTruss\n\nI\n\nHardware\n\nHex\n\nI\n\nPanel\n\n^-,yl[\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nFacet-t o-Pone I\n\nTree\n\nI\n\nPane I\n\nPanel\nLatch\n\nI Mirror FocQt\nTruss strut solar concentrator support structure provides three\ndegree of freedom vernier pointing capability by incorporation of linear\nactuators into two of the three tripod struts.\nThis allows for solar\nvernier tracking by articulation\nof the concentrator.\nThis also reduces\nthe inertia that the actuator motor must overc_ne by requiring movement\nof only the concentrator.\nThe drawback of the three strut tripod\nconcentrator support system is the relatively poor structural dynamic\ncharacteristics\nexhibited by such a structure.\nShould one of the struts\nfail, there would exist no structure to support the concentrator in a\nstable manner.\nAn alternative to the three strut tripod concentrator support is the six\nstrut tripod support system shown below. Static and dynamic structural\nanalyses were performed to investigate the structural integrity of the\nthree\n\nstrut\n\nand\n\nsix\n\nstrut\n\ntripod\n\nsupport\n\nstructures.\n\nPictorials\nof both concepts\nand their fundamental\nfrequencies\nare shown\nbelow.\nThe inside\ndiameter\nand wall thickness\nof the three strut support\nstructure\nwere taken to be 3.0 in. and 0.15 in., respectively.\nThe\nequivalent\ndimensions\nfor the six strut support\nstructure\nwere i. 5 in.\nand 0.075\nin.\n\n3-STRUT TRIPOD CONCENTRATOR\nSUPPORT STRUCTURE\n\n6-STRUT TRIPOD Load\nC.G.\n\ntripod\n\np2 - 3 strut\n\ntripoo\n\npl\n\n- 8 strut\n\ntripod\n\np2\n\n0.75\n\npl - 3 strut\n\n- 5 strut\n\ntripod\n\n0.5\n\n0.25\n\n-0\n\n-0.25\n\n-0.5\n\n-0.75\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n4B\n\nIA\nStrut\n\nLongitudinal\n\nAxis\n\nIA - Fixed End\n4B - Concentrator\nAttach Pt.\npl - Plane I\np2 - Plane 2\n\n165\n\nSUMMARY\nThe Parabolic\nOffset\nLinearly\nActuated\nReflector\n(POLAR) solar\ndynamic\nmodule was selected\nas the baseline\ndesign\nfor a solar\ndynamic\npower\nsystem aboard the Space Station.\nThe POLAR concept\nwas chosen\nover other\ncandidate\ndesigns\nafter\nextensive\ntrade\nstudies.\nThe primary\nadvantages\nof the POLAR concept\nare the low mass moment of inertia\nof the module\nabout the transverse\nboom and the compactness\nof the stowed module which\nenables\npackaging\nof two complete\nmodules\nin the Shuttle\norbiter\npayload\nbay.\nThe fine\npointing\ncontrol\nsystem\nrequired\nfor the solar\ndynamic\nmodule\nhas been studied\nand initial\nresults\nindicate\nthat\nif disturbances\nfrom\nthe station\nare allowed\nto back drive\nthe rotary\nalpha joint,\npointing\nerrors\ncaused by transient\nloads on the Space Station\ncan be minimized.\nThis would allow\npointing\ncontrols\nto operate\nin bandwidths\nnear system\nstructural\nfrequencies.\nThe incorporation of the fine pointing control system into the solar\ndynamic module is fairly straightforward\nfor the three strut concentrator\nsupport structure.\nHowever, results of structural analyses indicate that\nthis three strut support is not optimum.\nIncorporation of a vernier\npointing system into the proposed six strut support structure is being\nstudi ed.\n\nREFERENCES\n\nl\n\nMcCormick,\n\n,\n\nC. O f t h e n u m e r o u s\nL S A c o n c e p t s t h a t h a v e b e e n p r o p o s e d ( s e e f o r example Refs. 1 , 2 and 3 ) , o n e\nc o n c e p t , r e f e r r e d t o as t h e Hoop-Column c o n c e p t , has been f a b r i c a t e d ( R e f . 2 ) .\nA 1 5 meter diameter model h a s been c o n s t r u c t e d f o r deployment, e l e c t r o m a g n e t i c\nand s t r u c t u r a l t e s t i n g . A s p a r t o f t h e t e s t program,\ns t a t i c and dynamic t e s t s h a v e b e e n p e r f o r m e d i n t h e L a n g l e y 1 6 Meter Vacuum\nChamber.\nR e s u l t s f r o m t h e s e t e s t s a n d comparisons w i t h p r e d i c t e d s t r u c t u r a l\nbehavior w i l l b e described.\n\n1 5 METER D I A M E T E R HOOP-COLUMN\n\n168\n\nANTENNA\n\nOUTLINE\nA number of structural tests and analyses have been performed on the 15\nmeter antenna model. This paper will eoncentrate on vibration test and analysis\nresults.\nAs indicated in the outline below, a description of the experimental\nand analytical\nmodels will be presented first.\nSecond, test and analysis\nresults will be presented for the antenna mounted on a tripod\nsupport.\nVibration\nresults have been divided into three areas: global modeswhich are\ndominated by overall column bending and hoop motion; hoop modes which are\ndominated by inplane and out-of-plane hoop (ring) bending; and mesh modeswhich\nare localized surface meshdistortions.\nResults from a simplified\nanalytical\nmodel will be presented followed by a description of the antenna vibrations when\nsupported by a pendulum cable.\n\nDescription of:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n15 meter hoop column antenna\nAntenna vibration tests\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Antenna analysis models\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Vibration modes with tripod support\nglobal, hoop and mesh\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nReduced analytical model\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nGlobal vibration modes\nCable suspension\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSummary\n\n169\n\nANTENNA DEPLOYMENT\n\nThe f i g u r e below shows t h e a n t e n n a deployment s e q u e n c e . The\na n a l y t i c a l model, r e f i n e d u s i n g t h e s t a t i c t e s t d a t a , was t h e n used t o recompute\nt h e f i r s t f o u r v i b r a t i o n modes of the a n t e n n a .\n\n175\n\nREFINED\nANALYTICAL ODEL ESULTS\nM\nR\nThe table below lists\nthe analysis and test frequencies after the antenna\nmodel was refined.\nGood frequency agreement was obtained.\nThis indicates\nstatic\ntest data should be used to refine the analytical models of large space\nstructures particularly\nsince the accuracy of static data increases as the\nflexibility\nof the structure\nincreases.\nAlso listed in the table are the\nmeasuredfrequencies and damping of the first four modes of the antenna.\nThe\ndamping is somewhat higher than usually found in spacecraft perhaps due to the\ndeployable joints and the tripod interface.\nThe damping increases when tested\nin ambient air. Unlike panel structures, the antenna shows very small decreases\nin frequency when tested in ambient air.\nThis indicates\nlarge lattice\nstructures can be tested in ambient air without significant\nchanges in vibration\nfrequencies of the global modes.\n\nMode\n\nRefined analysis\n\nTest\nVacuum\n\nF(HZ)\n\nAmbient air\n\nC/CR (%)\n1.9\n\nF (HZ)\nO.076\n\nC/CR (%)\n3.8\n\n1\n2 not been successfully\nreduced\ninto\na set\nof\nrecognizable\nmode\nshapes.\nThe high damping\nof the knit mesh results\nin rapid\ndissipation\nof the excitation\nenergy.\nSince the input energy\ndid not propagate\nlong distances,\nit was extremely\ndifficult\nto produce\na standing\nwave\n(vibration\nmode)\nin the surface\nmesh.\nTo insure\nthat membrane\ntheory\nwas adequate\nto model\nthe circular\nrod,\nfriction\ncould\namount\nof tension\nthat\nis applied\nto the mesh.\nNevertheless,\nthe\n\nreduce\nthe\nfrequencies\n\nincrease\nby the square\nroot of the This paper presents a summary of the development of two\nsensors able to provide this capability. The first to be discussed is SHAPES, which\nstands for Spatial, High-Accuracy, Position-Encoding Sensor.\nSHAPES is an\nelectro-optic sensor that combines optical angular position measurement with multiple\nlight-pulse time-of-flight range measurements to provide three-dimensional position\nmeasurements.\nThe unique characteristic of SHAPES is the capability to track\nmultiple targets with precision at a data rate sufficient for control purposes.\nThe second sensor we will discuss is FORS, an acronym for Fiber Optics Rotation\nSensor. FORS is a new type of gyro that utilizes semiconductor lasers and fiber\noptic waveguides, together with a unique integrated optics (IO) multifunction\noptical circuit design for optical signal processing. One of the most significant\ncharacteristics of FORS is the capability o f very long life provided by solid-state\ncomponents. Additional advantages are low weight and cost.\nIn addition to its\napplication as a precision navigation-grade gyro, FORS can be configured for\nmonitoring many points of a large space structure by using miniature remote gyro\nheads connected with optical fiber to a central FORS signal processor.\n\nFORS\n\nSHAPES\nLASER\nTRANSMITTER\n\nCCD DETECTOR\n\n1-0CHIP\nWITH STREAK\n\nTUBE\n\nPhotoelectrons\nare\nproduced\nat\nthe\nstreak\ntube\nphotocathode\nby\neach\nreturning\noptical\npulse.\nThe\nelectrons\nare\naccelerated\nby a optical\npulses\nreflected\nfrom an individual\ntarget\nduring\neach\nframing\nsequence\nwill\nstrike\nthe\nphosphor\nat the same point.\nThus each image point\non the CCD is the result\nof W\nSHAPES TEST FACILITY\nThe SHAPES Test Facility was designed to demonstrate the sensor\'s capability to\nsimultaneously track multiple moving targets with high precision. A 4-m-diameter\nstructure was built to support the moving targets in a circular arrangement that\nsimulates an antenna application. The SHAPES sensor was located in an adjoining\nroom, 12 m from the target array. Shown below are an array o f laser sources, a\ncommercial streak-tube camera, and the CCD assembly. The individual components of\nthe sensor have been separated for easy viewing.\nNot shown are the custom\nelectronics used to read the CCD and the microcomputer used to control the system.\n\nLASER\nTRANSMITTER\n\nCCD DETECTOR\n\nSTREAK TUBE\nCAMERA\n\n-\n\n~YNAMIC\nTARGET ARRAY\n\n21 1\n\nMULTITARGET DYNAMIC RANGE DEMONSTRATION\nIn a recent laboratory demonstration of the ranging capability of SHAPES, the range\nto each of eight moving targets was measured simultaneously.\nThe targets were\nTarget\nmounted on the 4-m-diameter SHAPES Test Facility described previously.\nmotion was provided by mounting the retroreflectors on either free-swinging\npendulums o r on motor-driven actuators. For the demonstration, eight individual\nlasers were mounted symmetrically around the periphery of the collecting lens of the\nstreak tube and pulsed simultaneously in synchronism with the streak-tube drive.\nEach laser was used to illuminate a single target. The data were processed to\nproduce a plot of the target movement as indicated below. The framing rate for\neight targets was 10 Hz with a relative range accuracy of 0.1 mm. This data update\nrate is sufficient for most control system requirements.\n\nData Acquisition Parameters\nNumber of Targets\nSensor\n\nShapes Framing Rate\nPosition Resolution\n(Static CHiP\n\nOE .\nI CONTROL\n\n_\n\nI C\'RCU\'TRY\n_\n\nHIGH\n\nBIREFRINGENT\n\nF\'_S%;\'_C,L\n\n215\n\nFIBEROPTICROTATION\nSENSOR ITHFULLYINTEGRATED CHIP\nW\nIO\nThe fiber gyro design under development is shownbelow. It utilizes\na semiconductor\nlaser, photodetectors, fiber optic waveguide for the sensing coil, and a unique\nintegrated optics (IO) multifunction\noptical circuit\ndesigned for optical signal\nprocessing. This IO configuration has an element which provides the dual function\nof polarizing and beam splitting\n(PBS). The IO chip also includes an optical phase\nmodulator (_M) for use in a serrodyne closed-loop phase control providing operation\nover a wide range of rotation rates.\nThe output signal is obtained by reading the\noptical frequency necessary for Sagnac phase nulling directly through the use of a\nMach-Zehnder (M-Z) interferometer that is constructed by tapping off from the arms\nof the Sagnacinterferometer.\nThe M-Z interferometer provides incremental position\nangle by meansof beats as in a ring laser gyro.\nIn this design, utilizing\nthe\nclosed-loop phase nulling technique, the effect of any scale factor nonlinearity is\ngreatly reduced and the auxiliary\nelectronics are simplified.\nThe complete IO\ncircuit\nfits\non a 5 cm long lithium niobate chip.\nThe IO chips are under\ndevelopment at AT&Tfor JPL.\n\nHI-BIREFRINGENT\nFIBER-OPTIC\nSENSING\nCOIL\n\n0.1\n\ndB BS\n\n3 dB\n3 dB Allen\nNASA Langley Research Center\nHampton, VA 23665\n\nFirst NASA/DOD CSI Technology Conference\nNorfolk, Virginia\nNovember 18-21, 1986\n\nPRECEDING\n\nPAGE BLANK\n\nNOT FILMED\n221\n\nNASA and other Government agencies have been exploring the unique characterist i c s of large space systems, from large o r b i t a l antennas to manned space stations,\nand have initiated the development of technologies required to assure the successful\nperformance of these next-generation spacecraft. The COFS I Project provides the\ninvaluable opportunity to t e s t , Val idate, and measure the effectiveness o f theories,\nstructural concepts, control systems, and f l i g h t c e r t i f i c a t i o n processes f o r future\nmissions through a research program focusing on multiple issues i n large flexible\nstructures, dynamics, and controls. The COFS I Project consists of a s e r i e s o f\nground and f l i g h t a c t i v i t i e s b u i l d i n g progressively from modeling and dynamic\ncharacterization of large space systems to the more complex issues of flexible-body\ncontrol. The COFS I Project also involves the space structures and controls\nresearch community from universities, industry, and Government t h r o u g h a formal\nGuest Investigator Program i n which researchers are selected t o participate i n a l l\nprogram activity phases.\n\n222\n\nCOFS I TECHNOLOGY GOALS\nCOFS I is the initiation\nare required\nto achieve\nthe\ntechniques\nwill\nbe used FLIGHT EXPERIMENT\n\nI1\n\'\n\nThe approach, which has been adopted for COFS I research, centers on a f l i g h t /\nt e s t structure w i t h selected generic characteristics o f large space structures.\nThis\'centerpiece o f the COFS I Project, the Mast f l i g h t system, contains a large\n(60-meters long) depl oyahl e/res towabl e "next-generation" truss beam attached i n a\ncantilever fashion t o i t s c a r r i e r (the STEP) which i s , i n t u r n , attached within the\nSpace Transportation System (STS) Orbiter payload Say. Actuators, instrumentation,\nand avionics necessary for excitation, measurement, and control of the low-frequency\nmodes o f this structure are an integral p a r t of the f l i g h t system. The beam truss\nalso contains a Parameter Modification Subsystem (PMS) which provides the capability\nto change f l i g h t system physical properties, thereby a1 tering frequency spacing and\ncross axis coupling between modes. The Mast f l i g h t system will be used t o suppcirt\ntwo f l i g h t research missions from the STS Orbiter.\n\n~\n\nI\n\n227\n\nCOFS I\n\nFLIGHT\n\nTESTS\n\nThe Mast Flight\nSystem,\nbridging\nthe gap between\nground\nmodal verification,\non-orbit\nmodal verification,\nand validation\nof control\nmethodologies,\nmakes use of\nthe same flight\nstructure\nfor both the ground\ntests\nand the two flight\ntests.\nAs\nstructural\ncharacteristics\nare identical,\nground\nmodal testing\nand on-orbit\nmodal\ntesting\ncan be compared\nand evaluated.\nAlso,\nas no hardware\nmodifications\nare\nrequired\nfor flight\ntwo, the structural\ncharacteristics\nwill\nbe the same for\nthe\ndistributed\nflex-body\ncontrol\nexperiment\nof that\nflight\nand the systems\nidentification\nexperiment\nof the first\nflight;\ni.e.,\nthe control\nalgorithm\nwill\nwork against\na system model that\nhas been previously\ncharacterized,\nboth on the ground and onorbit.\nMast I. - The first\nflight\n(Mast I) of the two mission\nscenario\nbasically\naddresses\nthe aspects\nof large\nflexible\nstructures\nand structural\ndynamics\nwith\nrudimentary\nresearch\ntasks\nin structural\ndamping.\nThe Mast I experiment\nis\ndynamically\nexcited\nby the subsystems\nof the Mast experiment\nat various\nlengths\nof\nextension,\nwith\nand without\nmodal alteration\nby the Parameter\nModification\nSubsystem\n(PMS), and response\ndata collected.\nAt the conclusion\nof the flight\ntest\nsequence,\nwhich\nshould\noccupy three\nor four\ndays of activity,\nthe flight\nsystem will\nbe\nrestowed\nfor the descent\nand landing\nphase.\nUpon return\nof the flight\ndata,\nsystems\nidentification\ntechniques\nand ground-based\nanalyses\nwill\nbe conducted\nto structurally\nand dynamically\ncharacterize\nthe beam-truss\nin the space environment\nand to establish\nand improve\nthe validity\nof preflight\nground\ntest\ndata and analytical\nmodels.\nMast II.\n- In the second flight\nexperiment\nof the COFS I Project,\nthe Mast test\narticle\nwill\nbe reflown\nwithout\nmodification\nof its\nhardware\ncomplement.\nDuring\nthe\nMast II flight,\nthe beam structure\nwill\nbe excited,\nresponse\ndata collected,\nand the\nmodal characteristics\ndetermined\nby ground-based\nsystems\nidentification\nto verify\nthat\nthe structure\nis modally\nidentical\nto that\nflown\non the first\nflight.\nExperiments in distributed\ncontrol\nwill\nbe conducted\nto evaluate\nperformance\nof flex-body\ncontrol\nalgorithms\noperating\nin conjunction\nwith\na structure\nthat\nhas been modally\ncharacterized\nafter\nlaunch.\n\n228\n\nGROUND-BASED TECHNOLOGY\nPrior\nto flight\nmission\nactivities,\nthere\nis an extensive\nground-based\nprogram\nto be conducted\nin-house\nto prepare\nfor the flight\nphase.\nThese activities\ninclude:\n(1) Utilization\nof prototypic\nfull\nscale\n20-meter\ntruss\nbeam segment ("mini-mast")\nto aid in development\nof structural\nmodeling\nmethodologies\nand as a preliminary\ntest\nbed for examining\nMast full\nscale\ndynamic\ncharacterization\ntechniques\nand to evaluate flex-body\ncontrol\nalgorithms\nfor a distributed\ncontrol\nsystem,\nand (2) Development of one-quarter\nand one-half\nscale\nstructural\nmodels of the Mast beam truss;\ncomplete\nground-based\ncharacterization\nof these systems\nwill\nbe accomplished\nthrough\nmodeling,\ntesting,\nand analysis\nof the scaled\ncomponents\nand assemblies.\nPrediction\nof Mast full\nscale\ncharacteristics\nwill\nbe developed\nfrom sub-scale\nmodel results,\nand correlation\nof these\npredictions\nwith\nresults\nof Mast flight\nsystem testing,\nground\nand space-based,\nwill\nbe accomplished.\n\nGROUND\n\nGROUND\n\nTEST\n\nTEST It\nis planned to static test a mini-Mast two-bay model. This is a\ncalibration test which will be used in the model validation\nprocess to obtain a validated FEM of a two-bay model. Different\ntest configurations,\nsuch as varlous loads and different\nboundary conditions, are planned.\n\nSubassemblies\n\nAnalysis\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Two-bay\nFEM\nKx=\nF\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nEquivalent\nmodels\n\nPDE\n\nF\n\nTest\nStatic\n\nload\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 various\n\nvs deflection\nload\n\npoints\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 different\nboundary\nconditions\n\n241\n\nMini-Mast/Mast\nThe baseline\nbattens,\nand\n\nconfiguration\nthree diagonal\n\nBaseline\nconsists\nelements\n\n2 Bays\n\n242\n\nConfiguration\nof three longerons,\nper bay.\n\nthree\n\nScale Models\nThe objective of the scale modelprogram is to study the\nvalidity\nof using scale models to simulate flight structures.\nThe approach will be to procure and test four replica models.\nThe scales of the four models are I/2, I/4, and I/5. There are\ntwo I/5 scale models planned. Also, similar ground tests and\nanalyses programs as in mini-Mast and Mast are planned.\n\nObjective:\n\nvalidity\n\nflight\n\nApproach"\n\nStudy\n\nstructure\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFour\n\nof scale\n\nreplica\n\nmodels\n\nmodels\n\nfor simulating\n\nplanned\n\n-1/2,\n\n1/4,\n\nprograms\n\nas\n\n1 / 5 (2) scale\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSimilar\n\ntests\n\nin mini-Mast\n\nand analysis\nand Mast\n\n243\n\nGround Test/Analysis\n\nProgram\n\nThe full scale testing of the mini-Mast will initially\ntake\nplace in the Structural Dynamics Research Laboratory. The test\norientation is vertical and cantilevered from the base. In this\norientation the joints should all be loaded in one direction and\nstatic as well as dynamic tests will be run. Next, the truss\nwill be loaded in tension at the free end to load the joints in\nthe opposite direction. The sametests will be repeated. The\nresults of each test are run through the model validation\nprocess. It is anticipated that static test results will be used\nto update the stiffness matrix of the FEMand dynamics test\nresults used to update the mass matrix.\nThe mini-Mast tests will be repeated with the longitudinal axis\nin the horizonal direction. Cables will be used to support part\nof the weight of the structure.\nFinally,\nvertical\n\ncontrol research testing is planned for both the\nand horizonal orientations.\n\nAnalysis\n\nFull\n\nTest\n\nscale\n\nStatic - load vs deflection\n\nKX = F gravitYeffectsand\nincluding\nsuspension\n\nMX + KX = 0 gravity and\nincluding\n\nF_\n\n2_g\n\nM_ + Kx = F including gravity\nand suspension\neffects\n\nT\n\nV\n\n_\n\nsuspension effects\n\nI\n\nTTF\n-\'_\n\n/\n\nDynamic - vibration\n\n_\n\nand damping\n\nUpdated/\n\nClosed - loop control tests\n\xe2\x80\xa2 vibration modes,\nfrequencies and damping\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n244\n\nmodes,\n\nfrequencies\n\nactive damping\n\nMini-Mast Test Configurations\nTest configurations A and B are the vertical orientations which\nwill be located in the tower in building 1293Bat the Langley\nResearch Center (LaRC). Test configuration C is the horizontal\norientation which will be in the hanger annex of building 1244\nat the LaRC.\nFor test configuration A the vibration frequency spectrum from 0\nto 21 Hertz is given. The next frequency is\nthe first torsional frequency at 11.3 Hertz.\n\nT\n\nT\n\nT\n\nI\n\n2g\n\nI\n\nI\n\n_L ..................\nB\n\n(2)\n\n(2)\n\nI\nI\n\n0\n\nI\n\n,\n\n2\n\nI, I\n4\n\nI\n\nI\n\n6\n\nI\n\nI I\n\n8\n\nI\n\nI0\n\n(2)(3)\n\n,),,,I,I,\n,I,,,,\n12\n\n14\n\n16\n\n18\n\n20\n\nFrequency,Hz\nTest configuration\n\nA\n\n245\n\nFlight\n\nTest/Analysis\n\nProgram\n\nThe flight test of the Mast flight system will be conducted\nafter extensive ground testing has concluded. The result of the\nground tests should be a verified mathematical model of the Mast\nflight system. Initial\nflight tests will confirm the validity of\nthese math models. If necessary, which is likely to be the case,\nflight test data will be used to update and improve these math\nmodels. Both open-loop dynamics tests and closed-loop controls\ntests are planned.\n\nAnalysis\n\nFlight\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MY + Kx= 0\n\ntest\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xc2\xa2)i\' oo.\n\nData acquisition of\nfree decay responses\nmodes, frequencies,\nand damping\n\nMeasure performance\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MY+ Kx= Bu\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 active damping\n\nControl research\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 closed-loop\nmodes, frequencies,\nand damping\n"_\n\nComparsion/\n\nAdjust performance\nparameters\n\n246\n\nCable Suspension Effect Study\nA study of the effect of cables on the vibration frequencies and\nmodeshapes of the cantilevered Mast truss is shownbelow. The\nmajor conclusions of this study are that only the first natural\nfrequency is affected by cables. It was also concluded that\ncables do not affect the first modeshape and the first natural\nfrequency is not affected by the cable arrangement.\n\n50\n40\nPercent error in first\nnatural frequency\n\n3O\n20\n10\n\n0\n\n12\n\n,,\n\n24\n\n36\n\n48\n\n60\n\nCable length, m\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nOnlyfirst natural frequency\ni s affected\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCablesdo not affect first mode\nshape\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFirst natural frequency is not\naffectedby number of cables.\n\n247\n\nMast/Orbiter Vibration\n\nFrequency\n\nA 2160-degree-of-freedom finite element model of Mast with the\norbiter has been modeled in NASTRANnd EAL. The vibration\na\nfrequency spectrum from 0 to 21 Hertz is shown.\nThe first six frequencies are the rigid body frequencies. The\nnext two frequencies are the first bending frequencies in the\nfore and aft direction and the side-to-side or port-starboard\ndirection. The next group of three frequencies consists of two\nsecond bending frequencies and the first torsion. One second\nbending and the first torsional frequency are at the same\nfrequency which is one of the requirements of the structural\ndesign.\n\n2160-deg\nree-of -freed om\nfinite element model\n\nI\n\n8\nFrequency, Hz\n\n248\n\nI\n\n9\n\n, I,II\n10\n\n11\n\nClosed-Loop Simulation Of COFS-I Flight Test\nClosed-loop simulations of the Mast flight test have been\nconducted. The actuators have been modeled as ideal linear\ndevices in the simulation. The curve on the left shows\nexcitation, free vibration,\nand dampingof the second mode. The\ncurve on the right is only excitation of the second modebut\nwithout a spring or relative position control between the proof\nmass of the actuator and the structure. The results of this\nstudy indicate the need for relative position control.\n\nACTUATOR\n\nCOFS-I\n\nMODEL\n\n2rid mode\nActuators\n\nused for\n\n10 X 1(_\nSECOND-M\n20 X 1-0_\n\'_\n\nSECOND-MODE\n\nTEST\nOD.._E\n\n,2t_Exc.r --I- AMP\n\'ELt\n\n2\n\n4\n\nACTUATOR - BLANK\n\nNOT\n\nFILMED\n\n253\n\nc\n\n254\n\nMAST FLIGHT\n\nEXPERIMENT FLIGHT SYSTEM CONCEPT\n\nThe Mast Flight\nSystem is composed of several\nsubsystems.\nPrimary\namong\nthese\nis the Deployable\nMast Subsystem\n(DMS) which\nconsists\nof a beam assembly\nand an associated\nmechanism\nfor deploying\nand retracting\nthe beam.\nThe beam\nassembly\nis a joint\ndominated\ngraphite\nepoxy and titanium\ntruss\nas is expected\nof future\nlarge\nspace structures.\nImportant\nbeam characteristics\nare listed\nbelow.\nIntegral\nto the beam assembly\nare actuators,\nsensors\nand associated\nelectronics\nwhich\nare available\nfor excitation\nand damping as desired\nby the\nexperimenter.\nThe beam structural\ncharacteristics\ncan also be modified\nas\ndesired\nby the experimenter\nusing\nthe Parameter\nModification\nDevice\ninstalled\nat the end of the beam.\nData measured\non the beam by the sensors\nand commands\nto the actuators\nare transmitted\nalong\nthe beam digitally\nat 150 Hz using\na\nstandard\n1553 type bus.\nThe Modular\nDistributed\nInformation\nSubsystem\n(MDIS)\ncomputer\nfunctions\nas bus master\nand ensures\nthat\nall\nexperimental\ndata is\nsaved for future\nanalysis.\nThe MDIS computer\nalso performs\na safing\nfunction\nto prevent\ninadvertent\noverexcitation\nof the beam.\nFinally,\nthe Excitation\nand Damping Subsystem\n(EDS) computer\nis available\nto the experimenter\nfor\nimplementation\nof control\nalgorithms\nor any other\nnumerical\noperations\nas\ndesired.\nData from all\nsystem sensors\ncan be accessed\nby the EDS computer.\n\n255\n\nMAST\n\nFLIGHT\n\nEXPERIMENT\n\nFLIGHT\n\nSYSTEM\n\nCONCEPT\n\n100 kg TIP MASS\n\nPARAMETER CHARACTERISTICS\n\nBeam length\ncan be changed\nby the experimenter\nto coarsely\nalter\nbeam\nstructural\ncharacteristics.\nIn addition,\nthe Parameter\nModification\nDevice\n(PMD),\nlocated\nat the tip\nof the beam, can be used to effect\nfine\nchanges\nin\nthe beam structural\ncharacteristics\nand to cause frequency\nmatching\nand\ncoupling\nbetween\neither\nof the two beam second bending\nmodes and the first\ntorsion\nmode.\nThe PMD functions\nby changing\nthe moment of inertia\nof the\ntip\nstation\nabout the beam axis.\nAdditionally\nthe PMD can be used to move\nthe center\nof gravity\nof the tip\nstation\ncreating\nmass coupling\nbetween\nthe\nbending\nand torsion\nmodes.\nThe experimenter\ncan move the PMD masses as\ndesired,\nincluding\na slow sweep of the PMD masses from one location\nto\nanother\nwhile\nexcitation\nand damping are occurring.\nA typical\nmodal coupling\nmaneuver\nis shown below.\nThe beam length\nis first\nadjusted\nto nearly\nmatch\na second bending\nmode and the first\ntorsion\nmode (48 bays).\nThe PMD masses\nare then adjusted\nto precisely\nmatch the torsion\nmode frequency\nwith\nthe\nselected\nbending\nmode frequency.\nThe masses are then moved in the same\ndirection\nto offset\nthe tip\nstation\ncenter\nof gravity\nand cause the coupling\ndesired\nby the experimenter.\n\n259\n\nCONCEPT\n\nFOR MODAL COUPLING\nMANEUVER\n\nMAST SYSTEM ACTUATORS AND INSTRUMENTATION\nActuators\nand sensors\nare distributed\nalong the beam as shown below.\nAll\nsensor\noutputs\nare available\nto the experimenter\nfor control\nor monitoring\nof\nbeam performance.\nIn addition,\nselect\naccelerometer\nchannels\nhave dual\nranges\nand filtered\nor unfiltered\noutputs.\nThe filters\nused are two pole butterworth\nfilters,\nwith\na 3 db cutoff\nat 45 Hertz,\nfor aliasing\nprotection.\nStrain\ngages are additionally\nhigh pass filtered\nto eliminate\ndrift\nand thermal\napparent\nstrain.\nActuators\nare provided\nat three of the intermediate\nremote\nstations\nand the tip.\nThe actuators\nused at all\nlocations\nare Linear\nDirect\nCurrent\nMotors\n(LDCMs).\nThe LDCMs can be used to provide\nlinear\nforces\nperpendicular\nto the beam axis\n(parallel\nto the Orbiter\nX and Y axes)\nat\neach remote\nstation\nand torque\nabout the beam axis\nat the tip\nremote\nstation.\nAccelerometers\nare equipped\nwith\nself\ncalibration\nports\nand strain\ngage\nbridges\nhave shunt\ncalibration\ncircuits\nfor pre and post experiment\ninstrumentation\nhealth\nchecks\nif desired.\nSelect\ntemperatures,\nvoltages\nand\ncurrents\nare measured\nto provide\nfurther\nhealth\nand status\ninformation\n(not\nshown below for clarity).\nACTUATORS\n\nTIP STATION\n\n_\n\nBAY 44\n\n--\n\n_\n\nACCELEROMETERS\nAND INSTRUMENTATION\n\n4 LDCMS\n\n3 RATE GYROS\n3 LINEAR ACCELEROMETERS\n9 STRAIN BRIDGES\n\n2 LDCMS\n\n1 e c t current brushless motors. The motor has a\nfixed stator and a moving magnetic secondary. The secondary i s used as a\nreaction mass. O u t p u t force i s controlled by controlling motor current. I n\naddition the motor i s designed w i t h a sensing system to provide accurate\nsecondary position information. A position loop has been implemented to keep\nthe secondary centered. The LDCM, including the position loop, has a\ns t r a i g h t forward two pole transfer function. Secondary position, l i k e a l l\nother sensor data, i s available t o the experimenter. The figure below shows\na development model LDCM. T h i s motor i s capable of a 30 Newton force output.\n\n262\n\nMAST FLIGHT SYSTEM BLOCK DIAGRAM\n\nA block\ndiagram\nof the system is shown below.\nThe diagram\nillustrates\nthe primary\nelements\nof the system and the provisions\nfor experimental\ncommands\nand data gathering.\nThe mission\nspecialist\nwill\ninterface\nto the system from\nthe orbiter\naft\nflight\ndeck through\nthe DDCU. The beam will\nbe deployed\nand\nretracted\nthrough\nthis\ninterface\nvia the MDIS computer\nwhich commands the motor\ncontroller.\nExperiments\nwill\nalso be executed\nthrough\nthis\ninterface\nvia the\nMDIS computer.\nEach experimenter\'s\nsoftware\nwill\nbe contained\nwithin\nthe EDS\ncomputer.\nStandard\nsystem commands, such as sine sweep or broadband\nrandom\nactuator\nforce\nand PMD commands, will\nbe furnished\nwith\nthe system and can be\ncalled\nupon by the experimenter\nsingly\nor in series.\nAll\nexperimental\ndata\n(as well\nas system housekeeping\ndata)\nis passed\nthrough\nthe HRM onto the high data rate\nlink\nthrough\nTDRSS to the ground\nwhere it\nis stored\non magnetic\ntape for detailed\npost flight\nanalyses.\nA limited\namount of selectable\ndata (limited\nby 2Kbps serial\ndata link),\nas determined\nby the experimenter,\ncan be passed to the ground\nduring\nthe\nmission\nfor near real\ntime evaluation\nto verify\nproper\nexperiment\nconduct.\n\nELECTRONICS\n\nTIP\n\nBAY\n\n44\n\n_\n\n--1\n\nACTUATORS\nSENSORS\nPMD\nELECTRONICS\n\nI\n\n-\'_\n1553\n\nBEAM\nMOUNTED\nEQUIPMENT\n\nBAY\n\n38\n\nBAY\n\n30\n\nBAY\n\nBUS\n\n24\n\nI\n\nELECTRON,CS\nIl\nPOWE_\n24\n\nSENSORS\nELECTRONICS\n\nBAY\n\n12\n\nJ\n\nTO REMOTE\n341V\nSTATIONS\nPOWER\n\nI\n\n----)\nmum\n\nmmmm\nSENSORS\nI BASE\nELECTRONICS\n\nPALLET\n\nPALLET\n\nMOUNTED\nEQUIPMENT\n\n",_i\n\nMOTOR\n\nA\n\nCONTROLLER\n\nMDIS\n\nA\n\nI\n\nUPCONVERTER\n\nl\n\nDATA\nm\n\nn\n\nilmm m\n\nmilm\n\nail\n\nn\n\nm\n\nm\n\nlilu\n\nn\n\nn\n\nn\n\nDDCU\nSTEP\nPALLET\nEQUIPMENT\n\nnmm nm\n\nmmmme\niron\n\nA\n\nSFMDM\n\n___\n\nmmm m\n\nmnln\n\nCONTROL\nBOX\nPOWER\n\nl\n\nt\nCOMMANDS\n\nDISPLAY\nDATA\n\nLOW\n/\n\nRATE\n\nDATA\n\nHIGH\nRATE\nDATA\n\nV\nMISSION\n\nSPECIALIST\n\n263\n\nN87-\n\n16031\n\nMAST FLIGHT SYSTEM\nBEAM STRUCTURE\nAND\nBEAM STRUCTURAL\nPERFORMANCE\n\nDavid\n\nC.\n\nLenzi\n\nJohn\nW. TO EVALUATE\n\nBEAM\n\nDAMPING\n\nFigure\n\nOF THE\n\nBEAM\n\nUSING\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nREAL-TIME\n\n2\n\n301\n\nMAST\n\n2 FLIGHT\n\nTEST\n\nOBJECTIVES\n\nThe\nsecond\nflight\nof MAST will\nemphasize\nflexible\nbody\ncontrols\nexperiments.\nA major\nthrust\nwill\nbe the\nuse\nof\nboth\ncollocated\nand distributed\nactuators\nand\nsensors.\nBoth\nthe\nopen-loop\nand\nclosed-loop\nstability\nwill\nbe evaluated\nvery\ncarefully.\nOf\nparticular\ninterest\nwill\nbe the\nlevel\nof maturity\nof\nthe\nvarious\ncontrol\nlaw\ndesign\nmethodologies\nand whether\nthey\nprovide\nsufficiently\nrobust\ncontrol\nmechanizations\nfor\nlarge,\nlightweight,\nflexible\nstructures.\nThe second\nflight\nwill\nprovide\nthe\nvehicle\nfor\nimplementing\nthe\nselected\nguest\ninvestigator\nexperiments.\n(The\nguest\ninvestigator\nprogram\nwill\nbe discussed\nby Mr.\nAnthony\nFontana\nin\nthe\nnext\npaper.)\nThe\nremainder\nof\nplanning\nassociated\n\nMAST\n\nthis\n\npaper\nwill\nwith\nthe\nfirst\n\nfocus\nflight\n\non\n\nthe\nof\n\nflight\nMAST.\n\noperations\n\n2\n\nEMPHASIZES\n\nFLEXIBLE\n\nBODY\n\nCONTROLS\n\nCONTROL\nSENSORS.\n\nLAWS\n\nUSING\n\nCOLLOCATED\n\n2. TO DEMONSTRATE\nCONTROL\nSENSORS\nAND ACTUATORS.\n\nLAWS\n\nUSING\n\nDISTRIBUTED\n\n1. TO DEMONSTRATE\nACTUATORS\nAND\n\n3. TO DEMONSTRATE\nSTABILITY.\n\nBOTH\n\nOPEN-LOOP\n\nEXPERIMENTS\n\nAND\n\nCLOSED-LOOP\n\n4. TO PROVIDE A TEST STRUCTURE\nFOR IMPLEMENTATION\nGUEST INVESTIGATOR\nEXPERIMENTS.\n\nFigure\n\n302\n\n3\n\nOF\n\nTHE\n\nTYPICAL\n\nFLIGHT\n\nPLANNING\n\nPROCESS\n\nThe Marshall\nSpcce\nFlight\nCenter(MSFC)\nprovides\nthe Mission\nManager\nfor\nthe MAST\nFlight\nExperiment.\nThis Mission\nManager\nacts\nas the\ninterface\nor representative\nbetween\nthe experiment\nteam\nat Langley\nResearch\nCenter(LaRC)\nand the flight\nplanning\ngroup\nat Johnson\nSpace\nCenter(JSC).\nPersonnel\nat the Johnson\nSpace\nCenter\nprepare\nall the flight\nprocedures,\ncrew\nchecklists,\nflight\ntimelines,\netc.,\nnecessary\nto support\nthe actual\nflight\nexperiment\non the orbiter.\nThe physical\nintegration\nand mounting\nof the flight\nhardware\nis done\nby personnel\nat the Kennedy\nSpace\nCenter(KSC).\nThe\nkey to this whole\nprocess\nis an Integrated\nPayload\nRequirements\nDocument(IPRD).\nTo develop\nthis\ndocument,\nthe\nMission\nManager\nmust\npull\nin a number\nof different\nrequirement\ndocuments,\nbeginning\nwith\nthe Experiment\nRequirements\nDocument(ERD)\nthat\nis unique\nto the MAST\nexperiment\nand is\nprepared\nat LaRC.\nThere\nare a myriad\nof existing\nrequirements\ndocuments\nthat\nare levied\non any orbiter\nflight\nexperiment.\nThe\nprimary\nones\nare:\nNHB\n1700.7A\n- Safety\nPolicy\nand Requirements\nfor Payloads\nUsing\nthe Space\nTransportation\nSystem\n(STS),\nJSC 07700\nVOLUME\nXIV - Space\nShuttle\nSystem\nPayload\nAccommodations,\nSLP/2104\n- Spacelab\nPayload\nAccommodation\nHandbook,\nand JA 447 - Mission\nRequirements\non\nFacilities/Instruments/Experiments\nfor Space\nTransportation\nSystem\n(STS)\nAttached\nPayloads.\nThe\nIntegrated\nPayload\nRequirements\nDocument(IPRD)\ndevelops\na\nspecific\nset of requirements\nto guide\nthe STS\nintegration\nand the\nflight\noperations,\nspecifies\na set of design\nand performance\nrequirements\nfor any unique\ninterface\nhardware\nor software\nneeded\nto accommodate\nthe experiment\nsystem,\nand establishes\nthe\nrequired\nverification\nthe experimenter\nmust\nperform\nto demonstrate\nthat\nall experiment\nsystems\nadequately\nmeet\nall orbiter/carrier\nrequirements.\nOut of the IPRD comes\nan Instrument\nInterface\nAgreement\nthat\ngoverns\nthe design\nand development\nof the interface\nbetween\nthe experiment\nsystem\nand the STEP/Spacelab\npallet,\nand\nan Operations\nand Integrating\nAgreement\nthat\nguides\nthe\nintegration\nof the experiment/carrier\ninto the orbiter\nand the orbital\nexperiment\noperations.\nA detailed\nPayload\nIntegration\nPlan is developed\nat JSC for use\nin the actual\nphysical\nintegration\nat KSC and in the final\nflight\nplanning.\nThe actual\ncrew\ndocuments\ncome\nout of this\nflight\nplanning\nprocess.\nThere\nare many\ndesign\nreviews,\nplanning\nmeetings,\nflight\noperations\nreviews,\nflight\nreadiness\nreviews,\nsafety\nreviews,\netc.,\nthat cannot\nbe illustrated\nhere,\nbut\nthe\nexperiment\ndesign\ncertification\nprocess\nwill\nbe described\nfurther\nin the next\nfigure.\n\n303\n\nREQUIREMENTS\nNHB\n\nEXPERIMENT\nREQUIREMENTS\nREQUIREMENTS\nDOCUMENT\n\n1700.7A\nSAFETY\n\nJSC 07700\n\nVOL. FLIGHT\nDESIGN\n\nAGREEMENT\n\nPAYLOAD\nINTEGRATION\n\nPLAN\n\nPAYLOAD/ORBITER\nINTEGRATION\nFLIGHT\nOPERATIONS\n\nFigure\n\n304\n\nINSTRUMENT\nINTERFACE\n\n4\n\nEXPERIMENT\nDESIGN\nCERTIFICATION\n\nI\n\nVERIFICATION\nEXPERIMENT\nPLANS\n\nI\n\nCERTIFICATION\n\nSince\nthe primary\nemphasis\nin certification\nis safety,\nit will\nbe\nreferred\nto as the\nsafety\nprocess.\nThere\nare a number\nof\nrequirements\nthat must\nbe met\nin the safety\nprocess,\nbut the key\ngoverning\ndocuments\nare as follows:\nNHB 1700.7A\n(which\nhas\nalready\nbeen\nnamed),\nJSC 13830\n- Implementation\nProcedure\nfor STS\nPayloads\nSystem\nSafety\nRequirements,\nand JA-O12B\n- Spacelab\nProject\nOffice\nPayload\nSafety\nImplementation\nApproach.\nThe\nprocess\nis coordinated\nby the MSFC\nMission\nManager\nin cooperation\nwith\ncounterparts\nat JSC and KSC.\nFirst,\nthe exprimenter\nmust\nprepare\na set of hazard\nreports\nthat identifies\nand describes\neach\nhazard\nassociated\nwith\nthe flight\nhardware\nand experiment\nprocess.\nThe\ninitial\npreparation\nis soon\nafter\nthe experiment\nconceptual\ndesign\nreview\nand is submitted\nprior\nto scheduling\na\nPhase\n0 safety\nreview.\nHazard\nreports\nmust\nbe maintained\ncurrent\nand updated\nin keeping\nwith\nthe phased\nsafety\nreviews\nso that\nthey\nalways\nreflect\nthe current\nexperiment\nconfiguration.\nA set of safety\nverification\nplans\nmust\nbe developed\njointly\nbetween\nthe experimenter\nand the Mission\nManager\nthat\nspecify\nthe\nprocess\nby which\nthe experimenter/integrator\nwill\nverify\nthat\nthe\npayload\nmeets\nall safety\nrequirements.\nThe hazard\nverification\nplans\nmust\nalso\nbe updated\nand eventually\ncontain\nresults\nobtained\nwhen\nimplementing\nthe various\ntest/verification\nprocedures\nin the plans.\nBoth\nhazard\nreports\nand safety\nverification\nplans/results\nbecome\npart of the safety\ncompliance\ndata\npackages\nsubmitted\nto support\nthe phased\nsafety\nreviews.\nThe Mission\nManager\nreviews\nall experimenter\nprepared\ndocuments\nfor completeness\nand accuracy\nand assembles\nthe official\nsafety\ndata\npackages\nto the appropriate\nsafety\nreview\npanels.\nThis\nis\nan iterative\nprocess\nthat\nis related\nto the overall\nexperiment\ndevelopment\nand integration\nprocess.\nIt is geared\ntoward\nassuring\nthat\nthe payload\nis certified\nsafe\nto fly at some\ntime\njust\nprior\nto the final\nflight\nreadiness\nreview.\n\n305\n\nSAFETY PROCESSS\nGoverning\n\nRequirements\n\nand\n\nProcedures\n\nNHB major\nmilestones\nin\nthe\nexperiment\ndevelopment\nand\nintegration\nprocess\nare\nshown\nin\nFigure\n6.\nThe reader\nis\nadvised\nthat\nthis\nis\nnot\nthe\nofficial\ncontract\nschedule\nfor\ndevelopment\nof the\nMAST\nFlight\nSystem(MFS).\nRather,\nthis\nis an attempt\nto\nfit\na set\nof\nintegration\nmilestones\ninto\nthe\ntime\nbounded\nby initiation\nof\nthe\nprogram\nand\nthe\nbest\nestimate\nof\nthe\nlaunch\ndate.\nCurrently,\nthe\nMAST-1\nflight\ntest\nis\nscheduled\nfor\na May 9,\n1991\nlaunch.\nA\nconceptual\ndesign\nreview(CoDR)\nhas been\nheld\nfor\nthe\ntruss\nbeam,\nand\na preliminary\ndesign\nreview(PDR)\nfor\nthe\nMAST Flight\nSystem\nis\nanticipated\nabout\nthe\nend\nof\nMarch\n1987.\nCritical\ndesign\nreview(CDR)\nwill\nfollow\nPDR by approximately\n6\nto\n7\nmonths.\nAfter\nthe\nMAST Flight\nSystem\nis\ndelivered\nto\nLaRC,\nthere\nwill\nbe\na period\nof\nground\ntesting\nto refine\nthe\nmodeling\nand\npredictions\nas well\nas validation\nof flight\nsoftware.\nThe MFS must\nthen\nbe\nrecertified\nfor\nflight\nbefore\ndelivery\nto KSC.\nThe process\nof\nintegrating\nthe\nMFS with\nthe\nSpace\nShuttle\nhas\nalready\nbegun\nwith\ndicussions\nof\npreliminary\nrequirements\nand\npreparation\nof\nthe\ninitial\nreports\nidentifying\npotential\nsafety\nhazards.\nThis\nis\nleading\nto\na Phase\nZero\nsafety\nreview\nnear\nthe\nend\nof\nthis\nyear.\nAn integration\nrequirements\nreview(IRR)\nwill\nfollow\nthe\nexperiment\nPDR by some\n6 to 8 weeks\nand will\nresult\nin\ndocumentation\nof\nall\nthe\nrequirements\nthat\nmust\nbe met\nin\nthe\nprocess\nof\nintegrating\nthe\nMFS with\nthe\ncarrier\npallet\nand\nthen\nwith\nthe\nSpace\nShuttle\norbiter.\nThe actual\ninterface\n(both\nhardware\nand\nsoftware)\nbetween\nthe\nexperiment\nand the\norbiter\nnow\nstarts\nthrough\na design\nand\nverification\nprocess.\nThere\nwill\nbe\nan integration\npreliminary\ndesign\nreview(IPRD)\nand\ncritical\ndesign\nreview(ICDR)\nwhich\nwill\nresult\nin\nthe\nnecessary\nbaseline\ndocuments\nand\nconfiguration\ncontrol\ndocuments.\nAfter\nthe\nIPDR,\nthe\nflight\nplanning\nactivity\nbegins\nin\nearnest\nat\nJSC.\nThis\nis\nan iterative\nprocess\nthat\ninvolves\nthe\ncontinual\nparticipation\nof appropriate\nMSFC and LaRC personnel.\nNumerous\nflight\nplanning\ndocuments\nmust\nbe prepared\nand\nreviewed\nto\nestablish\nand\nvalidate\nprecise\noperational\nprocedures\nfor\nthe\nflight.\nThe various\nworking\ngroups\nand reviews\nat JSC are\ntoo\nnumerous\nto\ndetail\nin\nthe\nfigure.\nSome key\nreviews\nare\nof\nthe\nMAST\nproposed\nground\noperations(GOR)\nand flight\noperations(FOR)\nto\nensure\nthat\nthose\nJSC persons\nthat\ndevelop\nthe\ndetails\nfor\nthe\norbital\noperations\nthoroughly\nunderstand\nthe\nexperiment\noperations.\nA detailed\nintegration\nreadiness\nreview(IRR)\nis\nheld\njust\nbefore\nthe\nMFS is\nshipped\nto\nKSC.\nA Space\nShuttle\nflight\noperations\nreview(SSFOR)\nis\nheld\nto ensure\nthat\nall\nexperiment\nprocedures,\ntimelines,\ncrew\nchecklists,\netc.,\nare\nready\nfor\nthe\nflight.\nThe\nprocess\nof\nsafety\ncertification\nhas already\nbeen\ndescribed\nand only\nthe\nphased\nsafety\nreviews\nare\nshown\nhere.\nThe\nlast\nthing\nis\nthe\nflight\nreadiness\nreview(FRR)\njust\nbefore\nflight.\n\n307\n\nMAJOR MILESTONES\n19e6\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nFOR downlinked\nin\nreal\ntime.\nData\nwould\nbe\nrecorded\ncontinuously\nfrom\nsome\npoint\nduring\nquiescence\nto the\nobserved\nend of\nthe\nfree-decay\nperiod.\nThese\ndata\nperiods\nmay\nvary\nfrom\ntimes\nas short\nas 15 minutes\nto as much\nas 45 minutes.\nThere\nwill\nbe an option\navailable\nto\ninitiate\nan active\ndamping\nalgorithm\nafter\nsome TBD number\nof\ncycles\nof\nthe\ndynamic\nresponse.\nThis\nwill\nfacilitate\nreduction\nof\nthe\ntime\nneeded\nfor\nthe\ntotal\nMAST 30\n\n6rn\n\n24\n13m\n\n2\n-_BAY\n\n12\n\n13m\n\n3\n\n3\n\n10\n\n19\n\nFigure\n\n5\n\n11\n\n313\n\nORBITAL\n\nDATA\n\nPATHS\n\nThe planned\ndata\nflow\nduring\nthe\nMAST 1 flight\nexperiment\nis\nshown\nin\nFigure\n12.\nThis\nis\nnot\na complete\ndepiction\nof\nevery\ninstrument\nor\ninterface\nthat\nwill\nbe needed\nduring\nthe\nflight.\nRather\nit\nonly\nrepresents\nthe\nvarious\npaths\nthat\ndata\nwill\nfollow\nin\nreaching\nthe\nappropriate\nuser\nor\nrepository.\nThere\nare\ntwo\ndifferent\ndata\nrates\nrepresented:\na low\ndata\nrate\nand\na high\ndata\nrate.\nThe low\nrate\ndata\nis\ntransferred\nfrom\nthe\nmodular\ndistributed\ninstrumentation\nsystem\n(MDIS)\ncomputer\nto the\nsmart/flex\nmodulator/demodulator\n(SFMDM).\nThis\ndata\nis\na subset\nof\nthe\nhigh\nrate\ndata\nand\nconsists\nprimarily\nof\nhousekeeping\ndata\nand\nthe\nappropriate\nsafety\ndata\nthat\nmust\nbe monitored\nby\nthe\ncrew\nin\nthe\norbiter\naft\nflight\ndeck.\nLow rate\ndata\ntakes\ntwo\npaths\nfrom\nthe\nSFMDM.\nIt\nis\nbe directed\nvia\nthe\npayload\ndata\ninterleaver\n(PDI)\nto the\nS-band\ndownlink\nand\neventually\nreaches\nthe\nHuntsville\noperations\nsupport\ncenter\n(HOSC).\nIn the\nHOSC,\ndata\ncan be stripped\nout\nand\nrouted\nto\nappropriate\ndisplay\ninstruments\nfor\nmonitoring\nby the\nflight\nsupport\nengineering\npersonnel.\nLow rate\ndata\nis\nalso\npassed\nto\nthe\ndata\ndisplay\nand\ncontrol\nunit\n(DDCU)\nwhere\nit\nis\ndisplayed\nin\npredefined\nscreens\nthat\nare\nselectable\nby the\ncrew.\nThe\nprimary\ninterface\nbetween\nthe\ncrew\nand the\nexperiment\nis\nthrough\nthis\nDDCU-SFMDM-MDIS\npath.\nThe crew\nissues\nappropriate\ncommands\nto\noperate\nthe\nexperiment\noperations\nand\ntests\nand monitor\nthe\nfeedback\nfor\nappropriate\nresponses\nor progress.\nMore\ndetail\non this\nprocess\ncan\nbe found\nin\nthe\nprevious\npaper\nby Mr.\nRonald\nTalcott.\nThe high\nrate\ndata\nis\ntransferred\ndirectly\nfrom\nthe\nMDIS\ncomputer\nto\nthe\nhigh\nrate\nmultiplexer\n(HRM).\nThis\ndata\nis\nessentially\nevery\ndata\nsample\ntaken\nby the\nMDIS\nduring\nthe\nexpriment\nprocess.\nThe HRM routes\ndata\nto\nthe\nKu-band\ndownlink\nand\nto\nthe\nhigh\ndata\nrate\nrecorder.\nThe high\ndata\nrate\nrecorder\n(HDRR)\nrecords\nall\ndata\ntaken\nby the\nMDIS.\nThe data\nwill\nbe periodically\ndumped\nvia\nthe\nHRM/Ku\nband\nand\nsatellite\nlinks\nto\nthe\nGoddard\nSpace\nFlight\nCenter\'s\n(GSFC)\nsatellite\nnetwork\ndata\nreceiving\nand\nrecording\nfacilities.\nMaster\ntapes\nof all\norbital\ndata\nwill\nbe made\nfor\narchives\nand\nfor\nuse\nby the\nscience\nteams\nfor\npostflight\ndata\nanalyses.\nThe\nhigh\nrate\ndata\nwill\nalso\nbe downlinked\nin\nreal\ntime\nfor\nsome\nof\nthe\nearlier\nbeam deployments\nand\ndynamic\ntests.\nThis\nrealtime\ndownlink\nwill\nbe routed\nvia\nsatellite\nlinks\nto the\nHOSC\nwhere\nsome combination\nof\nHOSC instrumentation\nand\nexperiment\nground\nsupport\nequipment\n(GSE)\nwill\nbe used\nto\nprovide\nboth\nreal\ntime\ndisplay\nof\nselected\ndata\nand quick-look\nrecordings\nof\nall\ndownlinked\ndata.\nThe science\nteam\nwill\nuse\nthese\nquick-look\nrecordings\nto\ndo preliminary\nanalyses\nto verify\nthe\nbeam\nresponses\nas compared\nto\npreflight\npredictions.\nThis\nprocess\nwill\ncontinue\novernight\nfor\neach\ntest\nperformed\nto\nprovide\nconfirmation\nof\nreadiness\nto proceed\nwith\nthe\nnext\ndays\ntests.\nThe detailed\ndefinition\nof\nthese\ndownlink\npaths,\ndata\nformats,\nand\ndisplay/analysis\nprocesses\nis\nstill\nin\nprogress.\n\n314\n\nREAL-TIME\nMD__m\n\nLO-RATEDATA\n\n----\'---I\nY\n\n__ r\'_\n\n: DISTIIRBANCE SIIPPRESSION\n\no\n\nDISTRIRIITED\n\no\n\nAnAPTIVE CONTROL\n\nCONTROL\n\nHARDWAREDEVELOPMENT\nFigure\n\n3\n323\n\nDESIRED\n\nCONTENT\n\nOF\n\nEXPERIMENT\n\nPACKAGE\n\nThe COFS-I GI experiments\npackage\nshould\nbe an integrated\ncombination\nof\non-ground\nand on-orbit\nexperiments\nwhich\nbest supports\nthe development\nand validation of Controls/Structures/Interaction\n(CSI) technology.\nThe package\nshould\ninclude\nexperiments\nfrom each of the previously\ndiscussed\ncategories\nwith emphasis\non structural\ncharacterization\nexperiments\nand control\nlaw evaluation\nexperiments.\nGround-based\nsive since\n\nsystem\nidentification\nflight\nquality\nsoftware\n\nis technically\ncritical\nand\nis not required.\nTherefore,\n\nrelatively\ninexpena large number\nof\n\nthese ground-based\nsystem\nidentification\nexperiments\nis justified.\nOther structural\ncharacterization\nexperiments\nand the control\nlaw evaluation\nexperiments\nare technically\ncritical\nalso, but the number\nof these experiments\nwhich can be conducted\non-orblt\nis limited\nby the on-orbit\ntest time available\nand the cost of developing\nthe associated\nflight\nsoftware.\nEmphasis\nof these experiments\ncan be achieved\nby\ninitially\nselecting\na relatively\nlarge number\nof investigations\nwith subsequent\ncompetitive\nreduction\nto a relatively\nsmall number\nof investigations\nfor actual\non-orbit\nevaluation.\nCompetitive\nreduction\nis achieved\nby ground-based\nperformance\nevaluations\nusing a high-fidelity\nsimulation\nof Pyle\nDr. Raymond Montgomery\nNASA Langley\nResearch\nCenter\nHampton,\nVirginia\n\nFirst\n\nNASA/DOD CSI Technology\nConference\nNorfolk,\nVirginia\nNovember 18-21,\n1986\n\nPRECEDING\n\npAGEBLANK\n\nNOT\n\nFILMED\n327\n\nINTRODUCTION\nNASA has initiated a program to focus the development of Controls/Structures\nInteraction (CSI) technologies for the next generation of large, flexible spacecraft.\nThis program, called "Control Of Flexible Structures (COFS)", will address critical\ntechnologies in the areas of structural dynamics and controls through comprehensive\nground and flight testing of the fundamental elements of large, flexible space\nstructures.\nThe COFS program is a series of projects with the objective of\ndeveloping a validated CSI technology data base for the suppression of inherent\ndynamic responses and avoidance of the undesirable\ninteraction between flexible\nstructures and controls in these spacecraft.\nThe following paper discusses the\ncurrent development plans for the second in this series of projects, COFS II,\nThree-Dimensional\nDynamics and Controls.\n\nCONTROL\n\nCOFS II\n3-D\n\nDYNAMICS AND\nCONTROLS\nTECHNOLOGIES\n\nCOFS II\nOF FLEXIBLE STRUCTURES\n\nNASA/DOD\nCONTROLS/STRUCTURES\nINTERACTION\nTECHNOLOGY\nCONFERENCE\nNOVEMBER\n\n328\n\n20,\n\n1986\n\nPROJECT OBJECTIVES\nCOFS II is the natural\nextension\nof the beam dynamics\nand controls\nexperiments\nundertaken\nin the initial\nproject\nof the COFS program\nand will\nadvance\nthe technology\ndata base into\nthe areas of three-dimensional,\nmultibody\nconfigurations.\nThe\nCOFS II project\nwill\nbuild\non the ground\nand flight\ntechnologies\ndeveloped\nin COFS I\nand will\nimprove\non the development\nof a multi-input/output\ncontrol\nsystem for the\nsuppression\nof undesired\ndisturbances\nin these large,\nflexible\nspacecraft.\nSpecifically,\nCOFS II will\nevaluate\nprediction\ntechniques\nused to define\nthe dynamic\nand control\ncharacteristics\nof space structures\nthrough\na comparison\nof theoretical,\nground\ntest\nand on-orbit\ntest\ndata.\nThe project\nwill\ndevelop\nadvanced\ncontrol\ntechniques\nand on-orbit\nvalidation\nprocedures\nwhich will\nlead to optimized\nspacecraft\nperformance\nin minimal\non-orbit\ntest\ntime.\nThe COFS II project\ninvolves\nthe space structures\nand controls\ncommunity\nfrom universities,\nindustry\nand government\nthrough\na formal\nGuest Investigator\nProgram\nin which\nresearchers\nare selected\nto participate\nin all\nprogram\nactivity\nphases.\n\nDEVELOP\n\nAND EVALUATE\n\nIN MODELING\n3-D\n\nTHE METHODOLOGIES\n\nAND CONTROLLING\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nLARGE,\n\nINVOLVED\n\nFLEXIBLE,\n\nIN SPACE\n\n329\n\nCOFS WORKSHOP RESULTS\nA Controls/Structures\nInteraction\n(CSl)\nworkshop\nwas held at NASA Langley\nResearch\nCenter\non August\n27-28,\n1985.\nThe purpose\nof the workshop\nwas to review\nproposed\nCOFS I, Beam Dynamics\nand Controls\nproject,\nthe COFS II,\nThree-Dimensional\nDynamics\nand Control\nproject\nand the methods\nof interacting\nthe CSI community\nin\nexperimental\nphases of each of these\nprojects.\n\nthe\nthe\n\nMembers of the CSI community\nmade up of individuals\nfrom major\nuniversities,\nrelated\nindustries\nand government\nresearch\norganizations\nwere asked to define\nmajor\ntechnology\ndeficiencies\nfor the next generation\nof large\nspace structures.\nNine\ncritical\ntechnology\nneeds were identified\nwhich\nconcentrated\non the control\nof the\ndynamic\ninteraction\nbetween\nthe parts\nof a multibody\nspace structure.\nThe workshop\nattendees\nwere also asked to identify\nviable\nflight\nconfigurations\nwhich would provide\nthe basis\nfor critical\ntechnology\ndevelopment.\nMost panels\nagreed\nthat\nthe configuration\nwhich\nincluded\nmultibody\nbeams attached\nby gimbals\nto\na "realistic\nantenna"\nwould provide\nthe capability\nfor developing\nthe major\nportion\nof the critical\ntechnologies.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DEFINED CRITICAL\nCONTROLS\nAND STRUCTURES\nTECHNOLOGY\nREQUIREMENTS\nFOR 3-D SPACE\nEXPERIMENTS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 IDENTIFIED\nON-ORBIT\n\n330\n\n3-D CONFIGURATION\nTESTING\n\nFOR GROUND\n\nAND\n\nWORKSHOP CONFIGURATION/TECHNOLOGIES\nThe recommended workshop\nconfiguration\nshown in the figure\nwas generated\nutilizing\nthe COFS I Mast beam, a deployable\nboom attached\nto the tip\nof the Mast by\nmeans of a gimbal,\nand a real\nantenna\nattached\nto the boom by means of a gimbal.\nThe\nboom modal characteristics\nshould\nbe capable\nof combining\nwith\nthe structural\nmodes\nof the Mast to provide\ninteractive\nmodal characteristics\nof research\ninterest.\nThe\nantenna\nshould\nhave a mesh surface\nwith\nthe capability\nof changing\nthe surface\nshape\nto improve\nthe antenna\nperformance.\nThe critical\ntechnology\nneeds for the three-dimensional\nconfiguration\nare\nprimarily\nrelated\nto the stabilization\nof the multibody\nstructure\nand control\nof\nthat\nstructure\nduring\nrepositioning.\nThe primary\ndynamic\ncharacterization\nof the\nflight\nsystem will\nprovide\nthe basis\nfor developing\ncontrol\nmethodologies\nto\nstabilize\nthe structure,\nminimize\nthe dynamic disturbances\ncaused by deployment,\nmaneuvering\nand articulation\nof the secondary\nboom or antenna,\nand pointing\nthe\nantenna\nat a prescribed\nlocation.\nThe successful\ndevelopment\nand validation\nof\nthese\nprimary\ntechnologies\nwill\npermit\nfurther\ndevelopment\nof adaptive\ncontrol\ntechniques\nfor more complex\nconfigurations.\n\nCRITICAL\n\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nNEEDS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPOINTING/AIMING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nARTICULATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSLEWING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSHAPE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMANEUVER\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nALIGNMENT\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSYSTEMS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSTRUCTURAL\n\nEVALUATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDEPLOYMENT\n\nCHARACTERIZATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nVIBRATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nADAPTIVE\n\nCONTROL\nLOAD\n\nIDENTIFICATION\n\nCONTROL\nCONTROL\n\n331\n\nCOFS II\nEstimates\n\nof the COFS\n\nCONFIGURATION\n\nII project\n\ncosts\n\nCOST OPTIONS\n\nwere\n\ncompleted\n\nfor various\n\ntypes\n\nof\n\nlarge, flexible, three-dimensional\nconfigurations\nwhich would satisfy the critical\ntechnology recommendation of the workshop.\nThe results of this exercise are shown on\nthe figure with increasing project costs as the vertical axis and increasing\ntechnology return as the horizontal axis.\nThe COFS I project, Beam Dynamics and\nControl, was shown as a bench mark of the relative project development\ncosts.\nObviously, as the number of technologies and the complexity of the configuration\nincreases, the project costs also increase.\nThe workshop configuration\nprovides the\nfull development and validation of the primary technologies;\nhowever, the very high\ndesign, fabrication and development test costs for this concept preclude its\nutilization as the government proposed configuration.\nHowever, most of the\ntechnologies can be developed and validated through the testing of a simplified\nversion of the workshop configuration\npictured in the major contribution\nof the COFS II project\nwill\nbe the development\nand\nvalidation\nof a hybrid\ntest\ntechnique\nfor the ground\ntesting\nof very large,\nflexible,\n3-D space structures.\nThe structural\ncharacterization\nof the flight\nsystem will\nbe\naccomplished\nby combining\nthe smaller\nhardware\ncomponents\nsuch as the baseline\nadapter\nand two-degree-of-freedom\ngimbal\nwith\nanalytical\nsimulations\nof the Mast and\nhoop-column\nantenna.\nThis\nhybrid\nsystem will\nsimulate\nthe actual\nflight\nsystem and\nprovide\nthe capability\nto validate\nexperimental\ncontrol\nalgorithms,\nverify\nnew\ncontrol\nsynthesis\ntechniques,\nand to develop\nadaptive\ncontrol\nconcepts.\nThe\nestablishment\nof this\ntesting\ntechnique\nthrough\nthe COFS II project\nwill\nbe highly\ninstrumental\nin the ground\nvalidation\nand on-orbit\nsuccess\nof future\nspacecraft\nmissions.\n\nDEFINITION:\n\nHYBRID SYSTEM\nCOMPUTER\n\n- COMBINATION\n\nSIMULATION\n\nOF REAL HARDWARE\n\nOF LARGE COMPONENTS\n\nAND\n\nAND/OR\n\nSUBASSEMBLIES\n\nAPPROACH:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ACCOMPLISH\n\nLARGE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DEVELOP COMPUTER\nSUBASSEMBLIES\n\nCOMPONENT\n\n& SUBASSEMBLY\n\nSIMULATION\n\nOF COMPONENTS\n\nGROUND TESTS\nAND/OR\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 COMBINE SMALL COMPONENT\nHARDWARE WITH COMPUTER\nSIMULATIONS OF LARGE HARDWARE\nTO CREATE FLIGHT\nSYSTEM SIMULATOR\n\xe2\x80\xa2 UTILIZE\n\nTEST\n\nFLIGHT\n\nSYSTEM\n\nTO VERIFY\n\nCONTROL\n\nALGORITHMS\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DETERMINE DYNAMIC\nCOUPLED SYSTEM\n\xe2\x80\xa2 VERIFY GI CONTROL\n\xe2\x80\xa2 VALIDATE\n\n334\n\nSIMULATOR\n\nHYBRID\n\nAND CONTROL\nAND COMPARE\nALGORITHMS\n\nTEST APPROACH\n\nCHARACTERISTICS\nOF\nTO FLT SYS PREDICTIONS\n\nON FLIGHT SYSTEM\nFOR LARGE SPACE\n\nSTRUCTURES\n\nCOFS II\n\nHYBRID TEST METHOD\n\nThe development\nmethod for\nthe COFS II hybrid\ntest\ntechnique\nis depicted\nin the\nfollowing\nfigure.\nThe structural\ncharacteristics\nof the very\nlarge\npieces\nof the\nCOFS II flight\nsystem will\nbe obtained\nfrom a combination\nof individual\nground\nand/or\non-orbit\ntesting\nof these\nstructures.\nAnalytical\nsimulations\nof these\nstructures\nwill\nbe generated\nutilizing\nknowledge\ngenerated\nfrom basic\nResearch\nand Technology\ndevelopment\nwithin\nthe CSI community\nand in-house\nLangley\nexpertise.\nThese\nsimulations\nof the large\nstructures\nwill\nbe combined with\nthe smaller\narticulation\nhardware\nto provide\na combination\nof hardware\nand software\nwhich\nsimulates\nthe\ndynamic\ncharacteristics\nof the flight\nsystem in the on-orbit\nenvironment.\nThe\nresultant\nhigh\nand validating\n\nfidelity\ndeveloped\n\nCOMPONENT\n\nAND\n\nsimulation\ncontrol\n\nshould\nthen\nmethodologies\n\nprovide\nthe capability\nprior\nto actual\nflight\n\nof verifying\nvalidation.\n\nSUBASSEMBLY\n\nTESTING\n\nGROUND\n\nGROUND\n\nTEST\n\nBASIC\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCONTROL\nCONCEPTS\n\nTEST\n\nDATA\n\n/\n\nDATA\n\nR&T\n\nEXPERIENCE\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMODELING\n\n_------_---\'_\'_\'--\'--_\n\xe2\x80\xa2SYSTEM\nID\n\nHI-FI\n\nSYSTEM\n\nSIMULATION\n\n335\n\nFLIGHT\n\nEXPERIMENT OBJECTIVES\n\nThe baseline\nCOFS II configuration\nwill\nprovide\nthe opportunity\nof developing\na\nbetter\nunderstanding\nof the control/structural\ninteraction\ncharacteristics\nof a\nmultibody\nspacecraft\nin a zero-g\nenvironment.\nTechniques\nfor evaluating\ndeployment,\nstructural\ndynamics,\nsystems\nidentification\nand possible\nprocedures\nfor\nfault\ndetection,\nisolation\nand reorganization\nof sensor\nfaults\n(FDIR)\nwill\nbe\nexamined\non the flight\nsystem during\nflight.\nUpon achieving\na stable\nplatform\nthrough\nalignment\nand vibration\ncontrol,\nthe gimbal\nwill\nbe used to articulate\nthe\nantenna\nand examine pointing,\nslewing\nand articulation\ncontrol\nalgorithms\npreviously\ndeveloped\nand verified\non the ground.\nQuasi-static\nshape control\nof one quadrant\nof\nthe antenna\nwill\nbe demonstrated\nalong\nwith\ncontrol\nat the hoop.\nManeuver\nload\ncontrol\nutilizing\ninputs\nfrom the shuttle\nwill\nbe developed\nand adaptive\ncontrol\ntechniques\nfor the complex\nsystem will\nbe evaluated.\nIn addition,\nthe flight\nresults\nwill\nbe used to establish\nthe viability\nof the hybrid\ntesting\ntechniques\nused in\npredicting\non-orbit\nperformance\nof a large,\nflexible,\nand complex\nstructure.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DEVELOP\n\nAND\n\nVALIDATE\n\nADVANCED\n\nMETHODOLOGIES\nON A LARGE,\nIN A ZERO-G\nENVIRONMENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIDENTIFY\n\nAND\n\nCHARACTERIZE\n\nLARGE, 3-D FLEXIBLE\nENVIRONMENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CORRELATE\nFLIGHT\nAND ANALYTICAL\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nESTABLISH\nPREDICTING\nFLEXIBLE,\n\n336\n\n3=D\n\nTHE\n\nSTRUCTURE\n\nOF HYBRID\n\nON-ORBIT\nCOMPLEX\n\nFLEXIBLE\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nTEST RESULTS\nPREDICTION\n\nVIABILITY\nAND\n\nCONTROL\n\nOF A\n\nIN A ZERO-G\n\nWITH\n\nTESTING\n\nPERFORMANCE\nSYSTEMS\n\nSTRUCTURE\n\nGROUND\n\nTEST\n\nTECHNIQUES\nOF LARGE,\n\nFOR\n\nSTRUCTURAL EVALUATION & SYSTEMS IDENTIFICATION\nThe individual\ntechnology\ndevelopment\nand validation\napproaches\nwill\nbe shown in\nthe next\nseries\nof figures.\nDuring\nthe initial\ndeployment\nof the flight\nexperiment,\nits\ndynamic\ncharacteristics\nwill\nbe evaluated\nand compared\nto the predicted\nsystem\ncharacteristics\ngenerated\nfrom analytical\nmodels and the hybrid\ntest\nsystem.\nThis\ncharacterization\nwill\nprovide\nthe validation\nof the predicted\nflight\nsystem dynamics\nand interaction\nand verify\nthat\nthe control\nalgorithms\ndeveloped\non the ground\nwill\nprovide\nthe damping and stabilization\nrequired\nfor the flight\nexperiments.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, CHARACTERIZE\nFLI6flT\nSYSTEH\n, EVALUATE TECHNIQUES\nPREDICTION\nAPPROACH:\n, DEPLOY t ANTENNA\nDEAN\n, DETERNINE SYSTEH\nFLI6HT\nDYNAHiC\nCHARACTERISTICS\n, COHPARE 6ROUflD\nWITH\nRESULTS\nt ANALYTICAL\nPREDICTIONS\n\n337\n\nARTICULATION\n\n& POINTING\n\nTECHNOLOGIES\n\nTwo of the key control\ntechnologies\nrequiring\ndevelopment\nand validation\nfor\nthe\nnext generation\nlarge\nspace structure\nare controlled\narticulation\nand highly\naccurate\npointing\nof the antenna-like\nstructure.\nVarious\ntechniques\nwill\nbe developed\nby the\nGuest Investigators\nwhich will\nstabilize\nthe beam structure,\narticulate\nthe antenna\nby the two-degree-of-freedom\ngimbal\nand point\nit\nin a prescribed\nlocation.\nThe\ncontrol\nalgorithms\ngenerated\nfor this\npurpose\nwill\nbe verified\nwith\nthe hybrid\nsimulator\nand then validated\non the flight\nsystem\nin orbit\non the shuttle.\nThe\nflight\nresults\nwill\nthen be utilized\nto upgrade\nthe control\ntechniques\nto provide\na\nmore efficient\nmethod of articulation\nand pointing\nof the antenna\nsurface.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, DEVELOP\nANTENNA\nARTICULATION\nTECHNIQUES\n, DEMONSTRATE\nSTABLE\nPOINTING\nWITH ANTENNA\n\nAPPROACH:\n, SLEWANTENNA\nWITH2 DOFGIIJBAL\n, USECONTROL\nALGORITHMSO\nT\nSTABILIZEFLIGHTSTRUCTURE\n. EVALUATE\nANTENNA\nSTABILITYZ\nPOINTING\n\n338\n\nSLEWING & MANEUVER LOAD CONTROL TECHNOLOGIES\nWhen the COFS II flight\nsystem is fully\ndeployed,\nit will\nextend\nbetween\n30 and\n60 meters\nfrom the shuttle\nbay with\nthe tip\nof the antenna\noffset\nfrom the beam up to\n20 meters.\nThe beam, antenna\nand gimbal\nare subjected\nto a significant\nmaneuver\nload\nduring\na sustained\nvernier\nreaction\ncontrol\nsystem firing.\nThe articulation\nof the\nantenna\nby the gimbal\nwill\nalso\ninduce\na slewing\nmotion\ninto\nthe column support\nof\nthe antenna\nand a torsional\nloading\non the beam.\nControl\ntechniques\nto alleviate\nthese\nloads,\ndampen the disturbances\ncaused by maneuver\nor slewing,\nand stabilize\nthe\nflight\nsystem will\nbe developed,\nverified\nthrough\nthe ground\nhybrid\nsimulator\nand\nvalidated\non the baseline\nflight\nsystem during\nthe on-orbit\nexperimentation.\nThese\ncontrol\nmethods will\nbe further\nupgraded\nbased on the on-orbit\ntest\nresults.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, ALLEVIATE\nSHUTTLE\nWANEUVER\nLOADS\nNININIZE\nDYNAWIC\nDISTURBANCES\nFRON\nINDUCED\nNOTION\n\nAPPROACH:\n, CHANGE\nSHUTTLE\nATTITUDE\nOR\nSLEW\nANTENNA\n, EVALUATE SYSTEW\nFLIGHT\nDYNANICS\n, USE\nCONTROL\nTECHNIQUES\nTO\nNINIWIZE\nDISTURBANCES\n\n339\n\nSHAPE CONTROL & ALIGNMENT TECHNOLOGIES\nA means of determining\nthe precise\nshape of the antenna\nsurface\nmesh is\nrequired\nin order\nto improve\nthe efficiency\nof very\nlarge,\nflexible\nantennas\nin\nspace.\nThe COFS II baseline\nconfiguration\nwill\nprovide\nthe mechanism\nfor controlling\nthe surface\nshape of one quadrant\nof the antenna\nand the antenna\nhoop through\nindividual\nactuators\nand sensors\nattached\nto the lanyards\n(hoop/surface\nmesh chords).\nPrecise\nlocation\nof the surface\nmesh, the hoop and the beam structure\nthat\nthe\nantenna\nis attached\nto,\nwill\nbe attained\nthrough\noptical/digital\nsensors.\nQuasi-static\ncontrol\nmethodologies\nwill\nbe developed,\nverified\nand validated\nduring\nthe on-orbit\ntesting\nof the baseline\nflight\nsystem.\nIt is hoped to achieve\ntotal\nreshaping\nof one\nquadrant\nof the antenna\nwithin\nfive\nminutes\nand to within\n0.2 millimeters\nof a\npredetermined\nshape.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, DEVELOP\nPRECISE\nBEAM\nALIGNMENT\n| CONTROL\nTECHNIQUES\n, IMPROVE\nANTENNA\nEFFICIENCY\nTHROUGH\nCONTROL\nOFSURFACE\nMESH\n\nAPPROACH:\n, STABILIZE AND\nBEAM ANTENNA\nSTRUCTURE\n, ALIGN\nHOOP sensor/actuator\nmalfunctions\nduring\nlong periods\nof\nspace utilization\nof these\nvery large\nstructures,\nmethods must be developed\nto\nreorganize\nthe sensors\nand actuators\nsuch that\nalternate\ncontrol\ntechniques\ncan be\napplied\nto manipulate\nor stabilize\nthe structures.\nThe COFS II baseline\nsensors\nand\nactuators\nwill\nhave the capability\nof being\nutilized\nin various\ngroupings\nin order\nto\nunderstand\nand develop\nthe techniques\nrequired\nto verify\nthe FDIR technology.\nSimulated\nmalfunctions\nof sensors/alternators\nwill\nbe achieved\nby ignoring\nsignals\nfrom selected\nsensors\nor actuator\nand reorganizing\nthe information\nto perform\na\nspecific\ncontrol\nfunction\nduring\nthe flight\ntest experimentation.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, DEVELOP\nFAULT\nDETECTION\nTECHNIQUES\n, EVALUATE\nISOLATION LGORITHMS\nA\n, VALIDATE EORGANIZATION\nR\nMETHODS\n\nAPPROACH:\n, SINULATE NEXPERIMENT\nA\nSENSOR\nFAULT\n,
