TECHNOLOGY\n\nThe goal of the controls\ncommunity\nis to develop\ntechniques\nwhich automatically\ncompensate\nfor all\nvariations\nin the predicted\ncharacteristics\nof a large\nspace\nstructure,\nanalyze\nthe characteristics\nof dynamic\ninputs\nto the structure\nand\neliminate\nvibrations,\nlow frequency\ndynamics\nand any other\ndestabilization\nfactors.\nThe COFS II baseline\nconfiguration\nwill\nbe utilized\nto evaluate\npreliminary\nconcepts\nfor adaptive\ncontrols\nwhich may lead to the anticipated\ngoal.\nControl\nalgorithms\ndeveloped\nby the Guest Investigators\nwill\nbe verified\non the hybrid\nsimulator\nand\nvalidated\non-orbit\nafter\npreviously\ndiscussed\ntechnologies\nhave been fully\ndeveloped\nand validated.\n\nOBJECTIVES:\n, ESTABLISH\nk CONTROL\nMETHOD\nTO\nACCOMMODATE\nVARIATIONS\nIN\nSYSTEM\nDYNAMICS UNCERTAINTIES\nAND\nINSYSTEM\nPERFORMANCE\n\nAPPROACH:\n, DEVELOP\nSYSTEII\nANALYTICAL\nUODEL8\n, SCHEDULE\n\nThe COFS II flight\nhardware\ndevelopment\ncontract\nwill\nbe awarded early\nin 1989.\nThis\ncontract\nwill\ninclude\nthe design,\nfabrication,\nand testing\nof the flight\nhardware\nto be mounted\nin the shuttle\nbay for on-orbit\ntesting\nand the prototype\nhardware\nrequired\nto construct\nthe hybrid\ntest\nsystem.\nA preliminary\nRequest\nFor Proposal\n(RFP) which\nidentifies\nthe primary\ntechnology\ngoals,\nsystem specifications\nand\nspecific\nproject\nobjectives\nwill\nbe released\napproximately\n12 months prior\nto the\nofficial\nRFP.\nThis will\nallow\ncontractors\nadequate\ntime to evaluate\nalternate\nhardware\nconcepts\nwhich might\nprovide\nequivalent\nor better\ntechnology\nreturn\nto the\nCSl community\nfor the government\ninvestment.\nThe current\nflight\ndates\nfor\nthe COFS\nII experiments\nare expected to occur in late 1993 and 1994.\nThe development\nof the hybrid simulator of the COFS II flight system will begin\nshortly after the award of the hardware development contract.\nSome of the large\ncomponent and subassembly\ntests will be conducted within the Base R & T programs and\nthe COFS I project prior to the initiation of the ground test task.\nThe simulator\nwill be constructed\nat NASA Langley and will be available to the hardware and\nsoftware contractors and the Guest Investigators.\nThe software development contract will be awarded shortly after the hardware\ndevelopment contract and will provide the operational, baseline applications\nand\nGuest Investigator software development, the simulations of the large component\ndynamics to be used in the hybrid test system, and all required flight coding.\nThe Guest Investigator\n(GI) contracts will be initiated through the same\nprocedures discussed for the COFS I project and will begin shortly after the award of\nthe hardware development contract.\nSeparate GI contracts will be awarded for each of\nthe COFS II flights.\n\nMAJOR\n\nTASKS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HARDWARE\nDEVELOPMENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 system to the shuttle payload\nintegration facility at KSC. The following options will be acceptable approaches for\nproviding the required COFS II flight system:\n(1) Baseline Confiquration\n- The contractor will refurbish and modify the\ngovernment furnished hoop-column antenna and attach it to the COFS I beam by means\na two-degree-of-freedom\ngimbal to provide the required technology development\ncapability.\n\nof\n\n(2) Contractor Hardware - The contractor or a team of contractors may propose an\nalternate flight system utilizing contractor provided hardware which will satisfy the\ntechnical requirements of the COFS II project and provide at a minimum, equivalent\ntechnology development capability as the COFS II baseline configuration.\n(3) Combined Contractor, Government Furnished Equipment - The contractor or team\nof contractors may propose an alternate flight system utilizing a combination of\ncontractor provided flight hardware and government furnished equipment such as the\nhoop-column antenna or COFS I Mast.\nThe resultant flight system must satisfy the\ntechnical requirements of the COFS II project and provide at the minimum, equivalent\ntechnology development capability as the COFS II baseline configuration.\nThe cost of options (2) or (3) must\nand funding profile or be cost shared\n\nremain within the available\nby the contractor(s).\n\ncontractual\n\nfunding\n\nRFP WILL INCLUDE:\n- BASELINE\n- FUNDING\nRFP WILL complex\nand ambitious\nundertaking\nwhich will\naddress\nseveral\ncritical\ntechnology\nareas.\nAmong them are modeling,\nstructural\ndynamics,\ncontrols,\nand ground\ntesting\nissues\nwhich are not only germane to this\neffort,\nbut to\nother\nlarge\nspace structure\nprograms\nbeing\ncontemplated.\nThis\neffort\nrequires\nthe\nearly\nintegration\nof controls\nand structural\ndynamics\nconsiderations\nin order\nto\nachieve\nmission\nsuccess.\nSeveral\ntechnology\nadvances must be achieved\nin the areas\nof system modelling,\ncontrol\nsynthesis\nand methodology,\nsensor/actuator\ndevelopment,\nand ground\ntesting\ntechniques\nfor system evaluation\nand on-orbit\nperformance\nprediction\nand verification.\nThis project\noffers\na unique\nopportunity\nfor the\nintegration\nof several\ndisciplines\nto produce\ntechnology\nadvances\nwhich will\nbenefit\nmany future\nprograms.\nIn addition,\nthe opportunities\navailable\nto participate\nin the\nvarious\nlevels\nin the phases of this\nproject,\ne.g.,\nanalytical\ndevelopment\nand\nmodelling,\nground\ntesting,\nand flight\ntesting,\npermit\nfor the involvement\nof a\nsignificant\nnumber of interested\nresearchers\nand organizations\nfrom government,\nuniversities\nand industry.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 OPPORTUNITIES\n\nFOR RESEARCH\n\nIN deals\nwith\ndeveloping\nmultibody\ndynamics\nand control\ntechnology\nfor large\nspace structures.\nIt differs\nfrom COFS I & II in two respects.\nFirst,\nit\naddresses\na more complex\nclass\nof\nstructure,\nand second it\nis basically\na scale\nmodel ground\ntest\nand analysis\nprogram\nwhile\nCOFS I & II feature\nshuttle\nflight\nexperiments.\nThe specific\ntechnology\nthrusts\nwithin\nCOFS III\nare model sensitivities,\ntest\nmethods,\nanalysis\nvalidation,\nsystems\nidentification,\nand vibration\nsuppression.\n\nCOFS\n\nCOFS TECHNICAL APPROACH\n\nThe objective\nof COFS III\nis to develop\na verified\ncapability\nfor predicting\nthe\nstructural\ndynamics\nbehavior\nof multibodied,\njoint\ndominated\narticualted,\nflexible\nspace structures,\nwhich\nare too large\nand heavy to be dynamically\ntested,\nwhen fully\nmated,\nin earth\'s\none-g environment.\nThe technical\napproach\nfor achieving\nthis\ncapability\nis to demonstrate\nthat\na\ncombination\nof dynamically\nscaled\nmodel tests\nand theoretical\nanalyses\ncan provide\na\ncredible\nmethod for predicting\nthe dynamics\nbehavior\nof the full-scale\nstructure\nand\nalso provide\na means for evaluating\nvibration\ncontrol\ntechniques.\n\nOBJECTIVE:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 VERIFIED\nCAPABILITY\nFOR STRUCTURAL\nPREDICTION\nAND CONTROL\nOF\nLARGE\n\nMULTI BODIED,\n\nARTICULATED,\n\nTECHNICAL\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DYNAMICALLY\n\nFLEXIBLE\n\nJOINT\nSPACE\n\nDYNAMICS\n\nDOMINATED\nSTRUCTURES\n\nAPPROACH:\nSCALED\n\nMODEL TESTS\nPLUS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 THEORETICAL\nDYNAMICS\nANALYSIS\n\n_\n\nPREDICTION\nOF\nFULL-SCALE\nDYNAMICS\n&\nVIBRATION\nCONTROL\n\n349\n\nPROJECT\n\nFOCUS\n\nCOFS Ill is a multibody dynamics and control technology project which focuses\non the Space Station structure.\nThe Space Station was selected for several reasons.\nFirst, it is the first very large operational\nstructure planned to be built in space.\nSecond, it is typical of the structures of interest.\nThird, the Space Station will\nprovide the first opportunity to obtain direct full-scale measurements\nfor\ncorrelation with model tests and analysis, thereby providing for the validation of\nthe COFS Ill objectives.\nFourth, studying the dynamics of the Space Station is the\nfirst step in understanding\nand developing the technology for the growth Space\nStation, which is one of Langley\'s roles in the overall Space Station effort.\n\nSPACE\n\nSTATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 REAL STRUCTURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TYPICAL\n\nOF STRUCTURES\n\nOF MODELS\nThe rationale\nfor using\ndynamically\nscaled models\nto help predict\nthe behavior\nof very large\nspace structures\nis shown here.\nFirst,\nmany of these\nstructures\nare too\nlarge\nand heavy to be tested\nfull\nscale.\nSecond,\nthe use of a model provides\nfor a\nsignificant\nimprovement\nin the analytical\ncapability\nfor the reasons\nshown.\nThird,\nin many instances\nthe modeling\nof local\nflexibilities\nis overlooked\nwhen substructure\nsynthesis\nmethods\nare applied.\nThis\nleads\nto errors\nor the failure\nto identify\nsignificant\nstructural\nmodes.\nFourth,\nthe model can uncover\npotential\nproblems\nthat\ncan influence\nthe design.\nFinally,\nthe investigation\nof anticipated\nflight\nmaneuvers\nand flight\nanomolies\nsuch as the degradation\nin performance\nfrom damaged,\nloose,\nor\nmissing\ntruss\nmembers can be accomplished.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMATED\nFULL-SCALE\nGRAVITY\n& DEFINITION\n\nSTUDY\n\nA scale\nmodel definition\nstudy was initiated\nin May 1986 with\nLockheed\nMissile\nand Space Company to help identify\nthe scaling\nrequirements\nfor the COFS III\nmodel.\nThe study was just\ncompleted\nin November 1986.\nSix key topics\nwere addressed.\nFirst,\nan appropriate\ndual-keel\nhybrid\nSpace Station\nconfiguration\nwas selected\nas\nthe baseline\nfor the study.\nSecond,\nthe issue\nof replication\nversus\nsimulation\nwas\ninvestigated\nfor each assembly\nphase,\nthe IOC Space Station,\nthe proposed\nSAVE flight\nexperiment,\nand the growth\nSpace Station.\nThird,\nthe manufacturability\nof scaled\njoints\nand tubes was analyzed\nat a variety\nof scale\nfactors.\nFourth,\nthe effects\nof\na candidate\nmodel suspension\nsystem were investigated.\nFifth,\nthe impact\nof facility\nconstraints\nwas evaluated.\nAnd finally,\nthe implications\nof expanding\nthe model into\na growth\nconfiguration\nwere investigated.\nThe results\nof these\nactivities\nwill\nbe\nprovided\nas soon as they are available.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 STUDY\n\nTASK\n\nTASK:\n\nINITIATED\n\nMAY\n\n86\n\nWITH\n\nLOCKHEED\n\nMSC\n\nESTABLISH\nOPTIMUM\nMODEL AVAILABILITY\nCONFIGURATION\n\nVARIATION\n\nOF REPLICA SCALING\n\nThe selection\nof the model scale\nfactor\nis a trade-off\nsince\nall\nproperties\nscale\ndifferently.\nThis chart\nshows some of these\ndifferences.\nThe curves\nrepresent\nwell\nknown theoretically\nderived\nreplica\nscaling\nlaws.\nIt is our initial\nbelief\nthat\nfor\nthe basic\ntruss\nstructure,\nreplica\nscaling\nshould be pursued\nto the maximum extent\nin\norder\nto eliminate\nas much uncertainty\nas possible.\nFor replica\nscaling,\naccelerations\nand structural\nfrequencies\nvary with\nthe inverse\nof the scale\nfactor,\nthus they\nincrease\nwith\ndecreasing\nscale\nfactor.\nOn the other\nhand, displacements\nscale\ndirectly\nwith\nthe scale\nfactor.\nSome quantities\neven vary over several\norders\nof magnitude,\nthus small\nchanges\nin scale\nfactor\ncan significantly\nalter\nthese\nquantities.\n\n10.0\nAcceleration\nstructural\n1.0\n\nPendulum\n\n_\n\nfrequency\nfrequency\n\n- 155 ft\n\nSCALE MODEL SIZE & WEIGHT VS.\nSCALE FACTOR\nDimensions\n\nSCALE\n\nit\n\nEFFECT OF SCALE FACTOR\n\nON MODEL\n\nFREQUENCY\n\nThe size of the model test facility can also place constraints\non the selection\nof the model scale factor.\nFor example the LSL, which will be 310 ft in diameter at\nthe base and 150 ft high at the center, must provide sufficient test volume and\ndimensions for not just the scale model but for the model and suspension system\ncombination.\nIn addition the suspension system should have cable lengths such that\nthe ratio of the first model structural frequency over the suspension system pendulum\nfrequency is a factor of 5 or greater.\nThis minimum frequency ratio of 5 should\ninsure adequate data quality thereby allowing the suspension system interactions to\nbe more easily identified and removed from the test results.\nThis boundary is shown\nby the vertical line on the lower figure.\nA scale factor of .25 is believed to be\nthe minimum for replica scaling such that the components can be manufactured within\nthe tolerances required while providing for their interchangeability.\nThis boundary\nis also shown on the figure.\nThus the upper right quadrant represents the solution\nspace for the model, not considering test facility constraints.\nThe diagonal line\nrepresents the constraints imposed by the LSL facility.\nThus a 1/3-scale model is\nthe largest COFS III scale model that could be tested in LSL and not violate the\nabove constraints.\n\nLSL_\n\nSHADOW\n\nSTRUCTURE\n\n. MODEL/FpENDULUM\n\n361\n\nANALYTICAL\nIn the area of analysis,\nchart.\n\nthere\n\nCHALLENGES\n\nare many challenges.\n\nSome\n\nare listed\n\non this\n\nAs was previously mentioned, the model contractor will provide modular\nanalytical models which can be configured to represent both the model and full-scale\nstation assembilies at each stage of the selected build-up process in space.\nThe\ndegree of model fidelity required for each stage will be different, with more\nemphasis on local effects being required in the early build-up stages while such\ndetail may not be required for those stages approaching the fully mated\nconfiguration.\nHow the joints will be characterized\nanalytically is another\nchallenge, as is the means for analytically accounting for nonlinear effects.\nBoundary conditions, earth conditions such as gravity effects and aerodynamic\nforces,\nand suspension system interactions created by a multiplicity of suspension cables\npose unique analytical challenges.\nOne of the main challenges will be the\nextrapolation of sub-scale results to credible full-scale predictions.\nSome preliminary analyses and a few tests have been conducted\ninsight into these issues.\nThese are presented next.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nVARYING LEVELS OF ANALYTICAL\nMODEL\nREQUIRED FOR BUILD-UP JOINT\n\nAXIAL\n\nSTIFFNESS\n\nThis chart\nshows analytically\nthe effect\nof joint\naxial\nstiffness\non the Space\nStation\ntruss\nglobal\nfrequencies.\nSpecifically\nthe variation\nin the truss\nfrequency\nwith\nthe stiffness\nratio\nis depicted.\nThis\nstiffness\nratio\nis the ratio\nof the axial\nstiffness\nof the strut\nto the axial\nstiffness\nof the joint.\nNote that\nfor\neither\nsmall\nor large\nvalues\nof the stiffness\nratio,\nthe truss\nfrequency\nis somewhat\ninsensitive\nto small\nchanges\nin this\nratio;\nhowever,\nfor intermediate\nvalues,\nsmall\nchanges\nin the stiffness\nratio\ncan lead to large\nchanges\nin the truss\nfrequency.\nThis\nimplies\nthe importance\nof accurately\ncharacterizing\nthe joint\nstiffness.\nTypically\nfor truss\nstructures,\nthe goal\nis to design\nthe joint\nto have a stiffness\nratio\nof approximately\n2 or less\nso that\nthe effective\nstrut\nstiffness,\nincluding\nthe\njoint,\nis as high as possible.\n\nEffect\n\n_\n\nSpace\n\nof additional\nmeasure of complexity\nto the\nprocess\nof designing,\ntesting,\nand analyzing\nthe COFS III\nmodel.\nSome considerations\nfor the suspension\nsystem are shown here.\nSince we plan\nto replicate\nthe basic\nSpace\nStation\nstructure,\nthe model will\nnot be designed\nto sustain\nthe unsupported\nI-G\nloads\nof the payloads\nand modules.\nAn implied\nrequirement\nfor the suspension\nsystem\nis that\nit\nshould allow\nthe model to be tested\nwithout\nmasking\nthe model dynamics\ncharacteristics,\ne.g.,\nminimizing\nconstraints\nsuch that\ncoupled\nmode shapes can be\ndetected\nover a limited\noperating\nrange.\nBecause\nof the large\nnumber of concentrated\nmasses and joints,\na large\nnumber of suspension\ncables\nwill\nprobably\nbe required\nto\nsupport\nthe model.\nAlso,\nas previously\ndiscussed,\nthe facility\nsize\nand model scale\nfactor\ndetermine\nthe closeness\nof pendulum\nand model structural\nfrequencies.\nFinally,\na concept\nsuch as the shadow structure,\ndepicted\nat the bottom\nof the\nfigure,\nprovides\ntesting\nversatility,\nisolation\nfrom the structure\nof the facility,\nand a simple\nmethod for attaching\nand detaching\ncables.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SPACE STATION\nUNSUPPORTED\n\nREPLICATION\n1-G LOADS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CABLE SUSPENSION\nMODEL DYNAMICS\n\nSYSTEM\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LARGE CONCENTRATED\nCABLES summary, the COFS III\nproject\nis a technology\nproject\nwhich will\ndevelop\nthe\nmethods\nfor using dynamically\nscaled\nmodels\nand analysis\nto predict\nthe structural\ndynamics\nof large\nspace structures.\nThe project\nuses the Space Station\nas a focus\nbecause\nit is typical\nof the structures\nof interest\nand provides\nthe first\nopportunity\nto obtain\nfull-scale\non-orbit\ndynamics\ndata.\nFinally\nit\nprovides\nthe\nmeans for developing\nthe technology\nfor growth\nSpace Station\nand Space Station\nexperiments.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Hughes\nGeneral Electric Space Division\nPhiladelphia, Pennsylvania\n\nAbstract\n\nA conceptual design of the control system for\nPayload\nPointing Systems\n(PPS) is developed\nusin 9\nclassic\nProportional-lntegral-Derivative\n(PID) techniques.\nThe major source of system\npointing error is due to the disturbance-rich\nenvironment of the Space Station in the form\nof gimbai baseplate motions.\nThese baseplate\nvibrations are characterized using Fast Fourier\nTransform\n(FFT)\ntechniques.\nBoth time domain\nand frequency\ndomain dynamic models are developed\nto\nassess\ncontrol\nsystem\nperformance.\nThree\nbasic\nmethods\nexist\nfor\nthe\nimprovement\nof\nPPS\npointing\nperformance:\nincrease\ncontrol\nsystem bandwidth,\nadd Image Motion Compensation,\nand/or\nreduce\n(or\nchange)\nthe\nbaseplate\ndisturbance environment.\n\nA\n\nIntroduction\n\nA\npermanently\nmanned\nSpace\nStation\n(SS)\nis planned to be launched in the early 1990\'s\nand will orbit at about 460 Km and 28 I/2 degrees\ninclination.\nThe current baseline configuration,\nthe "dual keel" concept, is about 400 ft. long\nand is shown in Figure I.\nThe Station will\norbit\nin a nadir pointing,\ngravity gradient\norientation\nand an\ninitial\nelectrical\npower\ncapacity of approximately 7B KW will be generated\nby a combination of photovoltaic solar arrays\nand point focusing collectors with heat engines.\nA central thermal\nheat rejection system for\nAttached Payloads using fluid loops and large\narticulating radiators is planned.\nOf the approximately one hundred experiments/\ninstruments\nidentified\nas candidate\nAttached\nPayloads for the Space Station, about forty\nper\ncent\nrequire\nsome\ntype\nof\npointing\ncapability.\nThe\naccuracy\nrequirements\nfor\nthese pointed payloads vary from sub-arc seconds\nto degrees and include several high technology\nsolar and celestial telescopes.\nMost of these\ntelescopes\nand other\nsky-viewing\ninstruments\nwill be mounted on the upper boom; the earth\nviewing payloads will be mounted on the lower\nboom.\n\nCopyright \xe2\x80\xa2 American Institute of Aeronautics and\nAstronautics, Inc., 1_6.\nI_prtnted with permission.\nPresented as paper 86-1gM-CP at AIMA GIIIW)\nConference,\nWlillJmsburg,a., Aug. 18-20, lgN.\nV\n\n372\n\nFig. 1\n\nSpace station configuration\ndual keel concept.\n\n-\n\nThe Space Station attitude will be controlled\nby at least six large Control\nMoment Gyros\n(CMGs).\nMomentum\nbuild-up\nwill\nbe\nmanaged\nby off-nadir angles or Torque Equivalent Angles\n(TEA)\ngenerating\ndesirable,\nmomentum-dumping\ngravity\ngradient\ntorques.\nCurrent\nestimates\nof the maximum nominal TEA are : 5\xc2\xb0 in all\naxes with rates of .02\xc2\xb0/sec per axis.\nBecause\nthe Space Station will not be stationary in\nits\nreference\nframe,\ngeneric\nand\nreusable\npointing\nmounts\nare envisioned\nas\nthe most\ncost-effective\nmethod\nof\nachieving\nmost\nof\nthe\npayload\npointing\nrequirements.\nThese\npointing\nmounts\nare called\nPayload\nPointing\nSystems (PPS).\nMuch analysis and design effort\nhas been expended within\nthe past decade on\nShuttle\nbased\npointing\nmounts.\nLaskin\nand\nSirlin I compare twelve different types of gimbal\nsystems\nand\nperform\ntechnology\nassessments\nof\nactuators,\nsensors,\nisolation/suspensions\nsystems, and control techniques.\nThe European\nInstrument\nPointing\nSystem\n(IPS)\nrepresents\na type of design being considered for Space\nStation applications.\nIPS has a large payload\ncapability\n(up to 7000 Kg) and has achieved\nin-flight\nsub-arc second\npointing accuracies 2\nduring\nShuttle/Spacelab\nquiescence\ndisturbance\nperiods.\nA major drawback of the IPS is its\n\nORIGINAL\n\nPAGE\n\nOF POOR\n\nQUALITY\n\nend-mounted\ngimbal\narrangement.\nDue to\nthe\ncenter-of-mass\noffsets,\nthis\ntype\nof\nconfiguration is very sensitive to vibrational\ndisturbances\nbut\nis\nversatile\nin\npayload\naccommodation 3.\nThe disturbances\ncreated\nby\nnormal\ncrew activity\nduring\nthe Spacelab 2\nmission caused 2ointing\nerrors sizably larger\nthan anticipated _.\nThe Space\nStation\nas\nshown in Figure\nI\nrepresents\na\nLarge\nSpace\nStructure\n(LSS).\nAs there are no plans to either control the\nflexible modes (in an active distributed sense)\nor to estimate\n(observe) flexible vibrations\nfor\nfeed-forward\nreasons,\nnon-trivial\ndisturbances\nwill\nexist\nat\nlocations\nwhere\ngimballed payloads will be mounted.\nThis base\nplate disturbance\nrepresents the major source\nof error\nfor the gimbal control\nsystem.\nA\ntypical CG gimbai concept is shown in Figure\n2 and has a third axis of rotation which\nalleviates gimbal lock situations.\n\nIS\n\nIt has been determined that the maintenance\nof precision pointing during periods of large\ndisturbances\nwould\nnot be cost-effective\nas\nthe induced vibrations\nare more\nthan thirty\ntimes larger than the other disturbance tyoes.\nIt is expected that these discrete occurrences\nwill\nbe\ncoordinated\nwith\npayload\nmisssion\ntimelines\nin\norder\nto\nminimize\ndata\nloss.\nHowever,\npayload\npointing\ncapabilities\nmust\nbe maintained for the two smaller disturbance\nlevels.\nThese\nlevels\nrepresent\nquiescence\nor background disturbance levels.\nNASTRAN Model\nTo\ninvestigate\nthe\neffects\nof\nthese\nbackground\nsources\nof\nexcitation,\na\nNASTRAN model\nof\nthe Space\nStation\nwas\ndeveloped.\nForcing\nfunctions\nthat\nmodeled\na "standard" crew kick-off in the pressurized\nmodule, treadmill operations, and a centrifuge\nwith a 20 pound mass imbalance were used as\nmodel inputs.\nVibration levels were assessed\nat various locations on the SS where pointed\npayloads were likely to be attached,\nand a\nrepresentative\nrotational/translational\ncase\nwas selected.\nThis location\nturned\nout to\nbe at the corner of the upper boom (see Figure\nI) and Figure 3 shows the rotational motion.\n\nul, vibrations,\nsolar array and radiator motions, CMG torque_,\npayload articu]ations, and console operations a.\nLarger disturbances occurring in a less random\nmanner include:\ncrew member kickoffs, nominal\nMRMS\n(Remote\nManipulator)\noperations,\ntether\noperations,\nlaboratory\ncentrifuge\noperations,\nand\nastronaut\ntreadmill\nactivities.\nVery\nlarge disturbances occurring at very predictable\nand discrete points in time are Shuttle docking,\nRCS reboost\n(Station keeping via thrusters),\nand large excursions of the MRMS with massive\npayloads.\n\n40\n(SECONDS!\n\nUpper boom of the weighted peaks\nis\nthe calculation\nperformed\nby equation\n(Z2).\n\nPAGI! _3\nQUALITY\nSystem\n(AGS).\nInitially,\nan\noptimistic\ngoal for a 2.4 Hz bandwidth was set. However,\ndue to the combined effects of structural\nflexibility,\ngyro\nand\naccelerometer\nbandwidths,\ntransport\nlags,\nand\nsampling\neffects, a control bandwidth of .7-1.3 Hz\nwas achieved 12.\nBandwidths\nin this range\nappear reasonable for the PPS.\nIt\nis\nimportant\nto\nhave\na standard\ndefinition of bandwidth in order to compare\ndifferent\nsystems.\nThe\ntraditional\nand\nmost widely\naccepted\ndefinition\nis\n"the\n-3db point of the _losed-loop Bode Plot"lO, 13.\nIn some systemsA%\nfor example,\nthe open\nloop\ncrossover\nfrequency\nis\nnear\nthe\nclosed-loop -3db point and the two can be\nused interchangeably.\nSometimes the natural\nfrequency, wn, of the dominant closed loop\npoles\nis referred\nto\nas\nthe bandwidth.\nHowever, for the PPS system being considered\nherein, neither of these bandwidth definitions\nare correct.\nError vs. Bandwidth\nVariations in pointing\nerror or stability error are shown in Figures\n13 and 14 for different bandwidths,\nvarious\nCG offsets, and three different\ntypes of\nmagnitude/frequency\ndisturbance\ninputs.\nFigure 13 is the nominal case for a CG mount\nwith\nsmall\nperturbing\nCG offsets.\nIt\nis\nestimated that there will be a \xc2\xb1 S cm CG\nuncertainty on orbit even with the use of\nan on-llne mess balancing system.\nWithout\nsuch a balancing system, an error of about\n\xc2\xb1 20 cm ts estimated.\n\nFig. 12\n\nAttenuation functions without\nfeed-forward.\n\nBandwidth Considerations\n\nOne of the most important and descriptive\nparameters\nof\na\ncontrol\nsystem\nis\nits\nbandwidth.\nIn\ngeneral,\nbandwidth\n(or\npassband) is the range of frequencies that\ncan pass through a control system.\n"High"\nbandwidth\nsystems\nhave fast\nresponse\ntimes\nand\nhigh\nperformance\nfactors\nbut\nusually\nsuffer\nfrom high frequency\nnoise and reduced\nstability\nmargins.\n"Low" bandwidth\nsystems,\nwhile\nslower in reaction\ntimes,\nhave excellent\nnoise\nrejection\ncharacteristics.\nLaser\nPointing\nSystems 11\nhave\nbandwldths\nabove\n100 Hz while\nspacecraft\nattitude\ncontrol\nsystems\ntypically\nhave\nbandwidths\nof\n.OS\nHz and lower.\nMany ,factors\nimpact the bandwidth\nof\na system a and include:\ncontroller design,\nstructural\nflexlbllltles,\nnonlinearities,\ncomponent\nbandwldths,\ncomponent\nnoise\ncharacteristics,\nand\ngain/phase\nstability\nmargin\nspecifications.\nAn example of the\ndifficulties\nin\nachieving\nhigh bandwidths\nwas\nthe\nSperry-designed\nAdvanced\nGimbal\n\nLarge\nerror\nsensitivities\nto\ndifferent\ndisturbances\nare\nseen\nin\nFigure\n14.\nThe\nterm\n"8 Frequencies"\nindicates\nthe\nnominal\ncase of eight FFT frequencies\nfor the rotation\nand\nacceleration\ndisturbances.\nThe\nterm\n"3\nfrequencies"\nindicates\nthat\nonly\nthe\nfirst\nthree\nof\nthe\neight\nFFT frequencies\nwere retained,\nand "1 frequency"\nmeans that\nonly\none\nfrequency\nfor\neach\ndisturbance\nwas used as an input.\nFor the nomtnal case,\nthe\nlarge\nerrors\nwhich\nare\nrelatlvely\ninsensitive to bandwidth changes are caused\nby the high frequency disturbances\noutside\nthe control system bandwidth.\nFor the "3\nfrequency"\ncase,\nbandwidth\nchanges\naffect\nthe error more strongly. The single frequency\ncase was used to emphasize the importance\nof\nthe\nmagnitudes\nand\nfrequencies\nFFT\ndisturbances\non\npointing\nperformance\nand\nas a verification\ntool.\nIn this special\nconfiguration one frequency (w X\n1.97 Hz\nwith an amplitude of .023 ft/se\xc2\xa2) from the\nacceleration\nFFT and one frequency\n(wr =\n.51 Hz with an amplitude of l.OB arc-sec)\nfrom\nthe displacement\nFFT were\nused\nas\ndisturbances.\nA pointing error of _ 3.54\narc-sec resulted and is almost a four-fold\nimprovement\nin\npointing\nperformance\nover\nthe\nnominal\ndisturbance\ncase.\nThis\nconfiguration served as a verification case\nas the time-varying pointing error generated\nby\nthe time domain\nsimulation\nmodel was\nvirtually periodic over the simulation time\nand the maximum peak-to-peak variation could\nbe measured with accuracy.\nThere was less\nthan a I% error between values calculated\nby the frequency and time domain techniques.\n\n377\n\nloo\n\nPOINTING\nERROR\n\nN\'IASI\n(OEGI\n15\nBIUTY\n\n_\'_I0\n\n_._.......\n\nERROR)\n\nCG\n\n=\n\n2O i_\n\n_\n\n._ potential\nflexibility\nproblems Is to choose the open-loop crossover\nfrequency\nwith\nabout one-third\nthe frequency\nthat\nis\nnot necessary\nto control.\nAnother\ntechnique 8\nis\nto\ndesign\nfor\na\n"minimum\nbandwidth"\nwhich\nprovides\nfor\nthe\nmaximum\nleveIs\nof\nmargin\nwhile\nstill\nmeeting\nperformance\nrequirements.\nThe pole\nplacement\ntechnique\nused in\nthis\nstudy\nof the\ncontroller\nconceptual\ndesign\nassun_d that the payload was a lumped inertia\nand effects\nof gimbal/yoke\nstiffness were\nnot directly considered.\nLittle difference\nin\nresponses\nwill\nbe discerned\nfor\ntwo\npayloads\nwith\ndifferent\ninertlas\nas\nthe\nloop gain, K, will increase linearly with\nthe load inertia, J.\nIn reality, this is\nincorrect as a large inertia will result\nin a low destabilizing fundamental frequency.\nAdditionally,\na high electrical\nloop gain\nwill amplify\nthe effects of system noise\nand will\ndegrade performance.\nTo demonstrate\nthe effects\nof flexibility\non loop stability,\na single\nmedal\nfrequency\ntransfer\nfunction\nwas added in\nparallel\nto the payload\nblock\nin Figure\n6.\nThe results\nof this simplified\ntechnique are shown in the open loop Bode\nPlot {Figure\n17) for two payload\nmasses\n(I000 Ng and 2000 Kg).\nNote that for the\nlower\nmass\nthe\nresonant\nfrequency\nhas\nincreased\nthereby\nproviding\nabout\n6\ndb\nadditional gain margin.\n\nImprovement\n\nAs detailed\nin the previous\nsections\nthe expected dynamic disturbances\nfrom the\nSS acting through traditional control systems\nwith bandwldths of about\nI Hz will cause\nnon-trlval pointing\nerrors;\nand,\nin\nsome\ncases, a payload\'s\npointing\nerror\nrequirement\ncan\nbe\nexceeded.\nThe\nfollowing\nis\na\ndiscussion\nof\nseveral\noptions\nthat\nhave\npotential\nfor\nthe\nimprovement\nof\npointing\nperformance:\nController\nDesign\nModerate\nimprovements\nin pointing\nperformance\ncan\nbe\nreallzed\nthrough optima!\ncontrol\ndesign\ntechniques\nor a more rigorous classical design approach.\nBy advancing the state-of-the-art\nin sensor\nand actuator technologles I, higher bandwldths\ncan be achieved with smaller pointing errors\nas demonstrated in Figure 14.\nAdded\nStructural\nDamping\nAn\nincrease\nin\nthe SS\nstructural\ndamping\nwill\ndecrease\nthe amplitudes of the disturbance vibrations\nand a corresponding\ndecrease\nin pointiog\nerror will result.\nPassive damping methods z9\nappear promising.\nActive structural damping\nsuch as piezoelectric techniques are probably\nnot cost-effective for the current SS design.\nImage Motion Compensations (IMC)\nThis proven\ntechnique is usually\nan integral Dart\nof\nan\ninstrument\'s\ncontrol\ndesign2O,21,\nbut\nIMC also could be exterior to a payload 22.\nBasically, IMC entails high bandwidth systems\nthat control\nlow mass\nactuators\nsuch as\nmirrors.\nThe net effect of an IMC system\nis attenuation of the disturbance frequencies\npassed by the gimbal control system.\nDisturbance\nManaqement\nInternal\nvibration\nsources of the space station can be managed\nin\na\nfashion\nsimiliar\nto\nthat\nof\na\nspacecraft\'s\ntraditional\nweight\nand power\nallocation/budget. Vibration absorbers and/or\nisolators can be added\nto equipment with\noffending disturbance spectra.\n\n379\n\nPayload\nIsolation\nThis\nmethod\nattempts\nto isolate the pointed\npayload\nfrom the\ndisturbance source which is, in this case,\nthe entire space station.\nThere are three\nisolation options to be considered:\npassive,\nactive, and softmount.\n\nsystem and the isolator, payload\npointing\nis controlled by CMGs with a I Hz bandwidth,\nand\nthat all\nconnections\nto\nthe payload\nare such that the isolator system has a\nnatural frequency of .I Hz.\nThe resultant\nisolator/control\ncharacteristic\nis similiar\nto that derived in a previous study 26 and\nprovides for an order-of magnitude reduction\nin pointing error.\n\nPassive isolation, which typically uses\nsprings and dampers, suffers from practical\nproblems and does not appear feasible for\nthe space station applications.\nTo attenuate\nthe > I Hz frequencies, an isolator frequency\nof .l Hz or less would be required.\nFor\na 2000 Kg payload,\nthis\ncorresponds\nto\na\nspring\nrate\nof 790 N/M which\nis likely\nto\nbe\nsofter\nthan\nthe\npower\nand\nthermal\nconnections\ntO\nand\nfrom\nthe\npayload.\nAdditionally,\nthe\ngtmba]\nsystem\nwill\nnot\nbe able to react\ntorques\nagainst\nthe massive\nspace\nstation\nand\nthis\nwill\nintroduce\nadditional\npointing\nerrors.\nIt\nis\nalso\nlikely\nthat\nwithout\nany\npayload\nmomentum\ncompensation\ndevices\nclosed-loop\nstability\nproblems will exist.\n\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nA study of interface problems between the Space\nStation\nStructure\nand\nthe\nPayload\nPointing\nControl\nSystem has been completed.\nA major\nresult is that the Space Station will have\na disturbance-rlch\nenvironment\nand vibration\nbackground levels will be large enough to impact\nthe pointing performance of some payloads.\nThe dynamic model used to quantify\nthe pointing\nperformance\nof\nthe\nconceptual\ndesign\nof\nthe\nPPS\nincluded\nmany\nsimplified\nassumptions.\nIt is\nexpected\nthat larger\npointing\nerrors\nwill be observed when mope detailed\nand accurate\nmodels of the PPS are developed.\n\nMagnetic\nsuspension 23 and the Gimbalflex 24\nare\ncommon techniques\nof\nactive\nvibration\nisolation\nthat\nhave\nSS\napplicability.\nTypically,\na\nmagnetic\nsuspension\nsystem\nwill\nhave six-degrees\nof\nfreedom\n(three\nrotations\nand three\ntranslations)\nand will\nperform\nsignificantly\nbetter\nthan\npassive\ndevices 23.\nThe transfer\nof services\nacross\nthe\nisolator interface\nremains\na problem\nfor\nboth\ntypes\nof\nactive\nisolation\nin\nachieving adequate disturbance rejection.\n\nEnd mounted gimbal systems suffer\nfrom CG offset\nproblems and a CG mount is better from a pointing\nstandpoint.\nIt has been determined\nthat at\nleast\na \xc2\xb1 5 centimeter CG offset will exist\nfor a spaceborne\nCG mount even with\nan on-board\nmass balancing\nsystem.\nTo reduce the impact of Space Station vibrations\non pointing performance, several\ndesign\noptions\nwere presented.\nThe two most cost\neffective\ntechniques\nappear\nto be disturbance\nmanagement\nand payload Image Motion Compensation.\n\nThe softmount25 is a concept\nin which\nthe payload becomes\na virtual\nfree-flyer\nwith\nalmost\nno\ndynamic\ninteraction\nwith\nthe base body.\nThe payload would require\nits own attitude control system using CMGs\nor\nreaction\nwheels\nand\nwould\nbe loosely\nconnected\nto the space\nstation via tethers\nor guy wires.\n\nReferences\n\nAn example of how an Rhodes\n\nLangley\nResearch\nCenter\nHampton,\nVirginia\n\nNASA/DOD\nCSI Technology\nConference\nNorfolk,\nVirginia\nNovember\n18-21,\n1986\n\nPRE_\n\nPAGE BLANK NOT FILMED\n\n383\n\nINTRODUCTION\nAll of the structures considered for the Control of Flexible Structures\n(COFS)flight experiments are deployable truss structures and their response\nwill be dominated by the structural response of the joints.\nTo prepare for\nthese experiments some fundamental research work is being conducted in the\nStructures and Dynamics Division at laRC which will provide insight into\nstructurally\nefficient and predictable deployable truss joints.\nThis work\ninvolves generic studies of the static and dynamic response of joints as well\nas the development of analytical models which can be used to predict the\nresponse\nof a large\nin references\n1 and\n\nmultijointed\ntruss.\nSome of\n2, and additional\npublications\n\nthis work has\nare planned.\n\nbeen documented\nIn addition\nto\n\nthe generic\njoint\nstudies,\nthe research\neffort\nencompasses\nthe design\nand\nfabrication\nof a 20-meter\nlong deployable\ntruss beam\nfor laboratory\nevaluation\nof its structural\ncharacteristics\nand correlation\nwith developed\nprediction\nmethods.\nThe purpose\nof the present\npaper\nis to present\na snapshot\nof a limited\npart of this research\nactivity\nand discuss\nsome design\nconsiderations\nin the static behavior\nof joints\nfor deployable\nspace truss\nstructures.\n\n384\n\nSTATICTESTEVALUATION\nOF_9OJOINTTYPES\nThe work presented in thecurrentpapercovers\nsomerecent experimental\nstudies to evaluate the static stiffness of two joint types conmonly\nincorporated inmost deployable truss structures.\nThese joints are a pinclevis\ntype and a folding\nlinkage\nsystem\ndescribed\nherein\nas a near-center\nlatch\njoint.\nThe pin-clevis\njoint is usedin\na wide range\nof industrial\nand\ncommercial\napplications\nand there is considerable\ninformation\nin the open\nliterature\nregarding\nfactors\nthat affect\nits static\nstrength.\nHowever,\nvery\nlittle\ninformation\nis available\non the design\naspectsthat\naffect\nthe\nstructural\nstiffness.\nThe present\nexperimental\nstudy was a limited\nparametric\nevaluation\nof some factors\nthat affect\nthe stiffness\nof the high load region (loads above\n300 pounds) was determined from plotted results such as those shown on\nfigure 4 for several nominal pin diameters.\nTypical stiffness results are\nshown in figures 5a and 5b for the steel and aluminum plates respectively.\nThe\ndashed curves were faired through the points to represent the trends in the\ndata.\nThe stiffness for tensile loads increases in a well controlled manner\nwith increase in pin diameter until it reaches a plateau in the range of 45 to\n55 percent of the plate width.\nThen it decreases markedly for the largest\ndiameter\n(0.63 inch) tested.\nHowever, the coapression\nresults continue to\nincrease with pin diameter.\nThe area reduction in the section adjacent to the\npin is responsible for the loss in tensile stiffness but has no adverse effect\non the conpression stiffness because it is not in the compression\nload path.\nThe data also indicate that the structural stiffness of a pin-clevis joint is\nbilinear and that the stiffness in compression will always be higher than it is\nin tension.\nThis is because a tensile loaded joint has a longer load path and\nthe net section stress level adjacent to the hole, which is in that load path,\ncan be very high.\nTo minimize this bilinearity,\nthe results indicate that the\npin diameter should be about 45 percent of the plate width, regardless of the\nparent joint material.\n\nSteel plates\n\n18 x\n\n10 5\n[]\n\n16-\n\nD\n\n[]\n\n14-\n\n/\n\n/\n\n/\n[]\n\nD\nO..._ The parent metallic material is titanium with four\nhardened steel pins being used at the linkage and body hinge points.\nAll pin\nholes\n\nwere\n\naccurately\n\npositioned\n\nand\n\nthe\n\ncritical\n\npin\n\nholes\n\n(i.e.,\n\nthe\n\nones\n\nthat control\nthe closure\nposition\nof the linkage\nmechanism)\nand the hinge pin\nhole were drilled\nwith the parts\nassembled.\nAll holes and pin diameters\nwere\nsized\nto an accuracy\nof 0.0001\ninch, and the assembled\nparts had between\n0 and\n0.0002\ninches\nof interference\nfit.\nThe linkage\nsystem\nwas designed\nso that no\nmember\nwas in bending.\nThe preload\nin the joint is controlled\nby the position\nof the linkage\nand the torsion\nload applied\nby the closure\nspring.\nThe\nconfiguration\nwas designed\nto have a preload\nin excess\nof 80 pounds,\nhowever,\nthe response\nis very nonlinear\nin the closed\nposition\nand the actual\npreload\nvalue\nhas not been determined.\nConsiderable\neffort\nwent into the design\nof the\njoint\nso that a predictable\nand repeatable\nresponse\ncould be obtained\nfor a\nlarge\nnumber\nof units.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nParent joint\n\nmaterial-titanium,\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nLinkage members\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAll pins and holes have light interference\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCritical\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nInterior preload\n\nin load path take only axial load\n\npin holes drilled on assembly\n\nof 80 + Ibs\n\nFigure\n\n394\n\npin material-steel\n\n9\n\nfits\n\nfixture\n\nRESPONSE NEAR-CENTERIATCHJOINT\nOF\nThe load deflection response for the near-center latch joint is shown in\nfigure i0. importance of\nattention to detail in the design and fabrication of joints for deployable\ntruss structures.\nThe dimensional relations and material considerations for\nefficient\npin-clevis joints have been outlined.\nThe results of tests on the\nnear-center latch indicate that joint complexity does not necessarily sacrifice\nstiffness.\nHowever, the test joint was fairly massive. This is undesirable\nfor dynamic\nconsiderations,\nespecially\nif the joint\nis in the center\nof a long\nmember.\nBending\ncauses\nlow axial\nstiffness\nand must be eliminated\nin the\nmember\nand connecting\njoint\nif at all possible\nfor the high axial\nand torsional\nstiffness\nof the truss\nstructure\nto be realized.\n\nDesign\n\nrecommendations\n\nLinear load-displacement\n\nBending\ndesigned\n\nformulated\n\ncan be obtained\n\nmust be eliminated\nand fabricated\n\nfor pin-clevis\n\njoints\n\nin complex\n\nto realize attributes\n\njoints\n\nof well\n\njoint\n\nFigure\n\n12\n\n397\n\nREFERENCES\n\n,\n\nBelvin,\nW. Pappa\nDynamics\n\nLangley\n\nTECHNIQUES\n\nCenter\n23665\n\nTechnology\n18-21,\n\nConference\n\n1986\n\n399\n\n"\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nModal identification\nis the process of determining the modal parameters\n(natural frequencies, damping factors, modeshapes, and modal masses) of a structure\nfrom experimental measurements. In general, these parameters are not directly\nmeasurable quantities.\nA commonlyused approach is to infer their values from a set\nof measured frequency response functions\n(FRFs). This parameter identification\nprocess, though relatively\nstraightforward in principle,\nis often quite difficult\nand time-consuming in practice,\nparticularly\nfor large or built-up structures.\nLarge structures may have many local modes in the frequency range of interest,\nmaking it difficult\nto determine global characteristics\nat nearby frequencies.\nAnd\nbuilt-up structures, consisting of two or more components connected together with\nfasteners or joints,\nare often nonlinear due to imperfect load paths across the\nconnections.\nFor these structures,\nit may be difficult\nto determine the true number of\nmodes, and manyof the identified modeshapes may be questionable.\nIdentification\nresults can vary considerably using different analysis techniques.\nMany different approaches are possible for the identification\nof structures\n(e.g.,\nRefs. I-7).\nSomework has been done on comparing various techniques\nmathematically (Refs. 8-9). However, virtually\nno information is yet available\non\nhow the techniques compare in practice.\nTheoretical considerations, of course, form\nthe foundation of any technique. Unfortunately, however, the performance actually\nobserved in practice can be affected significantly\nby a number of factors not\naccounted for explicitly\nin most techniques. These factors include nonlinearities,\nunusually high modal density or damping, local modes, unmeasuredexcitations, large\ndifferences in modal response amplitudes, nonstationarity,\nsensor noise, off-axis\nsensor response, signal-processing biases, inconsistent data, finite dynamic range,\nand roundoff error, amongothers.\nThis paper discusses a new method for studying the performance of various\nidentification\ntechniques in practice.\nThe procedure is analogous to using\nsimulated data corrupted by random noise--a commonlyused procedure--except now the\nsimulated data is "corrupted" instead with a set of complex experimental data.\nFor\nthis study, a set of 128 frequency response functions from an erectable truss\nstructure with high modal density and significant\nnonlinearity was used. These data\nwere modified numerically by adding two artificial\nmodeswith known parameters. The\nresulting "hybrid data" were then analyzed using both the Polyreference technique\n(Refs. 10-13) and the Eigensystem Realization Algorithm (ERA) (Refs. 14-17) over a\nwide range of analysis orders (i.e.,\nassumed number of modes). Identification\nperformance is studied by comparing the identified parameters for the artificial\nmodes with their known values.\nSix different\ncases, using distinct\nand then\nrepeated eigenvalues at three different\nresponse amplitudes, were analyzed.\nRepresentative portions of the results are discussed.\n\n400\n\nCONSTRUCTION\n\nFigure\nI\nfunction.\nThe\n\nOF\n\n"HYBRID\n\nDATA"\n\nillustrates\nthe construction\nof a typical\n"hybrid"\nfrequency\nresponse\ntop plot shows\nindividual\nFRFs It is important to recognize that considerable freedom\nis available to a user in choosing these analysis parameters and that the results\nwill vary somewhatwith different selections.\nFor this study the following choices were made: For Polyreference,\na maximum\nmode count of 64 and a frequency interval from 35 to 99 Hz were selected; data from\nboth references and all 64 response coordinates\nwere accumulated into the\ncorrelation\nmatrix; Modal Confidence Factors (MCFs) and complex residues were\ncomputed; and the assumed number of modes was incremented from I to 64. These\nselections\nresulted in the use of 194 time samples from each unit impulse response\nfunction.\nFor ERA, a block data matrix of 384 rows and 376 columns was established; data\nfrom both references\n(inputs)\nand all 64 responses (outputs)\nwere used\nsimultaneously, resulting in a block size of 64 by 2; time shifts of one data sample\nwere used between all block rows, between all block columns, and between the two\ndata matrices; a frequency interval\nfrom 35 to 99 Hz was used; and the assumed\nnumber of modes(one-half the number of retained singular values) was incremented\nfrom I to 64. These values were chosen so that 194 time samples were also used from\neach unit impulse response function.\nThe unit impulse response functions for both techniques were obtained by\ninverse Fourier transformation of the FRFdata in the interval from 35 to 99 Hz (256\nspectral lines).\n\n406\n\nIDENTIFICATION\nRESULTS\nAll identification\nresults shown in this paper were generated on a VAX11/780\ncomputer, using Version 9.0 of Modal-Plus (Ref. 20) for all Polyreference analyses\nand the single-precision\nversion of the ERAprogram for all ERAanalyses.\nTo begin, the natural frequencies identified by Polyreference and ERA for Case\n2 are plotted in Figures 6(a) and 6(b), respectively.\nThese figures show all of the\nmodescalculated by each method as the assumednumberof modes (an analysis option\nin both techniques) was incremented from I to 64. The identified frequencies are\nindicated by short, vertical line segments. The height of each line segment is\nproportional\nto the corresponding MCF(for Polyreference) or to the corresponding\nInput Modal Amplitude Coherence (for ERA) calculated for the mode. Both MCF and\nIMAC are "accuracy indicators"\nused to help differentiate\nbetween extraneous\nelgenvalues due to noise and data distortions and the actual modes of the system.\nIf the value of the indicator\nis 100 percent, the corresponding line segment is\ndrawn as long as the distance between tic marks on the vertical\naxis.\nAll\nidentified\neigenvalues are included in these plots; if the indicator had a value of\nzero, the corresponding modeappears as just a dot in the figure.\nNumbered arrows\nat the top of the plots indicate the frequencies selected for the two artificially\nadded modes.\nWith simple, linear data these plots would also consist of straight, vertical\nlines located at the natural frequencies of the structure.\nAny data point falling\noff the lines would have a small accuracy-indicator\nvalue. However, as seen in\nFigure 6, things are not that straightforward with complex experimental data.\n\n407\n\n_1\n\n_2\n\n35\n\n42\n\n49\n\n56\n\n35\n\n42\n\n49\n\n56\n\n63\n\n" 70\n\n77\n\n84\n\n91\n\n98\n\n1 05\n\n63\n\n70\n\n77\n\n84\n\n91\n\n98\n\n105\n\n84\n\n91\n\n98\n\n105\n\nFrequency,\n\nHz\n\n(a) Polyreference\n\nResults\n\n77\n\no3\n@\n\n6O\nI\n\n0 5O\n\nI\n\no 4O\n\n6\nZ\n\n30\n\n-o\n(I)\n2O\n\nE\n\nO3\nCO I0\n\n(b)\n\nFigure\n\n408\n\n6.\n\nERA\n\nResults\n\nIdentified\nFrequencies\nfor Case 2.\nProportional\nto MCF (Polyreference)\nCoherence\n(ERA)\n\nHeight\nof Dashed\nLines\nor Input Modal Amplitude\n\ni\n\nIDENTIFICATION\nRESULTS\n(CONTINUED)\nFigures 7 and 8 provide a closer look at the results for Case 2 near the\nfrequency of the second added mode (55 Hz). Figure 7 shows the Polyreference\nresults\nand Figure 8 the corresponding ERAresults.\nThe identified frequencies are\nshown in the upper-left plots using the sameformat as in Figure 6. In this case,\nhowever, only the results for modes from 53 to 58 Hz are displayed to improve\nclarity.\nThe five other plots in the figures provide additional,\ncorresponding\nresults\nfor these modes. The true values of each parameter used for the artificial\nmodes are again indicated by numbered arrows above the top axes.\nThe two\ncorrelation\nplots at the bottom of the figures show the square of the correlation\ncoefficient\n(Ref. 21) between each of the two chosen modeshapes (Figure 5(b)) and\neach identified shape.\nA comparison bf Figures 7 and 8 shows that both techniques identified\nthe\nfrequency, damping, residue amplitude, and modeshape information accurately for\nCase 2 as the assumednumber of modeswas increased sufficiently,\nalthough the rate\nof convergence is different.\nHowever, the identified residue phase angle shows a\nbias of approximately +15 degrees in both results.\nThe cause of the bias is unknown\nat this time.\n\n409\n\n_2\n53\ni\n60\n"o\no\n\n54\n\nT ....\nI\n\nL\n\n_1&2\n\n55\n\n56\n\n57\n\n58\n\ni ....\n\n.6\ni .....\n\n,\'/\'\n\n:\n\n\' \' \'If;ll\n\'";\'\'\n\n.....\'\n\n1.2\ni,,. ....\n\nI I\n\ni\n\n!\n\nI\nI\nl\n\n"O\nID\n\nI\n\nI\n\nD\nm\n\nI\n3O\n\nIII\n\nI z1\n\n1.0\n\ni\nI ....\n53\n\n55\n\n56\n\nFrequency,\n\n57\n\n58\n\n0\n\n,.\'\n\n(n 60\n\n.8\n\nI I 1L I_ \'\n\n! st\n"\n\n-o\n\nZ\n\n!\n\nI\n\nii \'\n\nI\n\n1.6\n\nI\n\n2.0\n\nI\n\n\' |\'\n\n0\n\n!\n\n!\n\n3O\n\n!\n\nI\n\n;\n!\n\n!\n\n1.2\n\n72\n\n144\n\nl\n\n\'\n\n3.0\n\n%\n\n216\n\n288\n\n!!\n\n:\n\nI\n\nIf#\n\n{\n\nI;\n\n\',!\ni\n\nI\n\nI\n50\n\n\'!\nl\n\nIll\n\n;\n\n!\n\n40\n\n=\n\nI1\n11\nII\n\nI\nI\n\nI\n\n;\n\nt\n\nII\n\n3O\n\nI\n\n!!, =\n\nI\n\n20\n\n!\n!\nI\n\ni\n\n,\n\nI\n\nResidue\n\n,\n\n.8\n\n.4\n\nI\n1.2\n\nAmplitude\n\n,\n\nI\n1.6\n\n,\n\nI\n\n0\n\n(1 Y+/1 Y+)\n_I\n\n20\n\n40\n\n60\n\n80\n\n, ,\n\nI\n\n2.0\n\n$2\n0\n\n60\n\nI\n\nI\n!\n\n:\n\n!\n\nI\nl\n\nI\n\n!\n\n560\n\n;\n\nI\n\nII t\nI\n\n}\n\nI\n\n11\n\nI\n\nD\nffl\n\n2.4\n\nII\n\nl\n\nE 2o\n\n1.8\n\nFactor,\n\nI\n\n1\n\n-(3\nID\n\n0\n\nI0\n0\n\n12\n\nDamping\n\n\',,, \',:),,\n\nd\n\nIf\n\n_1&2\n\n1.2\n\n_l; \' _l\nil LIs\n\n!\n\n\'B 4o\n\ni\n\n:_\n\n.6\n\nHz\n\n_1&2\n.4\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI , , i . Complete ERA Results for Case 2 (Near\nFrequency of Second Added Mode)\n\n411\n\nIDENTIFICATION\n\nAdditional\n\nidentification\n\nresults\n\nRESULTS\n\nare\n\n(CONTINUED)\n\nprovided\n\nin Figures\n\n9 and\n\n10\n\nfor\n\nCases\n\n3\n\nand 5, respectively.\nCase 3 used\nthe same\nparameters\nas Case\n2 except\nthat\nthe\nresidue\namplitudes\nwere\nreduced\nby a factor\nof ten.\nAt this low amplitude,\nthe\ntotal\nenergy\nin each of the two added modes\nis only\nslightly\nabove\nthe\nbackground\nnoise\nfloor.\nThe amplitude\nreduction\nhas caused\nthe results\nto be uniformly\nless\naccurate\nthan for Case 2.\nThe residue\nand mode shape results,\nin particular,\nshow\nsignificant\ndeviations\nfrom\ntheir\ntrue values.\nAlso note that the scatter\nin the\nfrequency\nand damping\nresults\nfor the weak structural\nmode at slightly\nless\nthan\n56\nHz is similar\nin character\nto that for the 55-Hz artificial\nmode.\nThis suggests\na\nsimilar\n\nsecond\n\ndegree\n\nof\n\ninaccuracy\n\nin the\n\nFigure\n10 shows corresponding\nadded\nmode was lowered\nto\n\nidentified\n\nmode\n\nresults\nfor Case\n50 Hz to generate\n\nshapes\n\nas\n\nwell.\n\n5, in which\na repeated\n\nthe frequency\neigenvalue.\n\nof\n\nthe\nBoth\n\ntechniques\nsuccessfully\nidentified\nboth\nmodes.\nAgain,\nhowever,\nthe convergence\nrates\nare different.\nNote that the residue\nresults\nare not determined\nuniquely\nin\nthis case.\nThe sum of the two identified\nresidues,\nhowever,\nshould\nequal the sum of\nthe two true residues.\nNote also that the two correlation\nplots have been replaced\nby a single\nplot\nshowing\nthe total\ncorrelation\nbetween\neach identified\nmode shape\nand the shapes of the two added modes.\nThe total correlation\nwould\nbe 100 percent\nfor each mode for perfect\nidentification.\n\n412\n\n53\n\n54\n\n55\n\n56\n\n57\n\n_1&2\n\n_.1&2\n\n_1&2\n,6\n\n58\n\n1 2\n\n1 8\n\n2.4-\n\n.2\n\n3.0\n\n.4,\n\n,6\n\n.8\n\n72\n\n10\n\'\n\n\'\n\n......... seemingly\nsimple\nstructures,\nsuch as the generic\ndiscussed\nin the\npaper,\nis often\nsurprisingly\ndifficult\nin various\n\nof\n\ninformation\n\nfor\n\nthe\n\nsystem\n\ntruss\nto high\nthese\n\nsubstantially\na new "hybrididentification\n\nidentification\n\nresearcher.\nFirst,\nit allows\nactual\nexperimental\ndata\nto be used\nin the\nstudies,\nwhile\nmaintaining\nthe traditional\nadvantage\nof using simulated\ndata.\nThat is, the\nidentification\ntechnique\nunder study\nis forced\nto cope with the complexities\nof real\ndata,\nyet\nthe performance\ncan be measured\nunquestionably\nfor the artificial\nmodes\nbecause\ntheir\ntrue parameters\nare known.\nSecondly,\nthe accuracy\nachieved\nfor\nthe\ntrue\nstructural\nmodes\nin the\ndata can be estimated\nfrom the accuracy\nachieved\nfor\nthe artificial\nmodes\nif the results\nshow similar\ncharacteristics.\nThis\nsimilarity\noccurred\nin the\nstudy,\nfor\nexample,\nfor a weak\nstructural\nmode near 56 Hz (ref.\nFigure\n9).\nIt may even be possible--eventually--to\nuse the error\ninformation\nfrom\nthe\nartificial\nmodes\nto improve\nthe\nidentification\naccuracy\nfor the This is due to\nthe fact that differences in ground and space environments can significantly\nchange the measuredparameters. In addition, the time-varying nature of the space\nenvironment requires real-time "tracking" of key structural parameters.\nAn on-orbit ID function can provide the real-time knowledge of plant\ncharacteristics\nwhich greatly influence control performance, such as flex-body\nparameters, self-generated disturbances, shape distortions,\nactuation and sensing\ndynamics, etc. This information is then available for updating the controller\nplant model, and may serve as the data base from which a control function can make\nadjustments to autonomously tune the system performance and stability\nmargins. I\nIn this discussion, the focus will be on those identification\nand modeling\naspects primarily associated with large space structure dynamical control.\n\nCONTROL SYSTEMS\n\nI\nDYNAMI CAL\nEMBEDDED\nMODELS\n\nj,_SYSTEM\n\nQUAS\nISTATI\nC\n\xe2\x80\xa2 STIFFNESS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 INERTI\nA/CM\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DEFORMATIONS\n\n420\n\nI\nI\nI\n\nIDENTIFICATIONt_\nI\nI\n\nI\nADAPTIVE_\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PARAMETERERRORS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MODELTRUNCATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 NONLINEARITIES\n\nI\n\nCONTROL\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MODAL FREQIDAMPING\nDYNAMIC SHAPES\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MODE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2TRANSIENTDIsTURBANcEsAND\nSTOCHASTIC\nLFIGURE\n\nDYNAMIC\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DISTURBANCE SUPPRESSION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 VIBRATION DAMPING\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LOS STABILIZATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SURFACE DEFORMATIONS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 RELATIVE ALIGNMENTS\n\nGENERAL\nFLEXIBLESTRUCTURE\nCONTROL\nMETHODOLOGY\nIn general, the high frequency dynamics of LSS will not be well\nknown and the order of the dynamics will be too large to design an effective\nwideband control system. Newmethods are required to control the low\nfrequency modeswithout exciting the higher frequency dynamics inherent\nin the structure and to constrain the control gains from spill-over\ninto\nthe higher modescausing instabilities\nor performance loss. This area is\na major thrust in the extension of modern control theory and the emerging\ncross-discipline\ntechnology\nof active\nstructural\ncontrol\nrange\nof interrelated\ndesign\nand system\nfunctions.\n\nphase\n\nAs depicted\nbelow,\nthe general\nstructure\nstabilize\nmodes\nlying within\nthe control\n\ncovers\n\na wide\n\ncontrol\nmethodology\nbandwidth\n(in-band\n\nis to\nmodes)\nand\n\nto gain stabilize\nstructurally\ndamped\nmodes lying outside\nthe control\nbandwidth.\nSuch active\ncontrol\nmethods\nrequire\nvery precise\nknowledge\nof the\nlocations\nof the in-band\nmodes\nin order\nto allow correct\nphase\nstabilization\n(such sensitivities\nare even more pronounced\nwhen actuators\nand sensors\nare non-colocated).\nBecause\nsuch precise\nknowledge\nof system\ndynamics\nis\nnot possible\nvia ground\ntesting\nalone,\non-orbit\nsystem\nidentification\nis\nrequired.\n\ndB GAIN\n"--DI STURDANCE BANDWIDTH--"I _TRANs\nI\nIN-BAND identification\n(ID) on-orbit methodology is\ndriven by practical considerations.\nThe post-launch changes in a physical\nsystem\'s characteristics\nmust first be identified before the mission operations\nproceed with confidence; structural dynamics identification\nis essential for\naccomplishing active vibration control of large structures; and system\ncharacterization\nin space is required to verify the accuracy and adequacy of\nground-based models for predicting and modifying in-flight\nperformance.\nThe technical challenges involved in performing on-orbit identification\nare\nsignificant.\nAs opposed to ground testing which involves elaborate test beds with\nvirtually\nunlimited sensing, excitation,\nand computational resource, the on-orbit\nsituation is constrained to only a few sensors, operationally viable excitations,\nand restricted computational resource. 3 The diagram below illustrates\nthe\nintegrated systems approach for on-orbit dynamics identification.\nThe methodology\ninvolves a multi-stage identification\nprocess starting with robust nonparametric\nsurvey methods, and progressing to more refined parameter determination\nalgorithms.\n\nI\n\nTHRUSTER\nEXCITATION\nDI STURBANCE\nDYNAMIC\n\nACTUATORS\nT PROOF-MASS\n\n?t\'\nSYSTEM _\nI\nRESPONSE\n\nI\n\nr*k\n_/\n\ni\n\nFFT I\n1 I,l,l,lll,I,\n\nI\n\n\'MODEL PLACEMENT/\nSENSO,_\n\n423\n\nIDENTIFICATION\n\nEXPERIMENT\n\nCONSTRAINED\n\nINPUT\n\nDESIGN\n\nOn-orbit\nidentification\nof modal\nfrequencies\nof a 15 degree-of-freedom\nPower\nTower model\nof the Space Station\nwas considered\nusing maximum\nlikelihood\nestimation.\nLow frequency\nmodes\ncan successfully\nbe identified\nby the proper\napplication\nof multi-directional\nthruster\ninputs\nin the form of force and torques\nin orthogonal\nspaces.\nHigher\nfrequency\nmodes,\nhowever,\nare insufficiently\nexcited\nto permit\naccurate\nestimation\ndue to thruster\npower\nand bandwidth\nlimitations.\nAmong\nthe inputs considered,\nit is concluded\nthat staggered\npulse\nsequences\nare\nsuperior\nfor obtaining\na more accurate\nestimate\nof the unknown\nparameters.\nWhen\nstaggered\nthruster\npulses\nare used the identification\nprocess\nmay toleratean\ninitial\nparameter\nestimate\nerror of up to 50% of the nominal\nvalues\nfor the lower\nfrequency\nmodes.\nFurther\nresearch\nis needed\nfor the identification\nof modal\ndampings.4,5,\n6\n\n....\n\n.I....\n\nMODE I,\n\n0.129 Hz\n\nMODE 2, 0.163 Hz\n\nMODE 3, 1.172 control design utilizes infinite\ndimensional LQG\ntheory to design compensators that are robust with respect to errors introduced by\nmodel truncation.\nA second error source that requires accommodationare those\nerrors introduced by parameter error.\nAt the present time two approaches have\nbeen developed and validated to improve the robustness characteristics\nof LQG\ncompensators with respect to parameter error.\nThe first approach synthesizes\nstructured uncertainty and loop transfer recovery techniques to achieve a\nsystematic process for making trades between performance and robustness 7. In\nterms of synthesis, the method involves adjusting the weighting matrices in both\nthe regulator and estimator portions of the LQGdesign problem in a way that\nreflects the structure and magnitude of the modeling uncertainties.\nThis\nuncertainty information could be obtained from the covariance analysis provided by\nan on-line identification\nprocess. An alternative approach that has also been\ndeveloped consists of a sensitivity\noptimization of the eigenvalues of the closedloop system8. A nominal LQGcompensator is used to initialize\nthe nonlinear\nprogramming problem associated with the optimization.\nConstraints in the form of\nstability\nmargins are imposed in the optimization to insure adequate trades\nbetween performance and robustness. On the models to which these methods have\nbeen applied, both approaches have yielded substantial improvement in robustness\nover o+o_A_rdLQGcontrollers.\nThe chart below illustrates\nthe overall design methodology, and how the\nsensitivity\noptimization method can increase observer bandwidth in an LQG\ncompensator and simultaneously increase its robustness properties.\nHIGH PERFORMANCE\nDESIGN METHODS\n\n,\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSYSTEM\n\nMODEL\n\nPERFORMANCE\nOBJECTIVES\n\n=\'\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ni\n\nROBUST natural progression of technology development, from analysis and simulation\nthrough laboratory physical test beds to full scale flight experiments, is basic\nto major CSI research initiatives.\nThe COFS\nprogram and the ATSE(Antenna\nTechnology Shuttle Experiment) provide the meansto space test and enable\napplication readiness of advanced identification\nand control technologies.\nThe objectives of the ATSEin identification\nand control have the underlying\ntheme of an integrated system capability to support robust precision\nstabilization/control\nof the MSAT(2nd generation 20-meter dish).\nThis can be\nfurther described by the following criteria\nwhich include operational constraints,\nsystem design limitations,\nin-situ performance maintenance, and synergistic use CATION\n\nX = STRUCTUREDISPLACEMENT\nY = PROOF-MASS DISPLACEMENT\n\n427\n\nSUMMARY\nWe have\npresented\nthe\nmajor\ncomponents\nof a design\nand\nflight\nstrategy\nfor\nflexible\nstructure\ncontrol\nsystems.\nIn\nan initial\ndistributed\nparameter\ncontrol\ndesign\nis developed\nfrom\navailable\nground\ntest\ndata\nand\non-orbit\nidentification\n\noperational\nthis\nstrategy\nand implemented\nusing\nsophisticated\n\nmodeling\nand\nsynthesis\ntechniques.\nThe reliability\nof\nthis\nhigh\nperformance\ncontroller\nis directly\nlinked\nto the\naccuracy\nof\nthe\nparameters\non which\nthe\ndesign\nis\nbased.\nBecause\nuncertainties\ninevitably\ngrow without\nsystem\nmonitoring,\nmaintaining\nthe\ncontrol\nsystem\nrequires\nan active\non-line\nsystem\nidentification\nfunction\nto supply\nparameter\nupdates\nand\ncovariance\ninformation.\nControl\nlaws\ncan\nthen\nbe modified\nto\nimprove\nperformance\nwhen\nthe\nerror\nenvelopes\nare\ndecreased.\nIn terms\nof\nsystem\nsafety\nand\nstability\nthe\ncovariance\ninformation\nis of equal\nimportance\nas the\nparameter\nvalues\nthemselves.\nIf\nthe\non-line\nsystem\n1D function\ndetects\nan increase\nin parameter\nerror\ncovariances,\nthen\ncorresponding\nadjustments\nmust\nbe made\nin the\ncontrol\nlaws\nto\nincrease\nrobustness.\nIn one\nscenario\nfor\nexample,\nif\nthe\nerror\ncovariances\nexceed\nsome\nthreshold,\nan autonomous\ncalibration\nsequence\ncould\nbe initiated\nto restore\nthe\nerror\nenvelope\nto an acceptable\nlevel.\n\nGROUND\n\n428\n\nTESTING\n\nLAUNCH/DEPLOYMENT/\nCALIBRATION\n\nON-LINE\nIDENTIFICATION\n\nREFERENCES\n\nMettler,\n\nI,\n\nE.,\n\nOrbit\n23rd\n\nGibson,\n\n2.\n\nMilman,\n\nAerospace\n\nJ.S.:\n\nD.S.,\nDesign\nin\n\nRadaegh,\nfor\n\nLarge\n\nSpace\n\nHadaegh,\n\nF.,\n\nBayard,\n\nK.W.\non\n\nand\n\nMaine,\n\nR.E.\n\n1985.\n\nConvergence\n\nand\n\nA.\n\nand\n\nFlexible\n\nParameters\non\n\nMettler,\n\nE.:\n\nControl\n\n5,\n\nLikelihood\n\nSIAM\n\nFlexible\nDamping\n\n1986.\nwith\n\nWorkshop\n\nJ.\n\nand\n\nGibson,\n\nAppl.\n\nJ.S.:\nFourth\n\nSystems,\n\nP.A.\n\nand\n\nMingori,\n\nStructures,\n\nference,\n\nNapjus,\n\nParameter\n\nOptimal\n\nSystems-Survey\nVol.\n\nwith\n41,\n\nEmphasis\nIdentifi-\n\nIII,\n\npp.\n\nof\n\nProcess\n\npp\n\nPrac-\n\nand\n\n197-246,\n\nMeasure-\n\n558-578,\n\nAC-19,\n\npp.\n\nof\n\nProc.\n\npp.\n\n162-168,\n\nInput\nand\n\nModified\n\nAugust\n\nDesign\n\nNew\n\n753-768,\n\nSignal\nResults.\n\nClosed-Loop\n\non\n\nof\n\nJune\n\nLTR\n\nand\n\nDistributed\n\n1986.\n\nRobust\n\nControl\n\nControl\n\nfor\n\nNavigation\n\nCon-\n\n1986.\n\nOptimal\nIEEE\n\nEigenvalues\n\nControl\n\nCalifornia,\n\nGuidance,\n\nVA,\n\nG.:\n\nof\n\nSymposium\n\nAngeles,\n\nAIAA\n\nEstimation.\nFlorida,\n\nR.K.:\n\nVol.\n\nImplementation\n\nVol.\n\nSensitivity\n\nD.L.:\n\nWilliamsburg,\n\nMiami,\n\nand\n\nEstimation\nMath.,\n\nIFAC\n\nLos\n\nFlexible\n\nand\n\nFormulation\n\nParameter\n\nStructures,\n\non\n\n1981.\n\nParameter\n\nN.\n\nSpace\n\nthe\n\n1986.\n\nVibration\n\nEstimation\n\nof\n\nof\n\nJune\n\nOn-Orbit\n\nStation.\n\nMarch\n\n1981,\n\nExperiment\n\nBody\n\nSymposium\n\nSpace\n\nProceedings\n\nK.W.:\nfor\n\nRobustness,\n\nBlelloch,\n\nof\n\nlliff,\n\nNoise.\n\nDecember\n\nAdamian,\n\nControl\n\nOptimal\n\nSeptember\n\nCalifornia,\n\nNevada,\n\nMaximum\n\nData.\n\n5,\n\nAngeles,\n\nD.R.,\nfor\n\nNo.\n\nFlexible\n\nIFAC\n\nLos\n\nVegas,\n\nR.E.:\n\nAlgorithm\n\nment\n\nMehra,\n\nOn-\n\n1985.\n\ntical\n\nI0.\n\nJanuary\n\n19,\n\nD.R.:\ This LVDT output was processed\nby an analyzer and produced a typical curve as shown.\nFrom this curve it can\nbe deduced that the device\'s\nresponse\nwas probably\nmore than critically\ndamped.\nTo generate the hysteresis curve a sine wave was impressed on the\nlinear motor amplifier and this voltage along with the LVDT output voltage\nwas\nimpressed on the x and y input channels of a recorder.\nA typical hysteresis curve is also shown.\nIt was noted that as the frequency of excitation\nincreased,\nthe\njerking\nor\nsteplike\ncharacteristic\nof\nthe\nLMED\nhysteresis tended to decrease.\n\nTypical\n\nLMED Frequency Response from Volts\nPower Amp to Volts Out of LVDT\n\ninto\n\n30.000\n--_o.\n\nof averages,\n\n10\n\nlog 1986\n\n441\n\nCffARACTERISTICS\n\ncult\n\nProposed\nlarge\nto analyze\nand\n\nOF\n\nLARGE\n\nSPACE\n\nSYSTEMS\n\nspace structures\nhave many characteristics\ncontrol.\nThey are highly\nflexible\n- with\n\nthat make\ncomponents\n\nthem diffimathemati-\n\ncally\nmodeled\nby partial\ndifferential\nequations\nor very large systems\nof ordinary\ndifferential\nequations.\nThey have many resonant\nfrequencies,\ntypically\nlow and\nclosely\nspaced.\nNatural\ndamping\nmay be low and/or\nimproperly\nmodeled.\nCoupled\nwith\nstringent\noperational\nrequirements\nof orientation,\nshape control,\nand vibration\nsuppression,\nand the inability\nto perform\nadequate\nground\ntesting,\nthese characteristics\npresent\nan unconventional\nidentification\nand control\ndesign\nproblem\nto EDS\ncomputer,\ncalculation\nof the number\nof floation-point\nmultiply\nand add operations\nrequired\nfor a specific\ncase has been made.\nThe assumptions\nof the algorithm\nare\nthat all data are processed\n150 times per second.\nThis may not be required.\nFor\nexample,\nin a modal\ncontroller,\nthe lower frequency\nmode calculations\nmay not\nrequire\ncomplete\nupdating\nat 150 tlmes/sec.\nThe sensor\noutputs\nare assumed\nto be\nfiltered\nby a 6th order ARMA filter.\nTwelve\nsensors\nare assumed.\nThe six rigidbody and eight\nof the flexible\nmodes\nare included\nin the control\nalgorithm.\nThis\nproduces\nfully populated\nmatrices\nwith a state dimension\nof 28.\nEach scalar\noutput\nof the linear\ncontrol\nalgorithm\nis passed\nto a 4th-order,\nARMA form compensator.\nThe time frame used in the calculation\ntaken from a previous\nchart\nis 5033 mlcroseconds\nand 20 percent\noverhead\nis used to represent\noperating\nsystem\nand logic\nrequired\nto process\nthe control\nlaw.\nTo process\nthis algorithm\nunder\nthe assumptions\ndiscussed,\nthe EDS processor\nmust be capable\nof performing\na multiply\nand add\noperation\nin 1.2 microseconds.\nIf only the eight flexible\nmodes\nare used,\nthe\nprocessing\ntime requirement\nreduces\nto 2 microseconds.\n\nALGORITHM\n\nASSUMPTIONS:\n\n150 SAMPLES/SECOND\n12 SENSORS\n6th-ORDER\nFILTERS\n6 local controller.\nThe\nset of charts\nis the excitation\nphase of the simulation\nand the right is the\n\nrun\nleft\n\ncontrolled\nphase.\nThe applied\nforce was generated\nby open-loop\nslnusoidal\nforce\ncommands\nat the frequency\nof the first flexible\nmode.\nThe excitation\nphase lasted\nI0 seconds.\nNo attempt\nwas made to limit or monitor\nmotion\nof the Moving\nMass.\nThis would\nbe required\nfor the flight\nsoftware.\nAt the end of the lO-second\nexcitation\nphase, a collocated\nrate feedback\ncontrol\nlaw assuming\nperfect\nestimation\nwas engaged.\nThe collocated\nrate feedback\ngains were precalculated\nto produce\n5\npercent\ndamping\nin the mode excited.\nThe 15 lowest\nfrequency\nmodes of the MAST\nflight\nsystem were included\nin the simulation.\nThe design\ndamping\nwas indeed\nobtained;\nhowever,\nduring\nthe experiment\napproximately\n1.2 meters\nof relative\nmotion\nof the LDCM\'s was observed.\nIt is indeed\nunfortunate\nthat their travel\nis projected\nto be only 20 CM.\nA study using the local controller\nis in the process\nmade but is not available\nat this time.\nHopefully,\nthe local has proposed\na Baseline\nControl\nLaw software\npackage\nthat will,\nhopefully,\nsupport\nthe research\nof many guest investigators.\nElements\nof the\npackage\nare an Excitation\nand Disturbance\nInjection\nSubsystem,\na Linear\nControl\nAlgorithm\nSybsystem,\nand a Performance\nEvaluation\nSubsystem.\nDefinition\nand\nevaluation\nof the Baseline\nis in progress,\nbut it must be preceeded\nby a precise\ndefinition\nof the LDCM controllers.\nA real-time\nsimulation\nof the MAST flight\nsystem\nis currently\navailable\nat LaRC and will be used with hardware\nin the loop to\nevaluate\nthe Baseline\nand software\nfor potential\nguest\ninvestigators.\nThe hardware\nto be used in the simulation\nare actuators\nsimilar\nto LDCM\'s\nsave that they pAGE\n\nM.\n\nMarinos\n\nCompany\n\nNASA/DOD\n\nTECHNOLOGY\nNorfolk,\n\nG.\n\nCONFERENCE\nVirginia\n\n18-21,\n\nBLANK\n\n1986\n\nNOT\n\nFILMt_\n475\n\nINTRDDUCTIDN\nMany proposed\nfuture\nlarge\nspace\nstructure\ndesigns,\nincluding\nthe NASA\nSpace\nStation,\nmay need to incorporate\nactive\nand/or\npassive\ndamping\nmechanisms\nin order\nto meet pointing,\nslewing,\nor microgravity\nacceleration\nrequirements.\nMethods\nfor\nimplementing\nactive\nand passive\ndamping\nhave been the\nsubject\nof\nmany studies\nwhich\nhave indicated\nthe merits\nof passive\ndamping,\neither\nin\nitself\nor in concert\nwith\nactive\ndamping.\nIncorporation of passive damping for vibration suppression\nin the design\nof large space structures offers many benefits. Passive dampers require no\npower source, are inherently stable, and are potentially\nsimple and reliable.\nProperly\ndesigned\npassive\ndamping\ntreatments\ncan greatly\nreduce\nthe settling\ntime\nin transient\nresponse\nproblems\nand reduce\nthe peaks\nof steady-state\nresponse\nproblems.\nThe existence\nof small\namounts\nof passive\ndamping\nin an\nactive\ncontrol\nsystem\ncan reduce\nactive\ncontrol\neffort\nsuch as actuator\nforce,\nstroke,\nbandwidth,\nand system\npenalties\nsuch\nas the\nnumber of actuators,\nadded\nmass,\ncost,\nand on-board\npower and microprocessing\nneeds.\nPassive\ndamping\ndevices\nalso\nprovide\nan increased\nsafety\nmargin\nfor\nall\nactive\ncontrol\nsystems.\nPassive\ndamping\ncan be added to a structure\nthrough\na variety\nof\nmechanisms\nincluding\nconstrained\nlayer\ntreatments,\nimpact/friction\njoints,\ndiscrete\nviscous\ndampers,\nelectromagnetic\ndevices,\nfluidic\ndevices,\nand\ntuned-mass\ndampers.\nEach of these\ndamping\ntreatments\nperforms\nbest\nfor\ncertain\nclasses\nof damping problems.\nThe tuned-mass\ndamper is especially\nwell-suited\nfor\ndamping\nlarge\nstructures\nwhich\nare characterized\nby low,\nhighly\ndistributed,\nstrain\nenergy,\ne.g.,\nthe NASA Space\nStation.\nAlthough\ninitially\ndubbed a large\nflexible\nspace\nstructure,\nthe NASA I0C Station\nresponse\nto\norbiter\ndocking\nexhibits\nsmall\nloads\nand only\na few inches\nof deflection\nover\nthe distance\nof a baseball\nfield.\nThe corresponding\nlow strains\nmay not be\nenough to efficiently\n"work"\na distributed\ndamping material,\nor a damping\nmaterial\nor device\nplaced\nin the load path.\nThe advantage\nof the tuned-mass\ndamper is that it is "tuned" to draw energy from the main structure to a\nmechanism\nwhich works\nthe\ndamping material\nor damper.\nSome disadvantages\nof the\ntuned-mass\ndamper (also\ntermed\nvibration\nabsorber)\nare that\nit adds\nnonstructural\nmass,\ntypically\nprovides\nonly modest\ndamping\nlevels,\nand does not\ncompensate\nfor changes\nin the plant\ndynamics.\nThis\nwork was supported\nby the National\nAeronautics\nAdministration,\nLangley\nResearch\nCenter,\nContract\nNo.\nHarold\nG. Bush, Technical\nMonitor.\n\n476\n\nand Space\nNASI-I??60,\n\n2-DOF\n\nI_PULSE\n\nRESPONSE\n\nBecause\nof the variety\nof different\nSpace Station\ndisturbances\nand the\ncontinuin\nE evolution\nof the IOC design\nin the Phase\nB program\nit was decided\nto\nmodel the transient\ndisturbances\nusing\nan impulse\ninput.\nThis\nsimpliflcation\nis justified\nby the relatively\nshort\nduration\nof the transient\npulses\nin\ncomparison\nwith\nthe lone\nperiods\nof the dominant\nstructural\nmodes.\nChanges\nin\nthe design\nevolution\nof the Space\nStation\ncould\neasily\nalter\nthe mix of modes\nwhich\nare excited.\nBecause\nthe\nimpulse\ninput\nexcites\nall\nmodes,\nsome of the\ndependence\nof the\nstudy\nconclusions\non a particular\nevolutionary\nconfiguration\nis removed.\nThe absorber\nparameters\nwhich\nare varied\nin this\nstudy\nare the mass M2,\nthe stiffness\nk2, and the damper strength,\n\xc2\xa2.\nBecause\nabsorber\nperformance\nincreases\nwith\nincreasing\nabsorber\nmass\n(until\na saturation\npoint\nis reached),\nthe absorber\nmass is selected\na priori\nbased\non the\navailable\nmass budget.\nIn\nthe analysis\nfor\nthe chart\nbelow,\nthe absorber\nmass is assu=ed\nto be 2% of the\nplant\nmodal\nmass.\nThe value\nof _ which\nmaximizes\nthe\nabsorber/structure\ninteraction\nfor\na mass ratio\nof 2_ is 0.98.\nThe chart\nshows the\nimpulse\nresponse\nenvelopes\n(which\nconnect\nthe peaks\nof the\namplitude\nof the\nsinusoidal\ntransient\nresponse)\nas a function\nof the nondieensional\ndamper coefficient\np.\nThe chart\nshows that\ntoo high\na value\nof p \'locks\'\nthe damper,\nrestraining\nthe\nmotion\nacross\nthe damper and resulting\nin sub-optimal\nperformance.\nSimilarly,\ntoo low a value\nof _ provides\ntoo little\ndamping.\nA value\nof _ between\n.086\nand .11 seems\ndesirable\nfor\nthis\nmass ratio.\n\n/3\n\n=\n\nM2/M\n\nI\n\n6\n\n=\n\nCdA/Cd o\n\n_\n\n=\n\nC/2\n\nM I\xc2\xa20 o\n\n00A\n\n= OF ABSORBER PERFORMANCE WITH STRUCTURAL DAMPING\nThe\n\neffect\n\nof\n\ninherent\n\nstructural\n\ndamping\n\non\n\nabsorber\n\nperformance\n\nis\n\nanalyzed\nusing\nthe two-DOF\nsystem\nshown in the\nchart.\nA non-zero\nvalue\nfor \xc2\xa21\nis used to represent\nthe modal damping\nof the original\nplant\nmode.\nThe\nsettling\ntimes\nto 20_ of the peak impulse\nresponse\nare absorber\nbut with\na structural\ndamping\nlevel\nof 6_.\nSimilar\ninvestigations\nexamined\nthe effect\nof inherent\nstructural\ndamping\non the\napplicability\nof the absorber\ntuning\nlaws FOR\n\nABSORBERS\n\nCONTROL\n\nDESIGN\n\nPROCESS\n\nThe key concepts\nthat\nallow\napplication\nof feedback\ncontrol\ntechniques\nto absorber\ndesign\nare the placement\nof the design\nproblem\nin a linear\nformat,\nand the recasting\nof the combined\nstructure-absorber\ndynamic\nequations\nin a feedback\ncanonical\nform.\nThis\nlinear\nform\nis useful\nbecause\nmost\nof the\ncontrol-theoretic\nresults\napply\nto linear\nsystems\nand the linear\nformat\ngreatly\nsimplifies\nanalysis\nand design.\nThe\nfeedback\ncanonical\nform\nallows\nexpression\nof the absorber\nparameters\nas\ncontroller\ngains\nand provides\na convenient\nmethod\nfor the\nevaluation\nof absorber\nperformance.\nThis\nformulation\nalso\nprovides\nneeded\nvisibility\ninto the absorber\ndesign\nprocess.\n\nCONVERT\n\nNONLINEAR\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDESIGN\n\nOPTIMAL\n-SET\n\nPROBLEM\n\nSOLUTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMASS\n\nPLACEMENT\nTHE\n\n-\n\nMASS\n\nDO\n\nNOT\n\nLINEARIZE\n\nMAXIMIZATION\n\nMAXIMUM\n\nDESIGN\n\nTROUBLESOME\n\n-PICK\nLOCATIONS\nATTACHMENT\n\nPERFORM\n\nABSORBER\n\nAS multipath\nnature\nof the control\nproblem.\nThe control\ndesign\ngain,\nK, appears\nin two inner\nfeedback\nloops,\nand forms\nthe coupling\nmatrix\nbetween\nthe two\nsystems.\nAlthough\nthese\ngains\nseem to be independent\nand appear\nin different\nsystem\nloops,\nin actuality\nthey\nare identical\nparamaters\nthat\nappear\nsimultaneously.\nThis\nimplies\nthat\nparametric\nadjustment\nin one loop yields\nsimultaneous\nadjustment\nin every\nloop containing\nthat\nparameter.\nThis\nproperty\nmakes\ndesign\ndifficult\nand\nillustrates\na basic\ndesign\nlimitation\nof the\nabsorber.\nThe\nouter\nfeedback\nloop\nis positive\nin nature.\nPositive\nfeedback\nloops\nare generally\navoided\nin practice\nbecause\nof reduced\nstability\nmargins\nthat can cause\nsystem-wide\ninstability.\nHowever,\nstability\nconstraints\nare not a concern\nin\nthis\ndesign\nprocess,\nfor the entire\nsystem\nis guaranteed\nto\nremain\nstable\nas the passive\nnature\nof the\nabsorber\nguarantees\nstability.\nThe system\ndynamic\nequations\nare inherently\nstable\nfor all physically\nrealizable\nparameter\nvalues.\n\nP\n\n+\n\nG\n\nG\n\n1\n\nx2\n2\n\n+\n\nK\n\nK\n\nY2\n\nG1\n\n=\n\nMAIN\n\nSYSTEM\n\n(STRUCTURE)\n\nG2\n\n=\n\nABSORBER\n\nK\n\n=\n\nABSORBER\nCOUPLING\n[SPRING\nCONSTANTS\n\nSYSTEM\nPARAMETERS\nAND the linear\nguadratic\nregulator\ncost\nfunctional.\nThe positive\nsemi-definite\nmatrix\nQ\nand positive\ndefinite\nmatrix\nR describe\nthe weighting\nof the\nstate\nand control\nvariables\nin the performance\nindex.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nLQR COST:\n\nJ\n\n=\n\n{_TQn\n\n+ uTRu)\n\ndt\n\n0\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMAY ACCELERATION\n\nSPACE\nSTATIONAPPLICATIONS\nThe optimization\ntechniques described in the previous sections are applied\nto example vibration damping problems on the NASA dual keel configuration\nSpace\nStation.\nTwo example cases are considered which evaluate the capabilities\nof\nthe uncoupled dynamic optimization\nand the parameter optimization\nalgorithms:\n(1) micro-g acceleration response at the lab module, and (2) pointing response\nat a location on the lower payload boom.\nThe disturbance input for both cases\nis a unit impulse at the habitation module.\nThe force input at this location\nsimulates either a shuttle docking or a crew motion disturbance, depending on\nthe strength of the impulse.\nThe inherent structural damping is assumed to be\n0.5_.\nThe chart below illustrates the lOC configuration as of January 1986 which\nis modeled using a finite element code. Two absorbers are employed in each of\nthe example problems, located at the maxima of the two most prominant modes in\nthe transient response.\nThe response locations corresponding\nto the two\nexamples are also shown.\n\nREFERENCE\nTOP\n\nCONFIGURATION\n\n5m\n\nDUAL\n\nKEEL\n\nVIEW\n\n)(\nHAB\n\nLAB\n\\./r,\n\'_ x=\nUN
