BI__\n\ni_,_Oiir:=_\'5_..;\n\nPAGI__IIt-rEN.rlOItKI.\n\n_ _g\n\n495\n\nIntroduction\nThe\n\nCOFS\n\nprogram\n\nhas\n\nsupported\n\nthe\n\ndevelopment\n\nof\n\nan\n\nanalysis\n\ncapability\nat the Langley\nResearch\nCenter\ncalled\nthe Integrated\nMultidisciplinary\nAnalysis\nTool (IMAT)\nwhich provides\nan efficient\ndata\nstorage\nand transfer\ncapability\namong commercial\ncomputer\ncodes to aid in\nthe dynamic\nanalysis\nof actively\ncontrolled\nstructures.\nIMAT is a system\ncomputer\nprograms\nwhich\ntransfers\nComputer-Aided-Design\n(CAD)\nconfigurations,\nstructural\nfinite\nelement\nmodels,\nmaterial\nproperty\nand\nstress\nlinear\n\ninformation,\nstructural\nand\nsystem matrices\nfor control\n\napplications\nformulation,\n\nprograms\nIMAT was\n\nthrough\na\ndeveloped\n\nrigid-body\ndynamic\nmodal\ninformation,\nand\nlaw formulation\namong various\ncommercial\n\ncommon\ndatabase.\nspecifically\nto\n\nAlthough\naid in the\n\ngeneral\nin its\nevaluation\nof the\n\ntransient\ndynamic\nresponse\nand control\nof large flexible\nspace\nstructures.\nThis paper contains\na description\nof the IMAT system\nresults\nof an application\nof the system.\n\no _ Integrated\nIMAT,\nCenter.\n\no IMAT\n\nMultidisciplinary\n\nDeveloped\n\nuses\n\nat\n\nand common\nstructural\n\nbetween\n\ncommercial\n\ninteractive\n\ndatabase\nand controls\n\nto\n\na growth\nreviewed.\n\nto\nversion\n\nthe\n\nand\n\nTool\n\n_,\n\nResearch\n\nuser\n\nstore\nand\ninformation\n\ncodes.\n\nIMAT\ndevelopment\nsupported\nby\nprogram\nfor application\nto large\nspacecraft\nunder\nactive\ncontrol.\n\no Application\n\nreference\nof\n\nthe\n\nFigure\n\n496\n\nLangley\n\nunder\n\ncontrol\ntransfer\n\nO\n\nAnalysis\n\nNASA\n\nexecutive\n\nof\n\nspace\n\ni\n\nthe COFS\nflexible\n\nspace\nstation\n\nstation\nwill\n\nand\nbe\n\nDescription of IMAT\nIMAT consists of an assemblage of pre- and post- processors which,\nunder the control of an interactive executive system, convert and transfer\ninformation between application programs through a relational\ndatabase\nmanagement rogram. A simplified schematic of the IMATsystem is shown in\np\nFig. 2. A commercial database manager, RIM (Boeing Commercial Services\nInc.), forms the basis for storage and distribution\nof data using a database\nschemawhich accepts finite element model information, stiffness and mass\nmatrices, linear system matrices for control studies, and analysis results\nsuch as displacements, stresses, and natural modesand frequencies.\nAll\ndata are stored in a generic format, which insures that the data can be\nshared amongdifferent application programs. These data are loaded into or\nextracted from the database by processors, written in FORTRAN,\nwhich format\nthe data for specific applications programs. All input and output of data\nare controlled interactively\nby the researcher using a menudriven executive\ncode. The executive control procedures, formulated to direct the transfer\nand storage of information, are complicated by the fact that the commercial\napplications programs involved in the creation or use of information reside\nand operate on computers of three different manufacturers, Control Data\nCorp., Digital Equipment Corp., and PRIME\nInc, each with its own unique\noperating system. All computer systems are connected by a local area\nnetwork which can transfer data at approximately 250,000 baud.\n\nI\n\nAIDED\nCOMPUTER\nDES I GN\n\nELEMENT\nFINITE\nMODELING\n\nI\nI\n\nI\n\nIMAT\nRIM\nDATA BASE\n\n$TRUCTURAL\n\nI\n\n/\nCONTROLS\nSOFTWARE\nINTERFACE\n\nI\n\nANALY51S\n\nf\n\nI\n\nFigure\n\n2\n\n497\n\nCAD, Finite\n\nElement Modeling and Analysis\n\nFig. 3 describes the codes and data paths available for computer-aided\ndesign and finite-element\nmodeling. Wireframe geometry developed using the\ncommercial computer-aided-design code ANVIL (MCSinc.) is converted to input\nfor the commercial finite-element modeling code PATRAN-G\n(PDAInc.).\nA\nprocessor stores finite-element model information from PATRAN-G the\nin\ndatabase. The second commercial CADcapability available is the 3-D\nwireframe CADsystem CALMA\nDDM(GE/CAEInc.), which can transfer geometry\ninformation to GEOMOD\n(SDRC\nInc.), a solid modeling code. This latter code\ncan transfer three dimensional information to SUPERTAB\n(SDRC\nInc.), a\nfinite-element modeling code similar to PATRAN-G.A processor similar to\nthat installed between PATRAN-G the database has been developed to\nand\ntransfer a finite-element model description from SUPERTAB\ninto the database.\nEntry into IMAT can occur at several different levels:\nat the CADor\nmodeling level or simply by loading finiteelement model data prepared\nelsewhere through a processor into the database. Two commercial finiteelement solver codes are available: MSC/NASTRAN\n(MacNeal Schwendler Corp.)\nand EAL (Engineering Information Systems Inc.).\nThe finite-element\ndata\ncreated by PATRAN or SUPERTAB stored in a generic format in the\nG\nare\ndatabase. Processors have been developed which can create an MSC/NASTRAN\nbulk data input file or an EAL input file.\nProcessors have also been\ndeveloped which can convert an MSC/NASTRAN data input stream to an\nbulk\nequivalent EAL bulk-data input stream and vice-versa.\nStatic and dynamic\nresults obtained from the analysis codes are placed in the database, and are\navailable for inspection or use in performing dynamic analyses or generating\nlinear system matrices for controls analyses.\n\nAMAL_\nOAT\n\nUSC/NASTRAI\n_INITE\n\nELEMENT\n\nPROGRAM\n\nFigure\n498\n\n:INITE\n\n3\n\nELEMENT\n\nPROGRAM\nJ\n\nLinear SystemMatrix Generator\nFig. 4 is a schematic of the interactive capability provided in IMAT\nfor generating linear system matrices for a controls design or analysis.\nThe controls researcher has access to the modal, mass, inertia, and sensor\nand control influence information through the database interrogation\ncapability.\nThe researcher can use the information to evaluate the\nsignificant\ndynamic modes, select the modesand frequencies of interest used\nto define the state of the system and obtain the mass and inertia matrices\nnecessary for development of linear system matrices.\nThe user must provide\nsensor and actuator information as to type and location and must provide a\nmodal damping value for each modeselected to define the state.\nA processor\ncreates the system matnices which are then stored in the database or are fed\ndirectly to a commercial control design and analysis code called MATRIX\nx\n(Integrated Systems Inc.).\nThe controls analyst, external to the IMAT\nsystem, develops control laws, and creates state feedback matrices which are\nstored in the IMATdatabase.\n\nINTERACTIVE\nRIM\nData\nstorage\n2,\n\n0\n\ny\n\nProcessor\nforms\ncontrols\ndatabase\n(rim)\n\nUser selects\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Modes of interest\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Sensor information\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Actuator information\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Modal damping\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI Controls engineer\ndesigns control\nI\n[\ngains\n\nProcessor\nforms\nlinear\nsystem\nmatrices\n\nI\nP"-I\nI\n\nProcessor\nstores\nmatrices\nin database\nor inputs\nto matrix x\n\nFigure\n\n499\n\nDynamic Simulation in IMAT\nWith the control law and the natural modesand frequencies available in\nthe database, the structural dynamicist can use the IMAT capabilities\nto\ninvestigate the dynamic closed-loop transient response of the structure to\nexternal disturbances and the control actuator forces.\nThe user defines the\ninformation required for his state space matrices including disturbance\nlocations and forces and any structural grid points of interest.\nThe system\nmatrices are computed and transferred to MATRIX x . The time domain solution\nprocedures\nof MATRIX x are exercised\nto solve for the state\nvector\namplitudes\nas a function\nof time.\nIMAT creates\na solution\nfile and transports\nthe file\nto MSC/NASTRAN.\nThe user can then call the old problem\ntape with the\nphysical\nmodel\ninformation\nand the solution\nfile and use the MSC/NASTRAN\noutput\nroutines\nto compute\nphysical\ninformation\nsuch as stress\nand strain\nas\na function\nof time in the elements\nof interest.\n\nRim\n\nMatrix\n\nInteractive\n\nstorage\nof\n\nData\nw2, _o\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 the\nelastic\nresponse\nof the system.\nIMAT was used to define\na finite\nelement\nrepresenation\nof the station\nand determine\nand store\nthe modes\nand\nfrequencies\npreparatory\nto performing\ndynamic\nand control\nstudies.\nThe\nresults\nof a study of the expected\ndynamic\ncharacteristics\nof the reference\nstation,\ni.e.\nnatural\nmodes and frequencies,\nare presented\nhere as an\nexample\nof the application\nof IMAT and to present\nthe frequency\nand mode\ncharacteristics\nof interest\nin the COFS 3 studies.\n\nO\n\nReference\nbaseline\ncriteria\n\no external\ndisturbances.\n\nFigure\n\n6\n\n501\n\nReference Dual-Keel Space Station\nThe reference station is a dual-keel station with a transverse boom\nsupporting two types of solar power systems, photovoltaic and solar dynamic.\nThe work area of the station is located at the center of the transverse boom\nand has four life supporting modules, the habitation, laboratory, European,\nand Japanesemodules. The upper boomcontains stellar experiment payloads\nand the lower boomcontains earth resource payloads. Station attitude\ncontrol is provided by control momentgyros located in the first bay of the\nport transverse boomoutboard of the keel. Position and rate sensors are\nalso located in this bay but not co-located with the gyros. Reboost control\nis provided by four Reaction Control Systempackages with nine 25 ibf jets\nin each package. The packages are located on the upper and lower keels.\n\ny\n\nZ\n\nFigure\n\n502\n\n7\n\nFinite\n\nElement Representation\n\nThe finite element model is composedof 1312 rod elements and 98 beam\nelements with 483 nodes and approximately 1700 dynamic degrees of freedom.\nEach element of the trusswork is represented by a rod element and the\nmodules are represented by beamelements with the equivalent stiffness\nproperties of the module cylinders and connecting components. The truss\nmembersare 2.0 inch outside diameter graphite epoxy tubes with a 0.06 inch\nwall thickness and a longitudinal modulus of elasticity\nof 40.0 x 106 psi.\nAn approximation of the stiffness characteristics of truss joints is\nincluded in the stiffness of the rod elements by reducing the modulus of\nelasticity\nfor rod element based on the length of the rod element.\n\nY\n\n.4\ni\n\ni\nI\nV\n\nz\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ni\n\nFigure\n\n8\n\n503\n\nMass\n\nalong\n\nInertia\n\nCharacteristics\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nReference\n\nStation\n\nmass\n\nThe\nfigure.\ncentral\n\nand\nand\n\ninertia\n\ncharacteristics\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nmodel\n\ngiven\n\nare\n\nThe Growth\n\nStation\n\nof a growth\n\nversion\n\nof\n\nthe\n\ndual-keel\n\nspace\nstation\nduring\nan orbital\nreboost\nmaneuver.\nBecause\nof atmospheric\ndrag,\nthe space\nstation\nmust be reboosted\nperiodically\nto maintain\nits\ndesired\noperational\norbit.\nThis maneuver\nis performed\nusing\nfour constantthrust\nreactioncontrol-system\n(RCS) jets.\nThe purpose\nof the control\nsystem\nis to maintain\nstation\nattitude\nduring\nthe maneuver.\nIn the current\nstudy\nthe stability\nof the attitude\ncontrol\nsystem\nduring\nreboost\nand the\nelastic\nresponse\nof the Model of the Space Station\n\nThe growth space station configuration studied has over twice the mass\nof the reference station described above and is described in ref. I which\ngives a complete description of the masses and locations of the payloads,\npower systems, heat rejection radiators, subsystems, and the structural\nproperties and dimensions of the truss componentsand support structures.\nThe control sensors are located at the origin of the coordinate system at\nthe center of the center bay. Four RCSjets are located on the keel structure\nto provide reboost propulsion and attitude control.\nTo determine the natural\nmodesand frequencies of the space station framework, a detailed finite\nelement model of the station was developed using EAL. The model is shown in\nFig. 13 and details of the model and the modesand fequencies are given in\nref.1_\nThe finite-element model with 735 nodes connected by 235 beamsand\n2110 rods had 2238 dynamic degrees of freedom.\n\ne\n\n735 NODES\n\ne\n\n2238\n\nDYNAMIC\n\nDEGREES\n\ne 235 BEAM ELEMENTS\ne 2110 TRUSS ELEMENTS\n\nFigure\n\n5O8\n\n13\n\nOF\n\nFREEDOM\n\nReboost Control Law\nFour 75-pound, constant-thrust RCSjets are located with two jets above\nand two jets below the center of mass of the station on the keels. The jets\nare aligned with the x-axis of the station so that the thrust is in the\norbital direction.\nThe upper- and lower-keel RCSthrusters are located at\ndifferent distances along the y-axls from the station center of mass. Thus,\na reboost maneuverwith all thrusters active would produce an unbalanced\nmomentabout the y-axis, causing the station to rotate away from its\nvertical position.\nThe station attitude is kept within desired limits by\nintermittent\nfiring of the lower- keel RCS\njets.\nThe control logic which\ngoverns the firing of the lower-keel jets is given in Fig. 14. The logic is\ndesigned to hold the pitch attitude of the station within approximately one\ndegree from the local vertical while applying the desired velocity increment\nalong the flight path. The error signal (E) used to control the jet firing\nis a proportional-pluSdifferential\nsignal consisting of pitch and pitch\nrate, E = e + K8, measured at the sensor location.\nTwo control studies\nY\nY\nwere performed. The first study assumedthat the error signal contained\nonly rigid body motion, while the second study assumedthat the error signal\ncontained both the rigid body motion and the local flexible response at the\nsensor location.\nFollowing the control logic on Fig. 14, all jets are\nactive until the control error reaches 1.0 degree plus a 0.05 degree\nhysteresis.\nAt this point the lower jetS are switched off and the station\nrotation decelerates and eventually reverses direction.\nWhenthe error\nsignal returns to 1.0 degree the lower jets are switched on and the process\nrepeats as required_\n\nTorque\n\nHyteresis\n\nI\nI\nAll\njets\nON\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nE =\n\nUpper\n\n-.4\n\nJets\nON\n\nOy\n\n+\n\nKSy,\n\ndeg\n\n%._\nr\n\nI\nI\nDeadband\n\n------------lm" I\nI\nI\n\nFigure\n\n14\n\n509\n\nControl Stability\nThe stability\nof the reboost control system is illustrated\nvs. pitch rate phase-plane plots of Fig. 15 considering rigid\nonly and with both rigid-body and flexibleresponse\nincluded.\nspiral of the plots indicates that both methods of error signal\nlead to a stable control system during the time investigated.\n\non the pitch\nbody motion\nThe inward\nmeasurement\n\n.os\n\nI\n\n-.o5\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\n0\n\nI I\n.S\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\nOYI_\'\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI I\nI .O\n\ni\n\nI\n\nI\']\n\ni\n\ni\n\ni\n\nl\n\ni\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\ni\n\n1\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n| .S\n\nleg\n\n.os\n\nJ,\n\nr\n-.OS FI\n0\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nq I\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\n1.0\n\n0\xc2\xa51 _\n\n+ e:lrtle=,\n\nFigure\n\n510\n\nI\n\n,5\n\n15\n\ndeg\n\ni\n\nI\n\nI.S\n\nSolar DynamicSystem\nThe station is powered by eight solar dynamic systems located on\nsupport structures attached to the transverse beams. A typical solar dynamic\nsystem is shown in Fig. 16. For maximum\nefficiency,\nthe direction of the\nsymmetry axis of each Of the solar dynamic systems must be held to within\n0.1 degree of the solar vector, even during an orbital reboost maneuver.\nThls is done by controllers which command\nrotary joints located on the\ntransverse truss and a vernier joint attached to the reflector symmetry\naxis.\nFor the current study, these controllers are assumedinactive.\nOf\nparticular interest in the study was the elastic response of the outboard\nsolar dynamic system support structure and its effect on the pointing\naccuracy of the solar dynamic unit.\nAccordingly, no attempt was madeto\ndefine in detail the structure of the solar dynamic system itself or to\nconsider local dynamic response of the componentsof the systems. The\nsystem was represented instead by a rigid frame with the total mass and mass\nmomentsof inertia assigned on the frame at the center of mass of each\nsystem component. To simplify the problem, the sun-line vector and the xaxis are assumedto be coincident, although in actual practice the sun-line\nvector is continually rotating at orbital frequency.\n\nconcentrator/\nconverter\n\noffset\nsupport\nstructure\n\nI\n\nrotary\n\nFigure\n\n\\i \\\n\\\n\njoint\n\n16\n\n511\n\nSolar\n\nDynamic\n\nSystem\n\nResponse.\n\nShown in Fig. 17 is the flexible\ncomponent\nof the rotation\nof an\noutboard\nsolar dynamic\nsystem\nsymmetry\naxis during\nthe first 500 seconds\nan orbital\nreboost\nmaneuver.\nThe responses\nshown are for a rigid body\ncontrol\nswitching\nlogic.\nBecause\nof the one-degree\ndeadband\nrequirement\nthe control\nlogic,\nthe total\ny-axis\npitch always\nexceeds\nthe 0.1 degree\n\nof\nin\n\npointing\nrequirement.\nHowever,\nif the rigid body pitch angle were known,\nit\ncould\nbe nulled\nusing\nthe rotary\njoints\non the transverse\nboom of the space\nstation.\nThe higher\nfrequency\nflexible\nrotation\nresponse\nis well within\nthe\n0.1 degree rotation\nlimit\nimposed\nfor efficient\noperation\nof the solar\ndynamic\nsystem.\nA more complete\ndiscussion\nof the control\nanalysis\nis given\nin reference\n2.\n\nm\n\nM\nU\nm\n\nN\n0\n\nm\n\n-.I\n0\n\n100\n\n200\n\n300\n\nTime,\n\nFigure\n\n512\n\nsec.\n\n17\n\n400\n\n_00\n\nConcluding Remarks\nThe paper describes a computerized data distribution\ncapability,\nIMAT,\nin place at the NASALangley Research Center for the multidisciplinary\nanalysis of the dynamics and control of large flexible space structures.\nThe paper includes results obtained in using IMAT to investigate the dynamic\ncharacteristics\nof the NASADual-Keel Reference Space Station and the\ninfluence of structural response of the space station framework on the\ncontrol of a growth version of the station during an orbital reboost\nmaneuver. The method of control, using a proportional-plus-differential\ncontrol law, led to a stable control system even with local flexible\nresponse measured at the control sensor location included as a part of the\ncontrol error signal.\nThe flexible response at the outboard solar dynamic\nsystem sun-line axiswas well within the maximum\nrotation allowed for\nefficient\noperation.\nThe IMAT system can be used to support the COFS\nprogram by providing an efficient meansfor simulating response to given\nlinear control laws for the MASTstructure in COFS and to develop and\nI\nanalyze a scaled space station model for COFS\nIII.\n\n513\n\nReferences\nI.\nDorsey,\nJohn T.; Sutter,\nThomas\nR.; Lake, Mark S.\nand\nDynamic\nCharacteristics\nof Two 300 KW Class\nDual Keel Space\nConcepts.\nNASA TM-87680,\nMarch\n1986.\n2.\nYoung, John W.; Lallman,\nDaniel\nP.: Controls/Structures\n\nFredrick\nJ.;\nInteraction\n\nand Giesy,\nKW Dual-Keel\n\nSpace\n\n514\n\nCooper,\nStation\n\nTM-87679,\n\nStation\n\nConcepts.\n\nNASA\n\nCooper,\nPaul\nStudy of Two\n\nMarch\n\n1986.\n\nA.\n300\n\nPaul\n\nA.:\n\nN87-\n\nANALYSIS\n\nAND\n\nLucas\n\nSIMULATION\n\nG.\n\nHorta,\nNASA\n\nOF\n\nTHE\n\nJoanne\nLangley\n\nMAST\n\n(COFS-I\n\nL. The\nprogram i n c l u d e s t h e d e s i g n and f a b r i c a t i o n of 60 meter deployable t r u s s\nw i t h sensors and a c t u a t o r s a t t a c h e d a t p r e - s e l e c t e d l o c a t i o n s . The s t r u c t u r e\nw i l l b e used i n a m u l t i - f l i g h t experimental program t o understand t h e dynamic\nbehavior of joint-dominated structures, t o s u p p l y a test bed f o r t h e\n\nimplementation of modern c o n t r o l a l g o r i t h m f o r v i b r a t i o n s u p p r e s s i o n , and t o\nperform on/off l i n e system i d e n t i f i c a t i o n .\nT h i s paper p r e s e n t s some o f t h e in-house a n a l y s i s work performed t o\ne v a l u a t e the proposed d e s i g n c o n f i g u r a t i o n , c o n t r o l l e r d e s i g n as well as\na c t u a t o r dynamic modeling, and MAST/actuator dynamic s i m u l a t i o n f o r e x c i t a t i o n\nand damping. The MAST is modeled u s i n g t h e Engineering Analysis Language\n(EAL, ref. 3 ) f i n i t e element a n a l y s i s program. An o p t i m i z a t i o n procedure\nwhich minimizes weight w h i l e t r y i n g t o m a i n t a i n modal c o u p l i n g is d i s c u s s e d .\nT h i s o p t i m i z a t i o n procedure f a c i l i t a t e s i n c o r p o r a t i o n of a d d i t i o n a l\nc o n s t r a i n t s n o t considered i n t h e MAST b a s e l i n e design. considered\nin the baseline\ndesign\n- namely,\nthat the first\nbending\nfrequency\nof the diagonal\nmust be at\nleast\n15 Hz.\nThe optimization\nprocedure\ncombines\na general\npurpose\noptimization\nprogram\n(CONMIN,\nref. 4) with the EAL finite\nelement\nprogram.\nThe problem\nformulation\nis shown\nin figure\n4.\nThe objective\nis to minimiZe\nthe total mass of the system.\nThe four design\nvariables\nare the inner radii\nof\n\nthe\n\nouter\n\nstrong\nradii\n\nand\nof\n\nthe\n\nweak\n\nlongeron\n\ndiagonal\n\n(R S and\n\n(R I Hz (a Shuttle\nrequirement);\nand the second\nbending\nfrequencies\nmust be within\nI percent\n\nfrequency\nthe first\n(referred\n\nof the\ntorsion\nto as\n\nmodal\ncoupling);\nthe first bending\nfrequency\nof the diagonal\nmust be at least\n15 Hz (to simulate\na buckling\nconstraint);\nthe inner radius\nof the weak\nlongeron\nmust be at least 0.25 mm larger\nthan the inner radius\nof the strong\nlongeron\n(to ensure\nthat the weak and strong\nlongerons\ndo not change);\nand the\nminimum\nwall thickness\nof the diagonal\nmust be at least 0.56 mm.\nThe lower\nbound\nfor all the design\nvariables\nis 3.175 mm and the upper bound\nis 9.4 mm\nwith\nthe exception\nof the diagonal\nouter radius\nwhich\nis 11 mm.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nOBJECTIVE several design issues that need to be\naddressed. Physically, the actuator is an electromechanical device which can\nbe commanded a number of different ways. Using the actuator representation\nin\nshown in figure 8 (the structure deflection is denoted by Wwhereas the\nactuator position is defined by Y), the equations of motion are as sho_-n. The\ndesigner must decide how the actuators will be used and, moreover, what type\nof command\ninput the actuator will require.\nFor the system shown, Fa is the\nforce applied to the structure which is balanced by accelerating the actuator\nmass in the opposite direction.\nWhencommanded force, the actuator mass\na\nshould accelerate until the desired force is achieved (assuming no stroke\nlimits).\nIf commanded position, the actuator will move either relative to\na\nthe structure or with respect to an inertial reference. Deciding how the\nactuator is used determines instrumentation needs. Moreover, deciding how it\noperates (position of force command) etermines the actuator representation.\nd\nThe following work presents one of the available options.\n\nRepresentation\n\nIssues\n\n_L_--w --\'{_Actuator\nY\nStructure ]\n"_ , ,, ,, ,, /v zz511\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Position Command\nz\n\nEquatiOns of Motion\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Force Command\n\nStructure\nM_+kW=Fa\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Actuator Representation\n\nActuator\nmg=-Fa\n\nFigure\n\n8\n\n525\n\nDYNAMIC\n\nThe\npresented\nmain\n\ndynamic\nmodel\nin references\n\ncomponents.\n\nof the\n5 and\n\nThe\n\nMODEL\n\nactuator\n6.\nThe\n\nactuator\n\nmass\n\nis\n\n(back EMF) Cp, and the commanded\nforce\nbetween\nthe actuator\nand the structure.\nrepresented\n\nas\n\na single\n\noperation\n\nof\n\ncommanded\n\na constant\n\n(ideally).\nmass back\noperation\nfrequency\ninto\n\ntwo\n\nsuch\n\nIn\n\nspring-mass\n\na device,\n\nthis\n\nforce,\ndynamic\n\nif\n\nK\n\nthe\n\ndefined\nbasic\n\nas\n\nUp\n\nregions.\n\nused in this study\nschematic\nin figure\ndenoted\nfg.\nIn\n\nsystem\n\nthe\n\nby\n\nmp,\n\nis based on\n9 shows the\n\nstiffness\n\nKp,\n\nThe commanded\nforce\nthis schematic,\nthe\nM s and\n\ncentering\n\nKs .\n\nLooking\n\nthe work\nactuator\n\ndamping\n\nacts directly\nstructure\nis\nat\n\nthe\n\nspring\n\nKp\n\nforces\n\nthe\n\nactuator\n\nSince such a spring\ndoes not exist,\nthis\nlocal position\nfeedback.\nThe operational\n\nP divides\nThis\n\nACTUATOR\n\nand c\nare zero and the actuator\nis\nP\nP\nactuator\nmass will accelerate\nin one direction\n\nmodel\n\nto a nominal\nposition.\nis to be performed\nby\n\nOF\n\nwill\n\nbe\n\nthe\n\noperationalrange\n\ndiscussed\n\nin\n\nthe\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nactuators\n\nfollowing.\n\nf\ng\n\n#-h\nc\nm\n\nMS\n\nFigure\n\n526\n\nP\n\n9\n\nACTUATOR\nFREQUENCY\nRESPONSE\nThe dynamic characteristics\nof the actuator model are better described by\nthe frequency response plots shown in figure 10. These frequency response\nfunctions were obtained from a bench test of the actuator reported in\nreference 5. Although this actuator design is different from the LDCM\ndesign,\nthey are conceptually similar.\nMoreover, the actuator dynamic model can be\nassumedthe samefor simulation.\nThe upper plot shows the transfer function\nof the total actuator output force to the input commandedoltage. Note that\nv\nthe commandedoltage can be equal to any desired control.\nv\nThe frequency\nresponse function shown resembles that of a second-order high pass filter\nwhere above the operational frequency the response is "independent" of\nfrequency. Moreover, above the operational frequency, defined in the dynamic\nmodel as mp, the output force follows the input command\n(see phase plot)\nwhereas below mp the output is out-of-phase with the input. This is extremely\nimportant when the actuator is used for control.\nFor example, if flexible\nstructural modesexist on both sides of m and the commanded\ninput contains\nP\nrate information from multiple modes, then in the lower modesthe actuator is\nadding energy whereas in the higher modesit is removing energy. The\noperational frequency parameter is an adjustable parameter to someextent by\nadjusting Kp. This actuator model is used for the MASTdynamic simulation.\n\n527\n\nACTUATOR\n\nFREQUENCY\n\n-8O\n\nRESPONSE--MAGNITUDE\n\nIllli\nIIII\n\n-6O\n\nrt\nIIII\n\nA\n\nIIIII/\n\n(:13\n\n-40\n\n/\n\nP\n\nt"\n\nE\n\xc2\xa2o\n\n\\\n\n-2O\n\n_11\nII]_\n\n\\\n\n0.1\n\n0\n\n1.0\n\n.L_\nII1(I\nIIIII\nIIII1\nIIIII\nIIIII\n\nwp\n\n10. 0\n\nFrequency (Hz)\n\nACTUATOR\n\nFREQUENCY\n\nRESPONSE--PHASE\n\n180,\nI\n\nv_\n\nI\n\n9O\n\nI\nI.\n\ni\n\ni\nI\n\nI\nI\n\n!\nI/\nV\n\n/\noi\n\ni\n\n/\'\n\n:\n\ni\n\nO3\n\n!\n\n(-.\nO-\n\ni\nI\n\n-90\n\n-180-\n\ni\nI\n\ni\nI\n\n!\n\n0.1\n\n1.0\nFrequency (Hz)\n\nFigure\n\n528\n\nI0\n\nCUp\n\n10.0\n\nMASTDYNAMIC\nSIMULATION\nThe dynamic model of the actuator is incorporated into a reduced order\nmodel of the MAST. The\nwork involves evaluation of the proposed design configuration, controller\ndesign as well as actuator dynamic modeling, and MAST/actuator dynamic\nsimulation for excitation and damping. A complete finite element model of the\nMAST\nhas been developed. This finiteelement\nmodel has been incorporated into\nan optimization procedure which minimizes total mass while maintaining modal\ncoupling. Results showan increase in the total mass due to additional\nconstraints (namely, the diagonal frequency constraint) imposed on the\nbaseline design. A valid actuator dynamic model is presented and a complete\ntest sequence of the proposed flight experiment is demonstrated. The actuator\ndynamic model is successfully used for damping and the stroke limitations\nfor\nfirst modeexcitation are demonstrated. Plans are to incorporate additional\ndesign variables and constraints into the optimization procedure (such as\nactuator location) and explore alternative formulations of the objective\nfunction.\nA different actuator dynamic model to include hardware limitations\nwill be Investigated.\n\nSUMMARY\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMAST\n\nFINITE\n\nELEMENT\n\nMODEL\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nOPTIMIZATION\nPROCEDURE\nTO INCORPORATE\nDESIGN\nCONSTRAINTS\nIS DEMONSTRATED\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nACTUATOR\nDYNAMIC\nMODEL\nEXCITATIONAND\nDAMPING\n\nSUCCESSFULLY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCOMPLETE\nTEST SEQUENCE\nEXPERIMENT\nDEMONSTRATED\n\nOF\n\nFUTURE\n\nGENERATED\n\nTHE\n\nADDITIONAL\n\nUSED\n\nPROPOSED\n\nFOR\n\nFLIGHT\n\nPLANS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAUGMENT\nTHE OPTIMIZATION\nPROCEDURE\nPARAMETER\nMODIFICATION\nPACKAGE\nAND\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nINVESTIGATE\nTHE\nACTUATOR\nDYNAMIC\n\nPERFORMANCE\nMODEL\n\nOF\n\nTO INCLUDE\nACTUATORS\n\nA DIFFERENT\n\n531\n\nR EF ER ENC ES\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.\n\no\n\nHanks,\nB.R.:\n"Control\nof Flexible\nStructures\n(COFS)\nFlight\nExperiment\nBackground\nand Description,"\nLarge Space Antenna\nSystems\nTechnology\n1984.\nNASA CP-2368,\nPart 2, December\n1984, pp. 893-902.\nHorner,\nG. BASE AND STOP BLOCK ASSEMBLIES\n\nmG!NAL\nPOOR QUALrrV\n\nEach of the surface c o n t r o l cords and the l o w e r hoop cords pass through the\ns t o p blocks on t h e antenna base assembly ( f i g u r e 6). I n t h e stowed conf i g u r a t i o n , t h e c o r d s are wound o n t o drums under t h e base p l a t e . As t h e antenna\ni s deployed, the c a b l e s unwind from t h e drums and f e e d through t h e stop blocks.\nWhen t h e cords reach f u l l e x t e n s i o n , a bead which is bonded t o each cord i s\nengaged i n t h e stop block ( f i g u r e 7). The space a v a i l a b l e f o r t h e automatic\ns u r f a c e adjustment mechanism is l i m i t e d t o t h e area occupied by t h e stop block\na s s e m b l i e s and t h e a r e a under t h e base p l a t e .\n\nSTOP BLOCK ASSEMBLY\n\nAntenna B a s e\n\nFigure 6\n\nFigure I\n\n539\n\nSURFACE CONTROL SYSTEM ACTUATOR ASSEMBLY\n\nThe s u r f a c e c o n t r o l system c o n s i s t s of t h e 2 8 cord p o s i t i o n c o n t r o l\na c t u a t o r s l o c a t e d i n 7 a c t u a t o r a s s e m b l i e s , and t h e c o n t r o l e l e c t r o n i c s . Each\nof t h e a c t u a t o r assemblies c o n t a i n s f o u r motor d r i v e n a c t u a t o r s and a modified\ns t o p block assembly.\nEach a c t u a t o r c o n s i s t s of a t o r q u e motor, gearhead, i n c r e mental encoder, brake, a r c s e c t o r / e n c o d e r , a c t u a t o r cable and a spring-loaded\np o s i t i o n c o n t r o l p i s t o n ( f i g u r e 8).\nP o s i t i o n commands from t h e h o s t computer are compensated f o r v a r i a t i o n s i n\na c t u a t o r cable s t r a i n and c o n v e r t e d t o arcsector p o s i t i o n commands. The\np o s i t i o n of t h e a r c s e c t o r i s sensed by t h e encoder a t t a c h e d t o t h e motor s h a f t\nand referenced t o a simple a b s o l u t e p o s i t i o n s e n s o r which i s i n c o r p o r a t e d i n t h e\na r c s e c t o r assembly. The c o n t r o l l e r d r i v e s t h e t o r q u e motor t o p o s i t i o n t h e arcs e c t o r t o the commanded p o s i t i o n .\nThe a c t u a t o r c a b l e which is a t t a c h e d t o t h e\na r c s e c t o r p o s i t i o n s t h e spring-loaded c o n t r o l p i s t o n which i n t u r n p o s i t i o n s the\na c t u a t o r s u r f a c e c o n t r o l cord. The c o n t r o l p i s t o n c a p t u r e s t h e s u r f a c e c o n t r o l\nc o r d bead i n t h e same manner as i n t h e o r i g i n a l s t o p block assembly. The b r a k e\ni s used t o prevent back d r i v i n g of t h e system when t h e t o r q u e motor i s n o t\nenergized.\n\nBlock\nControl C o r d\nSpring\n\n7 iston\nP\n\nI\n\nI\n\nAntenna B a s e\n\nTorque Motor\n\n-\n\nArcsectorlEncoder\n\nBrake\n\nACTUATOR ASSEMBLY\nFigure 8\n\n540\n\nSURFACE\nCONTROL\nSYSTEMLECTRONICS\nE\nThe control electronics consist of seven station controllers and a system\ninterface unit (figure 9). The system is interfaced to the host via a MIL STD1553Bserial communications bus. The seven station controllers are connected\nto the system interface via an IEEE-488 general purpose parallel interface\nbus. The dual bus structure was selected to provide ease of bridge\nto\n\nthe\n\noperation\n\nprovides\nof\n\nthe\n\ncircuit\nPWM\n\nthe\n\nthe\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nencoder\n\ncord\n\nand\n\nprovides\n\ncurrent\n\nmonitors\n\nthe\n\ncord\n\nand\n\nrecovery.\n\ncompute\n\nproduce\n\na\n\nposition/\n\npulse\n\nThe\n\nmotor\n\ndrive\n\nto\n\nwidth\ndrive\n\nthe\n\nmotor\n\nmoducircuit\nwhich\n\ncommand.\n\nCONTROL\n\nSTATION\n\nCord\nController\n\nI\n\ninterprets\ncollects\n\ndetection\n\nposition,\n\ncircuit.\n\nmicrocontroller,\n\nstrain,\n\nbrake,\nerror\n\ndrive\n\na\n\nmicrocontroller\n\nfor\n\nsignals,\n\nmotor\n\nwhich\n\ninterface,\n\nThe\n\noperational\n\nerror\n\nto\n\nbus\n\n11).\n\ncompensation\n\ntrack\n\ncompensate\ncommand\n\nIEEE-488\n\n(figure\n\ncontrols\n\nlimits,\n\ndrive\n\nan\n\ndrivers\n\ndata,\n\ndata,\n\nload\n\ncontrollers\n\n(PWM)\n\ncontains\n\nand\n\nCord\nActuator\n\nI\nCord\nController\n\nCord\nActuator\n\nMicrocontroller\n\nCord\nController\n\nCord\nLoad\n\nCells\n\nController\n\nFigure\n\nCord\nActuator\n\nCord\nActuator\n\n11\n\n543\n\nCONTROL\nCONSIDERATIONS\nThe surface control system actually consists of three cascaded systems\n(figure 12). The measurementof the surface contour in real time is in itself\nan area which will be investigated during the CSEI program, and could not be\nincorporated into the initial\ncontrol system design. Thus, the position of each\ncord junction at the rear truss assembly is an open-loop function of the position of the control piston.\nSince the limited space available on the base\nprevented direct measurementof the cord control pistons, these, too, formed\nopen-loop systems cascaded onto the closed-loop arcsector position controllers.\nTo minimize the effect of the actuator cable and piston spring mass system, the\npiston assembly has been designed to maintain a preload on the actuator control\ncable, arcsector, and gearhead; and the piston assembly has been designed with a\nresonance more than a decade higher than the desired control bandwidth. As\nnoted earlier, corrections are madeto minimize the effects of actuator cable\nstrain on the accuracy of piston positioning.\nAnalysis and simulations have been performed which indicate that the\nposition control system, a prototype of which is presently being fabricated,\nwill meet or exceed all of the system performance.\n\n560\n\nactuator\n\ndynamics\n\nlying in 1st and Brd quadrants\n\nCONCLUDING\n\nREMARKS\n\nThe problem\nof designing\nrobust\ncontrollers\nfor flexible\nspacecraft\nwas\naddressed\nusing\ntwo approaches.\nThe first approach\nconsisted\nof an LQG-type\ncompensator.\nIt was found that this type of compensator\ncan be robustifled\nagainst\nunmodeled\ndynamics\nusing\nthe loop-transfer-recovery-(LTR)\nprocedure.\nThe presence\nof transmission\nzeros can cause performance\ndegradation,\nand should\nbe considered\nin\nthe early\ndesign\nphase while\nselecting\nactuator/sensor\nlocations.\nEffects\nof sensor/\nactuator\nnonlinearities\nwere investigated,\nand expressions\nwere obtained\nfor\nstability\nregions.\nThe second\ndesign\napproach\nconsidered\nutilizes\n"collocated"\ncontrollers,\nwhich\nwere shown to have excellent\nrobustness\nproperties\nin the\npresence\nof not only modeling\nerrors,\nbut also actuator/sensor\nnonlinearities\nand\ndynamics.\nFuture\nefforts\nshould\nattempt\nto obtain\nless conservative\nstability\nregions\nin the presence\nof nonlinearities,\nand to develop\nprocedures\nto robustify\nLQG-type\ncompensators\nsimultaneously\nFor collocated\ncontrollers,\nefforts\nfeedback\ngains,\nas well as stability\n\no\n\nLQG/LTR\n\nis a promising\n\n-Consideration\n(actuator/sensor\no\n\nagainst\nmodeling\nerrors\nand nonlinearities.\nshould\nbe directed\ntowards\nobtaining\noptimal\nresults\nwith higher-order\nactuator\ndynamics.\n\nmethod\n\nmust\nbe given\nlocation)\n\nto\n\ntransmission\n\nCollocated\ncontrollers\noffer highly\nin the presence\nof large
